THE RED PAGE.
THAT is another Australian writer’s farewell
to Australia, for a time at least—since
“London calls.” Louise Mack (Mrs. J.
P. Greed) was born at Hobart in the early
seventies. Her father, the Rev. Hans Mack, was
a native of Jersey, in the Channel Islands; her
mother an Irishwoman. Louise Mack was the
seventh of thirteen children ; and in her early
years the family removed in succession to Clare
(S.A.), Adelaide, Strathalbyn, Morpeth (N.S.W.)
and Windsor ■=—the rule of the Primitive
Methodist ministry limiting residence in one
place to three years. When Miss Mack was
about fourteen, the family settled in Sydney,
where it has since remained.
As a girl Louise Mack spent five years at
• Sydney High School ; and her memories of
school-days are reproduced in “Teens” and
“Girls Together.” Failing to matriculate
at Sydney University, she settled down as
" teacher, and later as writer. Even at school
she essayed literature as an editor of “ The
High School Magazine,” and in recent years her
pen has been very busy. Her published books
are : The World is Round (T. Fisher Unwin,
London, 1896); “Teens” (Angus and Robertson,
Sydney, 1897); “Girls Together” (Angus and
Robertson, Sydney, 1898). A booklet of her
collected verses, “Dreams in Flower,” will be
published by The Bulletin Newspaper Co. next
month. She takes for London publication a
novel, “ In the Coil of Things,” and another not
yet completed ; besides many short stories.
Louise Mack’s verses are perhaps the most dis-
tinguished that have been written by a woman in
Australia, though her manifestation of a true
poetic spirit is occasionally accomplished at
some expense of form. In tone they are usually
sombre; but they are natural lyrics, poignant
with emotion, and permeated by intense love of
Australia—Australian trees and landscapes, Aus-
tralian suns and glooms. Her prose work, though
always excellent, and noted for its sweet lucidity
of style, is not yet so remarkable in its field as her
work in verse.
For the past three years Louise Mack has been
on the staff of this paper, as writer of the
Woman’s Letter in Sydney. The Bulletin is
sincerely sorry to part from a good writer and a
good comrade, and wishes her all possible success
in the field of authorship in London.
BEFORE EXILE.
Here is my last good-bye,
This side the sea.
Good-bye ! good-bye ! good-bye l
Love me, Remember me.
This is my last good-bye,
This side the sea,
I bless, I pledge, I cling,
Love me, Remember me.
This is my last good-bye
To each dear tree,
To every silent plain,
Love me, Remember me.
This is my last good-bye,
This side the sea.
Oh, friends l oh, enemies i
Love me, Remember me.
You will remain, but I
Must cross the sea,
My heart is faint with love,
Oh, Land I Remember me.
You will not, even ask
What claim has she ?
She loved us, she has gone,~
—’Tis all : Remember me.
This 4s what you will say,
My Land across the sea,
She was of us, is gone—
And you ’U Remember me.
Here is my last good-bye,
This side the sea.
Farewell ! and when you can
Love me, Remember me.
LOUISE MACK.
Some Victorian Painters .
Among the men who have succeeded,
Longstaff stands easily shoulders above
the rest. In his landscape sketches,
which have never been seen outside his
studio, he has struck the genuine Australian note
—a note not so jaueb of literal interpretation as of
atmosphere, a suggestion of the strange aloneness
and fatality of the Bush, that essence of romance.
It is pity that Longstaff should have to paint
portraits for a living, and greater pity that he
should leave this country, for he thinks in pictures
as a true artist should, and there is imagination
in his handling.
None of the Victorian painters except Long-
staff paint the bush deep enough in tone. Instead
of transposing the values to accentuate the tone
interest of their pictures they try for a faithful
transcript—sometimes with a synthesis, generally
without.
John Ford Patterson is now with the decadents.
His tenet is that Nature is too horrible, too
“near.” One must go away from her as far as
possible ; and in his latest picture, “ A Bush
bymphony,” he has certainly gone away. It is
like a faint tapestry seen through a film of gauze.
He has tried to do in paint what the French sym-
bolists have done in literature; but has not, T
think, succeeded. The scheme is too high’ in
tone and altogether not convincing enough. But
Mr. Patterson should feel satisfied : he has roused
the ire of every Philistinish person who has seen
his picture; and the Melbourne Gallery paid £2OO
for the joy of possession.
Fox is a clever and versatile painter. I like his
portraits best ; but he has tried to introduce
French effects into an Australian setting. It may
not be unpardonable to paint Australian land-
scape in a French method, but it seems outside
the pale of common reason to look for the atmos-
pheric effects of France in a land altogether alien
From them in tone and color and form.
' Withers lias a charming sense of colour, and
with the years his handling and knowledge of
values has increased, but he has not escaped the
domestic note that characterises rural England
the path leading to the cottage on the top° of the
hill, the village nestling in the comfortable valley
the carter returning through the twilight. Still
his work is always interesting : it never lacks a
personal charm.
Dave Davies (now in England) painted some
° f /-ustralian landscape astonishingly
evcnimf a S d 4 fcru y ; Hls excellent observation of

The reason why Velasquez is such a great
things’- 13 ri ;i at he P ainted only paintable
r!rlv£ li the . sooner the Australian artist com-
prehends the pamtability of his country the better
nTtnre I ? Stead °. f tryin g to look at life and
6 th /su sh eyes ’ he P ersist s usually in a re-
dundancy of the same compositions. If he paints
H® B f\} t 1S f f om , the shore. Ti-tree and cliff to
the right, sea to the left, beach in foreground. He
never thinks of painting it from above the blue
?® a> d .c e P x . n tone, through the twisted branches so
beautiful in line. And the same with his up-
country work.
However, the artist himself is not to blame for
his limited field; for, to make a living, he must
stay m Melbourne. But if he had the chance to
*4 i A-'®, back-country— not to the stations or
the goldfields but to the Bush itself, and see it as
the explorers saw it, how his outlook would
cha,nge . Most of Australia’s artists have been no
further inland than a bush township : they have
never seen the limitless expanse of sun-burnt
p ains, broken only by an outcrop of rocks, vibrat-
ing ni the. silent heat. Neither have they re-
garded (at least, with a seeing eye—which should
mean a doing hand) the hillside of a gully full-
taced to the midday sun, slap up against a blue
,y, y ith lts creeping cloud atop, everything
almost oppressively quiet in the heat. An in-
scrutable landscape which reveals nothing because
it is the oldest in the world, and nothing has been
written upon its face.
And then, plastically, Australia is one of the
most paintable countries under the sun ; its land-
scape is so essentially one of masses, masses that
simplify themselves to a few tones of deep and
harmonious colour. There is no flickering foliage
to tire your brush; no sentimentality to sicken
your soul.
When I think of Australia’s landscape I see no
pictures of early pioneering, no settlements or
mining camps, but a land older than Arabia Felix
or Chaldean Egypt, a land which has faced the
unbroken centuries silent and unknown—a strange
land peopled by the earliest animals and fringed
with strange trees and desolate shores I—a 1 —a country
of no traditions. There is more mystery to me in
the bronze gums palpable in the stillness of the
naked noonday than in all the so-called mystery—
generally a mere vague, undefined suggestiveness
—which permeates the morbidity of Christian art.
It will be the impersonal man, who considers
his subject and his art instead of himself, who will
paint this country rightly. The everyday man
is so parochially human that his heart must
pant after the waterbrooks of the domestic and
the traditional ; and that is why artless, unthink-
ing work always pleases him.
In the future—say in another two hundred
years, when Australia shall be the nation we are
always talking about—Sydney will play Athens
to the Ptolemyan Alexandria of Melbourne.
Then the man who tramps the galleries will see
what is at present unrealised—Australian land-
scape painted by an Australian who loves and
understands it.
Biit I have strayed slightly from a consideration
of Art in Melbourne—which, for the present, I
should say, is, to use that comfortable expression
of the actor— Besting.
v.
LIONEL LINDSAY.
Just Nothing.
f'lnHE man who knows nothing in all its pro-
I fundity is a seer. He scans the howling
-JL void with sleepless eyes and its
blackness full of points of light. He
cannot sleep; he is the night-watchman, the
caretaker of those myriad stars. Should he
maunder or snore at his post the sleeping
world would wake with horrid dreams. to find
the darkness blank. So you wake at sea when
something is wrong and the throbbing engines
ceasB. The light is out; you stumble up on
deck. The sky is blanked with clouds ; the sea—
there is no sea ; only a hungry, growling, lurking
monster there in the dark. The ship—there is
no ship; we ride a reeling nightmare. The men
—there are none ; only demented, accusing voices
calling, wailing out of hell—only there is no hell.
.1 was saying, I think, that but for the man who
knows nothing, chaos were come again. Fancy a
world made up of people who know some-
thing, and others who know everything. Of
course, these two species of creature make up the
majority as it is. . And they sleep—the good God
be praised ! It is a mad-house where narcotics
are your only physic; and the warder wakes, and
walks,' and watches the void. Think if they were
all to wake, with all their dreadful manias fright-
fully alive. The warder would be no
more; mercifully, his end would be swift ii
that leaden hail of demented, fragmental,
shattered, shattering shrapnel of would-be wis-
dom. The denizens of such a' world would
destroy one another, and then we would begin
again, happily with nothing. Perhaps we shall.
I think,—indeed, I am sure,—that it is best to
be boru blind. Such a one knows nothing truly ;
—he is your only seer. The profundities are all
his; he cannot even argue with you that black is
white or is n’t, or ought to be. Deep into the
nothingness beyond all black and white;—past
the absence and the presence of all colour and
light; beyond the great negation, through the
everlasting question, into the Never Never his
sight pierces, the while he sits with veiled, lustre-
less orbs—globes of deified clay,—how use-
less! No—how much more useful than yours
or mine ! for they are shutters that close out
the false and the foolish and the filthy
and the thousand .natural abominations that your
eyes and mine let in upon our souls till we fall,
and wallow where we fall. Were I possessed of the
sinister design upon suffering humanity (after
the manner of W. Besant, Knight Bachelor) to
project an academy for the encouragement of
authorship, [ should teach the students nothing,
deliberately and carefully, if not prayerfully;
and, though I say it, 1 should cfo well. All
other systems must fail. The man who knows
something ceases to exist. He might as well be a
Government expert or a newspaper man for all
the use he can be to his fellows, or any other
fellows.
To descend to examples—a dangerous practice—
Milton, before he became blind, was nearly
becoming a pestilent fellow, with his absurd
erudition. Fortunately his sight went, and
he saw. Shakespeare and Carlyle knew
nothing, and so did good work. Buskin’s fatal
failing was s*me knowledge. Dickens, poor man,
went about and learned things, and failed.
Thackeray and Oscar Wilde and some others —I
think I might include Victor Daley—have guile-
less wit’s diviner way, and stand under the stars
absorbing and diffusing light. You would ask,
“What of Darwin and Huxley and Bacon?”
The making of three clever men only partly
spoiled by their learning. Gutta serena might
have made any one of these a seer; a trifling
accident, say with gunpowder, would have done
it. Possibly you wouldn’t ask. Perhaps you
know—most men know something, it is the primal
curse, since Adam ate the fruit —the proverb is
something musty.
An expert would attempt to explain what it is
to know nothing. Not so here, for, though not a
seer, Ido not know. If you seek more, be an
infant lying naked and alone under the great
black vault of space, and if you are a very, very
simple sort of infant you may inherit the universe.
V.
THE OWL.
“ Scribe ” talks sense—
Some weeks a go, in these columns, I urged Australian
pressmen at large to be on the lookout with regard to
their accommodation in the National Parliament, which
is not in any way the appurtenance of Melbourne. There
is nothing tangible proposed, as I write. The first ques-
tion to consider is whether a Central Press Association
can do the reporting for Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide,
Hobart, and Perth. No. Impossible. Such a thing may
grow into being, but could not be fixed up now in time.
Well, then, I say the following papers must have equal
facilities: Age, Argus, Sydney Herald and Telegraph.
Brisbane Courier and Telegraph, Adelaide Register and
Advertiser, Hobart Mercury, Perth Herald. Here is
your front line. There are about as many for the
second line. Of course, if there are more front seats, the
best of them may get there. The Gallery Committee
should be composed of one man each from Melbourne,
Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Hobart, and Perth ; and we
might allow the Melb. man as chairman with a casting
vote, in addition to his regular vote. I assume the National
Parliament will meet in the Vic. Parliament House, because
Barton is understood to wish it, and Peacock says he will
be guided by Barton. However, these remarks, with a
little modification, will serve for the Exhibition. At Par-
liament House the Leg. Assembly Press accommodation
is a notorious scandal, even for Melb. requirements. One
solution that occurs to me while writing (I have not met
with it anywhere) is that the National House of Repre-
sentatives should meet in the Vic. Leg. Council Chamber,
and the Senate in the Assembly Chamber. As you
are aware, the Press could he capitally ranged along
the galleries of the Leg. Council, with ample room
for all. Even in the Exhibition they could never
make such a handy Press accommodation. The diffi-
culty is that the Legislative Council Chamber could
hardly be adapted to hold all the members with comfort.
Reverting, then, to the Assembly Chamber. It must be
gutted, if adopted. The Press must have, both the Gal-
leries of the Speaker, which are on the floor of the House.
With seats graded Up in tiers, they would serve all the
legitimate Press. The Members’Rooms behind must be
turned into Press Rooms. The present Reporters’ Gal-
lery could be made the Second Public Gallery, while the
present Public Gallery is. made the Speaker’s Gallery.
Let me urge Newspaper Proprietors all-over the Con-
tinent to look to their interests in this regard They
should all have agents on the spot at once. Co-operation
will suitably restrain Melbourne influence.
Hear,! to this. No Melbourne supremacy is
wanted in an Australian press gallery. The press
of every State should have an equal chance to
watch and report fot its State. And any enter-
prising country paper, or the warrantable repre-
sentative of a country-paper combination, should
also be given good room. You can’t have too
many working pens. But the State press galleries
are too often crowded by loafers and metropolitan
stragglers favoured by officials.
“F.C.S.”:
There still stands at Coonunbene, N.S.W., the old
house, low and squat, with its plastered and uneven
walls, where Henry Kendall, 32 years ago, found repose
during his wanderings amid coastal woods and ranges.
At its rear, to westward, are the dim gorges and hills
wrapped (when I saw them in March) in rain-charged
mist. In front and to the right are the waters of an arm
of Broken Bo ; y, beside which Kendall dreamed and wove
verse. A pioneer of the district sits gout-stricken and
grey in the shadow of the house that sheltered Kendall.
He tells how the singer came from somewhere n the
northern rivers and arrived at Coorumbene (four syllables
—not “ Cooranbean,” as generally written) tired and
almostspent. He came in Robinsoii-Grusoe garb to help the
children in their wood-carrying tasks, and at times brought
the mails from Gosford, Kincumber and Wamberal. In the.
glens, gorges, and dark, unfooted dells ho spent whole days.
The results of these lonely contemplations were “ Narara
Creek,” “ Mooni,” “ Coorumbene,” “ Bell Birds,” and the
prize-poem for the opening of the Sydney International
Exhibition. For the last, Kendall felt no inspiration ;
but—allowing himself to be persuaded—competed, and
carried off the prize. At the feet of the dark hills
Narara creek winds past Coorumbene, and joins the sea
half-a-mile further down. It is silent and but little
traversed, this Narara creek of Kendall’s. In the hills a
little beyond it are the “ clinging mosses,” the “ leaves
and shining woodland waterheads,” the “channels of
coolness, and echoes that call.” There has been hardly
any change in Coorumbene during the years since Ken-
dall kept tryst with Nature amid its solitudes.
“Ignotus ” brings a problem for mathematical
readers—
In Laing’s “ Human Origins," p. 146,1 find the follow-
ing re the Great Pyramid: Ist. The triangular area of
each of the four sloping sides equals the square of the
vertical height. 2nd. The united length of the four sides
of the square base bears to the vertical height the same
proportion as that of the circumference of a circle to its
radius. Can the above statements both be true ?
AUSTRALIAN TOWNS.
Combo, king of Carrawinjie,
Puffed with pride' and ’possum, sat
On his hams and stroked his binjy,
Eyed the Jaelceroo, and spat :
“ Yoivi, boss , me king dis nation,
You bin jump it Combo's claim ;
Squatter make ’implenty station —
Blackfulla bin gib it name.
Cunnamulla, Ulladulla,
Tarnagulla, Burr era long,
Murrumbu rrah, Millamurra,
Muttaburra, Mittagong.
“ Imba, king o’ Burrundulla,
That un brother b’long a me,
Cobaun Brince o’ Wales tha’ fulla —
Me ’n him Royal Family.
Plenty places we bin christen —
Hundreds, t’ousands, near a score ;
Gib it match, ole man, an’ listen,
Combo yabber plenty more :
Currawilla, Muckadilla,
Boggabilla, Boggabri,
Coolangatta, Wangaratta,
Parramatta, Mungundi
“Unsophisticated niggah,”
Lisped the Johnny, “ I admiah
Your obese and kinglike figgah,
But your Majesty’s a liah.
And your beastly appellations
G-wate on educated eahs;

Wyalong, Way, Bigga, Price,
TantanOola, Walmer, Coolah,
Purga, Taree, Paradise.

Sweetah music nevah set!
Wule Bwitannia ! Bwitons nevah ! ”

Bards of the Backbloeke
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Melb. has no climate. Only samples.”
—Yank.
In N.S.W. there are 64 completed bores
yielding artesian water, and 94 privately-owned.
The former, it is estimated, yield 31,762,415 gals,
water daily; the latter 45,499,900 gals. The
hottest water thrown up in N.S.W. is from the
Goondabline private bore (148 deg. F.), 2800 ft.
deep : but the hottest in Australia is from the
Dag worth bore, near Winton (Q.) —196 deg. F.
This bore is 3335 ft. deep.
Writes “ Deeby ” :
How this country does bleed in the interests of private
individuals and incompetent or crooked departmental
officials ! For instance, a Govt, experimental farm, in-
cluding a tank, engine, pump, &c., was leased some years
ago at a merely nominal rental for a term of five years.
During that period a mining town sprang up not far off,
which had to depend almost wholly on this Govt, tank
for its water supply, thereby, of course, enriching the
lessee of the Govt. farm. Meanwhile, a petition was pre-
sented to the department on behalf of the mining popu-
" lation of this town, asking the Govt, for a primitive water
supply nearer the town. The petition was refused on the
ground that there was already a Govt, tank in the locality.
This practically created a monopoly for the lessee of the
Govt. farm. Yet on the expiration of the lease this same
lessee was granted a further lease of five years at a yearly
rental of less than £5, and without calling for tenders, in
spite of the fact that it was well-known locally there
would have been keen competition. The matter was
brought before the Department of Water Conservation,
which replied that it was too late to take any steps in
the matter, they having relied on their official’s report.
Yet this official is still in the department. Meanwhile,
the .lessee of the Govt, farm is piling up wealth (by selling
water alone) at the rate of over £2O weekly. There are
other matters in connection with this affair involving the
competence or honesty of local officials. Our “ bleed-
ing ” country !
Cincinnatus.
[The writer of this, while loathing the Boer war and in-
expressibly ashamed at Australia having taken a hand in
choking a brave little nation beset by fearful odds, is
inclined to believe that some good may ultimately come
out of the Australian-contingent business, which has
taught Australians that they are quite as good in a fight
as John Bull or any other man. Also, this scribe is Quite
aware that exception may be taken (on historical grounds
to his rhyme by literalists.]
There was once an ancient Roman, Cincinnatus
was his name,
He worked on his selection and he didn’t crave
for fame;
His wool was long, his whiskers waved like
banners in the breeze,
And he wore a pair of moleys tied with bowyangs
round the knees.
But if he wasn’t pretty, he was willing, tough and
game,
And so, when Rome got cornered and her fighting
crack went lame,
The people raised a rumpus and to save a further
fuss
They sent for Cincinnatus —same as they did for
MS.
The rank-and-file would snigger,
And the officers would*'scoff ;
It took the consul all his time
To stop them “ chucking-off ” ;
And the army paid its premiums,
For it knew its days were o’er,
When a backblock chump like Cincy
Had to lead them into war.
But they didn’t scoff next morning when he
marched them off at dawn—
They hadn’t time to do the Block or tell their
clubs they’d gone ;
Their plumes grew wet and ragged ; their mous-
tachioes lacked wax,
For he worked those tonev soldiers till they tum-
bled in their tracks.
If potatoes wanted peeling, and the cooks had
graft to do,
When no one else was handy, he would tell a
colonel to;
So when the foemen hove in sight, those toney
soldiers swore
Such ignorance of tactics never had been seen
before. ,
They said there was no hope for it,
Their time had come to die ;
But Cincy calmly corpsed the foe
And winked the other eye.
For he’d recognised the principle,
In drawing up his plan,
That the very best of tactics
Is to kill the other man.
The world grows old and wiser, but the world goes
still to war,
And it barracks for its tailor-dummies as it did of
yore ;
It cheers its Johnnie warriors, and it piles it on
them strong,
And it can’t restrain its roars when Cincinnatus
comes along.
It scoffs when first it sees him, just the way they
did in Rome,
It says his drill is awful, and he’d better stay at
home;
And leave the war entirely to the dudes who know
the game—
But, in the end, they find that Cincy gets there
all the same.
The rank and file may snigger,
And the officers may rail,
But they find that Cincinnatus
Takes the biscuit where they fail.
For he’s recognised the principle,
That’s held since war began,
That the very best of tactics
Is to best the other man.
V.
DUDLEY W.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
IVestralia and JHaoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
■6l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition oflhi. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —l \d. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition o/The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with portage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.

SATURDAY, APRIL 20, 1901.
The Began Scandal and Party Politics.
The appointment of J. L. Fegan, an ex-
Minister in the Lyne Cabinet of N. S.
Wales, to a lucrative permanent place under
the Commonwealth, is scandalous enough
when considered as an isolated incident.
Naturally, George Reid, whose own political
overthrow was not long ago caused by just
such another incident—when he wished to ap-
ply public funds to reward his supporter, J. C.
Neild —has expressed undisguised horror at
the intrusion of “ jobbery " into Common-
- wealth politics. The spectacle of Satan
reproving Sin is almost too familiar for
comment. But honest men of all
shades of opinion may well unite
to denounce the Fegan appointment;
and the supporters of the Barton
Cabinet should press for its cancellation.
The Bulletin attaches no weight to the
argument that Fegan is disqualified because
he fought against Federation. That strife is
dead and buried. Opposition then is quite
consistent with loyal citizenship now. And
the Commonwealth is not generous, it is
merely just, when it dates A.R.C.—from the
Day when the Republic was Founded—and
admits on equal terms after the Fact both the
opponents and the supporters of the Dream.
The Constitution Act is also an Act of In-
demnity for all such offenders.
• * * *
Nor would The Bulletin contend that
the Under-Secretaryship for Internal Affairs
must necessarily be given to an official who
has served the Government of a State. It
is indeed a rule of healthy and efficient
public administration that the promotion of
public servants should be frequent and
regular, and should invariably follow desert;
so that the man in the lowest rank may be
sure that assiduous ability will gradually
raise him to the highest rank. Nothing is
so disheartening to the individual, nothing
so demoralising to the service, as the belief
that the most lucrative posts are reserved
or I . n ® uenfcial outsiders, and that no talent,
no industry, will avail against the wire-
pulhng of a jobbing politician’s nominee.
When a zealous servant sees that pro-
motion goes, not by merit, but by favour ;
when a class of servants realises that the
public service is a stagnant pool where
ambition has no spur, and effort no reward ;
is it any wonder that zeal decays, that
effort relaxes, and that the public suffers
from a spiritless and unintelligent adminis-
tration of public affairs ? The sloth of “ the
Government stroke ” is proverbial ; —and
what is responsible for “ the Government
stroke if not the corruption and inefficiency
of the elected heads of Government ? Yet
Ihk Bulletin sees clearly that, in filling
important public offices, seniority of service
is less important than capacity to fill the
ti C - e ' ■ §°°d of the community overrides
all individual pretensions. Petrifaction in
the public service for a quarter-century
is no qualification for a post where
brains and energy are indispensable. In
filling a new and important office, The
Bulletin would unhesitatingly, if it were
necessary, appoint a man from beyond
the ranks of public servants. But it would
first ransack the existing public service to
find its man, in order to retain the vital, the
indispensable impetus given to the public
service by the legitimate Hope of Pi •omo-
tion.
\** * *
But what are the facts of the Fegan
case ? Here is a low-class politician who
has succeeded in reaching Ministerial office
by dint of persistency and cunning. A
treacherous partisan, familiar with the back-
stairs of intrigue, he made himself useful to
ex-Premier Lyne ; and he is rewarded for
his utility. Not, perhaps, more illiterate or
less gifted than his patron, his apparent
recommendation is that he seems too
servile to be ungrateful, and too well-
trained to be officially over-scrupulous.
Minister Lyne has naturally chosen an
Under-Secretary to suit his political self :
like master, like man. “Job!” the ap-
pointment shrieks; and “Job!” and “Job!”
again. And tlm Public Service may go to the
hell which back-stairs politics has constructed
for it.
* * » *
Here we come to the worst significance of
the Fegan scandal : it is crucial. The first
Commonwealth Government stands at the
parting of the ways : it can take the New
Road of- Honest Administration, or it
can take the Old Road of Vicious Mal-
administration traversed by so many
State Governments. The Fegan appoint-
ment made by Minister Lyne, with
which Prime Minister Barton “sees no
reason to interfere,” is a finger-post to show
that some members of the first Common-
wealth Ministry are willing to travel the old
road to administrative perdition. On a reason-
able calculation, there may be a couple of
thousand officials in the existing State ser-
vices who can fill the post in question as
efficiently as Premier Lyne’s nominee.
There are probably one thousand officials with
better brains and better training who can fill
it much more efficiently. And these officials
are free from the taint of backstairs intrigue
which scents the Fegan appointment. The
place is eminently one to be filled from the
ranks of present Civil servants ; since experi-
ence of administration counts highly among
the qualifications of an Under-Secretary. For
this place, then, and for all similar places,
let Civil servants be invited to apply, and
let the applications be considered by the
whole Cabinet, or by an independent Board
nominated by the Cabinet. Let the Com-
monwealth Government at the very outset
establish the administrative principle of the
Career Open to Talents, the office open
to Experience and Capacity ; and destroy
the practice of reserving the Best Billets as
a reward for Personal Services to Politicians.
* * * *
Prime Minister Barton is reported to
have said that Minister Lyne’s appointment
was not considered by the Cabinet; and
The Bulletin is glad to hear it. From
Minister Lyne this paper expected nothing
less ; from Prime Minister Barton it ex-
pected little more; but from the Barton
Cabinet it expected a great deal more.
Minister Lyne— who is a Minister by the
skin of his teeth because he had influence
enough to prevent the metropolitan Free-
trade organisation from earnestly supporting
his opponent in the Hume electorate ; and
because he had influence enough to prevent
(in a thoroughly characteristic manner) the
most active metropolitan Freetrade news-
paper from vigorously opposing himself—
evidently considers that the Commonwealth
Parliament is an arena for the same tactics
which he has successfully employed in
N.S.VV. State politics. The Bulletin
guesses that Premier Lyne will find himself
mistaken. It is Australia that is talking
now ; and The Bulletin is proud to believe
that there are democratic representatives
assembling from East and West and
Centre who will not tolerate a system of job-
government even for the pleasure of sup-
porting such a steadfast Protectionist as
Minister Lyne. And it is because The
Bulletin desires to see a Protectionist Ad-
ministration continue in office that it asks
Ministers Kingston and Turner and Drake
to do their best to keep their wobbling col-
leagues straight on the line of Honest
Government. Protection is an essential
factor in Australian democratic policy ; but it
is not the only factor ; and the espousal of a
Protectionist policy b,y any Minister is no
excuse for introducing a system of rotten
administration. The Barton Cabinet wil 1
have to face a vigorous Tory Opposition ;
and its policy must be sound at all points.
There are men coming from Queensland,
from Westralia, and from South Australia,
who are not likely to sanction jobbery merely
because a Protectionist Minister is respon-
sible for the job. And The Bulletin
fancies that the first Commonwealth
Parliament, with the romance of the
New Birth still ringing in its ears,
and with the eyes of all-Australia fixed
upon its deeds, will exhibit a quality of con-
scientiousness which will surprise the veteran
political tricksters of the provincial school.
• * * *
The Bulletin advises the Barton Cabinet
to meet Parliament with clean hands. Let
it cancel the Fegan appointment before its
supporters officially complain. Mr. Fegan
offers to retire : let him retire. If Minister
Lyne grumbles, let him grumble. Minister
Lynk is an unhappy accident in a democratic
Cabinet ; and if he insists on perfuming the
Cabinet with his peculiar political aroma,
it were better to be rid of both him and his
aroma. Make it understood that Protection
is synonymous with Clean Politics, and Aus-
tralia will gladly accept Protection. When
there are Federal billets to fill, give them to
Proved Desert, and not to Personal Friend-
ship. If the Barton Cabinet renders, it
necessary for Protectionists to support the
Barton Cabinet at the cost of general demo-
cratic principles, then the Barton Cabinet is
riding for a fall.
An Absurd Lunacy Law.
A short time ago an insane woman threw he'rself
from the cliffs at Beaumaris, near Melbourne, and
by her death added another to the long list of
murders practically committed by the absurd and
antiquated Lunacy Act of Victoria. The usual
nine days’ wonder is eventuating ; the Chief Sec-
retary will answer various irrelevant questions,
and then the matter will be comfortably shelved
until another such occurrence serves to re-open
the solemn farce at the instance of the news-
papers. There is an indistinct idea that Victoria
has “an awful lot of lunatics,” and that they cost
a good deal to support, but the public has no con-
ception of the real state of affairs. From the
political standpoint, inquiry means trouble.
Lunatics have no votes, and people without votes
are politically mere lumber. The “member” is
not in the business “ for his health.”
* * * *
Thirteen years ago was passed a Lunacy Act
providing for the up-keep of certain public Hos-
pitals for the Insane ; the establishment of receiv-
ing-houses for the observation of doubtful cases ;
and the licensing of outside establishments
under certain conditions of supervision by the
Departmental medical officers. Official visi-
tors were appointed, arrangements were made
for legal inquiry into all deaths among the
insane, and the medical service was re-modelled.
Which all sounds very proper and complete, if
read over quickly. But viewed from the practi-
cal standpoint that lunatic-asylums are princi-
pally intended for the cure or relief of the insane
on scientific lines, the Act has turned out to be
about the most defective ever framed in .a lawyer-
ridden State. The expensive asylums are built on
the “ barrack ” system—which is hopelessly out of
date ; and no provision has been made for increase
in the numbers of inmates. There is not at present
m Victoria a single hospital for the insane which
at all approaches present-day standards, whether
from the point of view of the alienist or
of the sanitarian. The great bulk of patients
are herded together irrespective of their par-
ticular form of insanity, so long as it
is not actually violent, and in the dormitories
the sights and sounds of a single night
are amply sufficient to upset all hope of recovery
in patients who might otherwise improve. Every
modern alienist of repute has strongly condemned
the heterogeneous barrack system as being a
powerful preventive of the cure of the insane.
Vet people in Victoria wonder at the small propor-
tion of recoveries !
* * * *
As to the receiving-houses provided for by the
Act, there are apparently none. In a large com-
munity the receiving-house is an absolute neces-
sity for the interim-observation of doubtful cases
to be subsequently either discharged or trans-
ferred to an asylum for treatment. But, at pre
sent_ these' cases are committed to gaol “for
medical observation,” a conviction thus being
necessarily recorded against the unfortunate
patient.
* * * *
The clause relating to the establishment of out-
side asylums is, perhaps, the most serious blot on
the Act. In a vague sort of way it states that,
while regular private asylums are illegal, yet
philanthropic institutions of a sort may be estab-
lished under the supervision of the departmental
officers. But, owing to the opposition of the
Lunacy Department—an opposition which de-
pends largely on the scandalous undermanning of
the medical service—no such establishments have
been instituted. And, since private asylums
are an absolute necessity as long as the public
institutions remain in their present awful condi-
tion, the law is evaded in various ways, all more or
less winked-at by the authorities and aided by the
outside medical profession. It was from one of
these illegal “ homes ” that the unfortunate Beau-
maris victim went to her death.
* * * *
All the supervision these places get comes from
the visiting private doctors, and, as the institu-
tions are, for obvious reasons, run on the cheapest
practical lines, the attendants are often not above
resorting to the straight-waistcoat, at a pinch.
In fact, mechanical restraint is absolutely neces-
sary under these conditions, and “accidents”
arising therefrom are easily got over by a. friendly
medico. The writer does not wish to cast any re-
flection upon such “Homes” or their attendants.
He recognises that private-asylums are at present
an urgent necessity, that departmental influence
is used to prevent their legal establishment, and
that, in order to keep them going, it may be
necessary to hoodwink the Registrar, in order to
prevent unpleasant inquiries. Correct diagnosis
is exceedingly difficult with lunatics, and deaths
often arise from what appear to be quite inade-
quate causes, but the necessity for such action is a
shaipeful one in a civilised community.
* * * *
The Official-Visitor scheme is another ludi-
crous failure. In an undermanned service
there is much that has to be neglected, and
accordingly, from the Superintendent downwards
n \T UIn officials are banded together against
the O. V., who takes his position seriously. The
Ghakles Reade type of lynx-eyed person before
whom every attendant trembles and blurts out
awful disclosures is as extinct as the Alfred
nm-'! T7 v . ariet y patient. The only useful
Urncial V lsitor would be a Government Inspector,
chosen for special qualifications, and empowered
to turn out an asylum from top to bottom, if
necessary. The present style of respectable old
gentleman, who looks with great gravity at what-
ever is shown him, and draws his screw with
touching regularity, may lie ornamental, but he is
certainly not useful; while esprit-de-corps will
always block an earnest reformer.
* * * *
The inquest system and the post-mortem exam-
inations by independent medical men work as
well a,s can be expected, but the annual reports
show far top large a proportion 0f “casualties.”
these casualties (under which head suicide is
usually included) are in great part preventible,
ana are directly due to the undermanning of the
lunacy service; and, with a system under which
an inexperienced attendant is often placed in sole
charge of an absolutely unmanageable number of
patients, such accidents must continue to occur.
* * *
. rnggardly method of administration, in
tact, the Victorian Lunacy Service is rendered
a laughing-stock among alienists. The proportion
ot recoveries is absurdly low, and, of deaths,
criminally high. In a service where, above all
others, special knowledge and a special faculty of
observation are required in the medical officers,
the rates of pay are so low as to debar the best
men from applying. Also, although magnificent
opportunities for scientific research are available,
not only is no advantage taken of them, but any
attempt at the introduction of new treatment or
at original research by medical officers is actively
discouraged. The object of the department seems
to be the encouragement of routine and the
suppression of individuality. The fact is not
recognised that the skilled alienist has evolved
from the old “ mad-doctor, ” and represents a
capable of enormous potentialities for good.
Ihe lunacy vote, ’ represented by the warders, is
the sole subject of political interest, and the whole
administration is subordinated to it; so that, in a
department where rigid discipline is an absolute
essential, a superintendent is required to write
acres of reports before he can secure the transfer-
ence of a disobedient attendant. Asylum-doctors
are often laughed at, but it is remarkable that
there are so many good men among them consider-
ing how poorly-paid and thankless is the work.
* • * *
Everywhere except in Victoria the scientific
treatment of lunatics is now regarded as an impor-
tant department of Government administration.
And goo 1 example is not lacking close at hand ;
the results of tl-e remodelling of the N.S.W.
lunacy-service have conclusively shown that
with modern methods lunacy is essentially a cur-
able disease. Until Victoria comes into line
with the newest alienistic ideas, she must remain
in this respect a standing reproach to her in-
capable Legislature.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted, a Titles Bill.
In England, about two centuries ago, whenever a
politician became too aggressively democratic, the
Royal custom was to confer upon him a post or a
pension, and out of sheer gratitude tin?'recipient
invariably and forthwith became an ardent adorer
of the Throne and its occupant. Wherefore the
Commons passed a Place Bill, which enacted that
when any member accepted an office of profit
under the Crown he, ipso facto, vacated his seat
and his election became void, but he was eligible
for re-election. If his constituents wanted to be
represented by a Royal pensioner they could re-
elect him, but they generally didn’t, and therefore
the system fell into disuse, for a mere man who
was no longer a member of the “ Faithful
Commons ” was apt to find that his post
or pension was suddenly discontinued. The
Royal offer lost its financial charm when accept-
ance meant loss of a seat and of the post as well.
Now, after the labor of those two centuries, there
comes what looks very much like a revival of the
ancient expedient. The “ honors ”in that carpet-
bag on the Ophir. will be awarded where they will
do most good. Not necessarily where they will
do most good to Australia, but most certainly
where, in the view of the donor, they will be most
useful in advancing Imperial interests. Whether
they will equally or at all advance Australia’s
interests is a point on which Royalty need not con-
cern itself. But Australia must consider that
point; and, the danger being obvious, the remedy,
hallowed by British precedent, should be applied.
As England, under Anne and the Georges, found
that a Place Bill was necessary to prevent the
Commons from being diverted from their duty to
the people, so the Federal Parliament will find a
Titles Bill essential to the interests of Australia.
Every member who accepts any title ought to be
comi elled to forfeit his seat and go before his con-
stituents to see what they think of the business.
If they approve, so much the better for the knight;
if they don’t, so much the better for Australia.
The mere motion for leave to bring in such a
Bill will make every member think hard
to ascertain whether he wants to be an
Imperialist, or just an Australian and a democrat
at that. Any M. lI.R. who wants to show that he
doesn’t hanker after a handle to his name, but
does want to prove what a whole-souled democrat
he is, can earn the desired reputation in one act
by linking his name to such a measure. And if it
is passed, knighthoods will be very cheap in
Australia. They are fairly cheap now, but with
a Titles Act in operation they couldn’t be given
away without a cash bonus. Now, ye Labor
members of the Commonwealth Parliament!

A Plea for the Oppressed.
“James Scott” writes to The Bulletin from
Westralia :
In the interests of a race, powerless to assert their
rights, or even make their wrongs and sorrows known,
will you kindly let me place before your readers a few
facts, culled from evidence given at a recent trial, which
may be accepted as typical of the lot of the toad-native
in this State of Westralia.
A few “indentured” natives ran away from a station
were pursued, run down, and brought back a long dis-
tance. To travel “runaways,” a chain-collar is placed
around the neck, and through each a leading-chain is
passed, one end fastened to the trooper’s saddle, securing
the string of poor nude creatures. Thus equipped, the
horse gives the pace, and measures out human endurance
to the captives, who trot to the music of the stock-whip 1
At night, unit is joined to unit, and limb to limb, and in
this cramped-up position tire forlorn captives must pass
the night!--those inhuman precautions being deemed
necessary to prevent escape.
The “runaways” reached the Station under a swelter-
ing sun, and were denied either food or water while
awaiting their doom. Indue course the flagellators ap-
peared, armed with knotted ropes—the veritable “ knout ”
of W.A.


I will not dwell upon the operation further than observe
that male and female, youth and age, were alike served.
Those whom Death spared were turned adrift, barely able
to move away, their furrowed backs a mass of clotted
blood—unpitied and uncared for ; the bodies of the others
were placed in a heap to a-wait the solemn mockery of an
inquest.
The jury found that a little severity had been em-
ployed, but then the awful crime! Mind you, the
“niggers” left the flocks intact to their lusty owners,
did not even wait for “wages,” but quietly “ cleared ” to
their haunts and free roaming life. As, however, sheep
mean money, and “ niggers ” can be had for nought —dis-
carding antiquated, ethical notions, and viewing the
matter from a more modern monetary standpoint, the
Coroner’s Court held that the law had been simply
vindicated, the Dr. and Cr. accounts nicely squared in
W.A. fashion, and “ reported ” accordingly.
Somehow, “ murder will out” even in this State where
the thickly-set and sparsely-tenanted bush, aided by a
loose administration of justice, affords exceptional facili-
ties for the commission as well as the concealment of
crime. In this instance, rumour got abroad that dark
deeds had been done, with the result that a personal
friend moved in the matter.
A second inquest was held, not in the interests of the
“ System,” but of the public, and under its eye. The
putrescent bodies of the murdered natives dragged out of
a hole, into which they had been thrown like dead dogs,
appealed to their Maker more eloquently than words
could express ! Affrighted humanity shuddered while
the grand old flag gazed on the spectacle and wept!
Item : The jury in the final act found a verdict of wilful
murder, and His’ Honor, in passing sentence, stigmatised
the crime as one of the most unprovoked, cowardly, and
brutal ever brought before him—a reproof the author and
buttress of the “ System ” so encumbered with honors
may surely well take to heart.
One word and I am done. Under cloak of “Native
Contracts ” or “ Indentures,” for which, however,
“ Assign ” is the more correct term, offences of the
blackest type can be perpetrated upon the impotent and
unresisting children of misfortune, yet hid from public
view, neither to be revealed nor avenged until the
Great Day of Account.— James Scott. Lauderdale, \Ves-
tralia, March, 1901.

Justice (?) at Goulburn, U.S.W.
On March 28, at Goulburn, N.S.W., before the
Police Magistrate, James M'Cormack was charged
with fraudulently appropriating £lO belonging to
Percival R. Johnson, teller of the B. N.S.W.,
Goulburn. Johnson swore that M'Cormack
cashed a cheque for £25 17s. 6d., and that he in-
advertently gave M'Cormaok two tens and fifteen
one-pound notes, instead of five one-pound notes.
As corroboration, he produced the cheque en-
dorsed on the back as having been paid
by two tens, fifteen ones, and the balance
in coin; and swore that the endorsement
was made at the time. M'Cormack swore that
he was paid the correct amount. It was oath
against oath, with no corroboration on either
side; the prosecutor was a bank-clerk, the de-
fendant an ordinary person whose avocation does
not appear except in so far as it is stated he sold
wool for his cheque ; and the P.M. said, accord-
ing to Goulburn Post, March 30—
He could not dismiss the case without branding Mr.
Johnson as a deliberate scoundrel.
(What has that got to do with the matter if
McCormack was not clearly proved guilty ?)
He believed .there was corroboration of Mr. Johnson’s
evidence
(Where was it? —the bank-slip written by Mr.
Johnson himself was no corroboration ; and there
was nothing else.)
—and the defendant’s manner when giving evidence
was not that of an innocent man.
(If the police magistrate, whoever he is, had seen
as many innocent men with guilty manners, and
guilty men with innocent manners, as The
Bulletin has seen —then he would know that only
Omniscience can decide guilt or innocence from
manner ; and he might learn to do his obvious
duty of deciding a case on the evidence in the case.)
He would impose a fine, because Mr. Johnson would be
recouped his loss.
(In The Bulletin’s opinion, Mr. Johnson or any
other bank-teller, or any man in the community,
is not entitled to complain about losses due to his
own mistakes.)
Defendant was fined £2O, with 4s. lOd. court costs, and
21s. professional costs, to be paid within a month ; in
default three months imprisonment with hard labor.
The Bulletin says this is a case for the Attorney-
General’s inquiry. It is also a case for appeal, and
for public subscription if M'Cormack is unable to
bear the cost of appealing. M‘Cormack is asked
to appeal at once, if he has not already done so ;
and if the facts are as reported he can count on
The Bulletin as a subscriber.

Death Under Chloroform.
•'"Pdtyaka” on a subject of eternal interest alike
to doctors and laymen :
The writer is with “ Medi-Cuss ” (6/4/’O3) in his advocacy
of nitrous oxide (laughing-gas) for those minor operations
which yield the greater part of the chloroform death-roll.
For operations requiring little time in the doing, it is al-
most ideal, the quickness in getting the patient under
(50 secs, average) the adequate available period after
withdrawal (about 30 secs.) and the almost absolute
safety, all being in its favour, while the portability of the
cylinders put out of court the old arguments against it on
the score of difficulty of carriage. Recent experiments
with a mixture of oxygen and nitrous oxide have yielded
very satisfactory results in lengthening the period of
available anaesthesia, and as far back as 1877 Pban,
the French surgeon, amputated a leg under its
effects. The cumbrous apparatus then required
has since been superseded, and Hillischer of Vienna
has given the mixture in 2900 cases, comprising persons
ranging from to 82 years of age, and suffering from all
manner of organic diseases, without a single death.
The available anaesthesia after withdrawal here averaged
70sec., asagainst 30 with nitrous oxide alone; but by
continuous inhalation the ansesthesia can be kept up for
several minutes, and probably for longer periods.
Claude Bernard, the French physiologist, kept a dog
under its effects for 72 hours, using 2500 litres of the mix-
ture, without any bad results. As the writer has pre-
viously pointed out in The Bulletin, the best of the pre-
sent anaesthetic methods for long operations is. found in
nitrous oxide, followed by ether. The rapidity with
which the patient “goes off” under the gas, and the
facility with which the ether effect can be grafted on by
means of a proper Clover’s combined inhaler, completely
demolish the arguments of those chloroform faddists who
allege that their dangerous fetish “ does away with the
waste of time so frequently found in the
administration of ether.” Such an allegation is,
in writer’s opinion, a confession of incompetency
to properly administer ether—but this is by the way. The
small quantity of gas required renders the process suit-
able even for emergency operations, while the danger to
life is less than that of ether by itself, which, again, is a
mere fraction of that of chloroform.
It is only from public opinion reacting upon medical
education that the suppression of the chloroform fiend
can come about. There are cases where chloroform is
preferable and necessary—in using the actual cautery
about the mouth, for instance, the inflammable nature
of ether-vapour here rendering it dangerous in cases
where venous turgescence would have bad effects, or in
patients suffering from chronic bronchitis or advanced
consumption ; but it is utterly unfit for comparison with
ether as a general anaesthetic in the hands of an unskilled
administrator. The average young medico only learns to
give an anaesthetic after he is launched as a duly
qualified practitioner, and the. lack of proper teaching
in this respect is a serious blot on the curriouli of
most medical schools. The young practitioner looks upon
“giving chlo.” as the simplest and easiest of ways to
earn a guinea. He gives “ chlo.” because he has no notion
of any other anaesthetic, because the grooviness of hospital
methods has made him look upon ether as a sort of
second-rate affair, and because the action of nitrous oxide
is utterly unknown to him beyond a vague notion that it
is not quite professional, donteberknow, because—aw—
dentists use it. Not one in a score of medicos has ever
read a text-book on anaesthetics, and the average admin-
istrator works by certain rule-of-thumb methods, which
he would scorn to use for any other professional proce-
dure. The “ whiff of chlo.” is easy to give, and it is the
very “ whiff ” which sends up the death statistics, by
depressing the heart action while leaving the sensory
centres intact, so that a patient dies of heart failure
produced by actual fright. Ether is a stimulant, chloro-
form a depressant; ether poisoning is preceded by a
definite train of marked premonitory symptoms, chloro-
form poisoning is rapid and insidious.

The See Ministry.
The formation of a State Ministry by N.S.W.
Premier John See has moved Sydney Telegraph
to rear up on its latest labor-saving machinery and
scoff. That a Protectionist Ministry should reign
in a State which, only two weeks before, went bald-
headed for Freetrade in the Commonwealth elec-
tions, strikes the King-street organ as highly
absurd. The fiscal issue, it declares, is unsinkable,
because in the event of any extraordinary vacancy
occurring in the Senate the two State Houses,
sitting together as one body, appoint a Senator to
the vacancy; and if the majority of the two
Houses are Freetraders, they can, of course,
further increase that alleged tiEin majority in the
Federal Upper House. The Telegraph might
have urged with equal wisdom that the spectacle
of a clean-shaven Ministry ruling a full bearded
community is anomalous, for that has just as much
to do with State politics as .the fiscal question has.
To argue that both Houses of the State Parliament
must be chosen from Freetraders in case (perhaps
once in three years or so) they may be called upon
to elect a Senator, is possibly the weirdest proposal
that has emanated from the very remarkable Free-
trade press; and as an exhibition of blind fetish-wor-
ship it will take a lot of heating. Evidently, in
the Telegraph’s economy, the interests of the
Sydney Importers are absolutely paramount; and
all else must he subservient to them. As
usual, the Telegraph carefully misrepresented
the case with regard to the appointment of a
Senator. Every*three years, there will be in each
State an election of three Senators whose
tenure of office has expired by effluxion
of time. At that election all extraordinary
vacancies will be also referred to the people.
One of the great advantages Australia hoped to
reap from Federation was the shifting of the ever-
confusing fiscal issue to another arena, leaving
State members free to deal with questions of
social and industrial importance. But apparently
such questions as Local Government, Education,
Closer Settlement, and the thousand-and-one
urgent matters left to the State Parliament, are
all to be sacrificed in order that a Freetrade
majority may be able, should the opportunity for-"
tunately occur, to elect a Freetrade Senator to
swell theREiD-M'MiLLANfollowingfor a few miser-
able months. By the Billet of J. Scandal
Carey, this is too much !

“ Specific Inherited Disease.”
Dr. Neild, of Melbourne, shook up the news-
papers somewhat in his evidence in a recent
infant-death case, by stating that over half of the
Vie. farmed-baby mortality is due to “ specific
inherited disease.” One disease oidy is meant,
and every general practitioner knows how terribly
frequent it is in this country. A year’s collection
of the miserable, puny creatures who are born
to rot slowly away, after a few months of
wretchedness, wouid form a fine exhibit for
Australia at the Jookoyork’s reception; and
the prudish idiots who scream at the mere
hint of a C.D. act might form a committee
to introduce it to his notice. So chaste and deli-
cate are the sensibilities of the saints who run
Australia that the public ignorance on the subject
is positively appalling. There is no question more
talked about amongst men, at least, and none
about which less is popularly known. Any
attempt at legal restriction would be howled
down as a flagrant insult to “all pure-,
minded persons,” as the phrase goes; and that
portion of the Vic. Health Act which provides
for the restriction of possible contagion is allowed
to remain a dead letter. Walking centres of in-
fection are allowed to ply their trade unchecked,
although they could be arrested and detained for
treatment by a simple enforcement of existing
laws ; and a yearly-increasing number of helpless
babies are born with the finger-mark of Death
upon their decaying skulls. Young men, piti-
fully ignorant, sacrifice themselves all unknowing
upon the altar of “purity,” raised by the prurient
apostles of the great faith of “ Keep It Dark,” and
in their turn infect innocent women with whom
they come in contact. Ignorance—pure ignorance
—is at the root of the whole evil, and so long as
Australia consents to allow herself to be dictated
to by the Gimlet Brigade, so long will the curse
continue as the rottennest of plague-spots affecting
her domestic life.
A Federal Arbitration Court.
At the 16th annual meeting of the Victorian
Employers’ Union, President Arnall arose and
delivered a series of short, sharp yelps with regard
to the recent labor legislation. He opened by
abusing the workers, who, he declared, were never
satisfied and were always making new demands.
When they were satisfied as to working hours,
wages, and so on, they were not content, but needs
must evolve a desire to control their employers’
businesses by insisting upon unionists only being
employed. He fairly howled about the injustice
of the minimum wage, and wailed bitterly that
when the Wages Board fixed a minimum re-
muneration the men were not compelled to
work for that particular sum, and would go
on strike to secure a higher figure. Mr.
Arnall does not understand that a minimum
wage is binding only on the employer, who is
thereby not allowed to pay less, but can go as
high above it as he chooses. His soul revolted at
the sight of the feeble and indifferent workman
being driven by this iniquitous Act to look to the
State for employment. He adjured his brother
employers to watch carefully the proposed
Federal Arbitration and Conciliation Bill, with
a view to prevent drastic legislation. He'
cursed the Old Age Pension scheme as a hollow
fraud, which, while excellent in theory, dis-
couraged Thrift, and was an evil thing that would
burst up the provident associations. It would
be more necessary than ever for employers to
stand together if the proposed Arbitration Bill
became law. The Federal Industrial Arbitration
Court, with the power to enforce its awards, is
greatly troubling the sleep of Fatman. The Vic-
torian section of him is kicking very hard against
the Minimum Wage law and Factories Act, and
will make the most of any excuse to declare that
the Minimum Wage law is unworkable. N.S.W.
has no such law, and immediately the Customs
barrier is knocked down along the Murray a yell
will go up from the Vic. employer that he is un-
able to compete with the N.S.W. manufacturer,
who need pay no wages at all if he can, by judicious
application of the law of supply and demand, in-
duce people to work for food only. And the yell
will have a basis of fact which will make it dan-
gerous, and might lead to the suspension of the
Act in some way. Judging by Mr. Arnall’s
yelps, the Employers’ Union is very sore about
that minimum wage, and is much afraid that
the Federal Arbitration Court will shackle it on
employers for all time. So it will, if the Federal
Labor Party acts promptly, and, by the estab-
lishment of such a Court before inter-State free-
trade begins, gets the machinery ready to chain up
in one act the sweaters of Australia.

A Discarded Prophet,
Brisbane Courier deserves the eternal gratitude
of all good Australian Democrats. The Courier
insisted that the one issue at the Q. Federal
elections was the question of black labor. It is
quite possible that, but for insistence on this
point, a number of minor matters might have
obtruded themselves and confounded the
issue; but the Courier’s attitude averted that
calamity. Hitherto the voice of Bananaland on
this question had never been properly heard. The
eternal Property vote; the wretched juggling
with electoral rolls ; the miserable manoeuvring of
election dates and arrangements, had all conspired
to stifle the expression of the real sentiments of
the.country. At last, however (thanks to Federa-
tion) the Q. voter obtained an opportunity of
expressing under a system of pure manhood-
suffrage his feelings on the most vital matter
affecting his country —and what was the result ?
The Courier and its following were simply kicked
into oblivion. Anti-black-labor men headed the
Senatorial poll and occupied it almost exclusively;
anti-black-labor carried the North for the Lower
House; anti-black-labor (Page and Paterson)
carried the centre; while Fisher and Wilkinson
(both anti-Kanaka) carried the Wide Bay and
Moreton districts in the South, leaving the
Courier to comfort itself with the election of
Macdonald-Pateuson (who was struck off that
journal’s ticket), Edwards for Oxley, and W. H.
Groom (indefinite and sinuous as ever) for the
Darling Downs. To rejoice the alien-coddlers
still more, the Wide Bay and Herbert districts
(the great Kanaka centres) both returned anti-
blaqk Labor men—to clinch the country’s verdict.
Furthermore, there cannot be the least doubt, in
the face of these facts, that the moment Queens-
land gets electoral reform the State elections will
pan-out just as strongly anti-alien as the Federal
elections have done. The allotment-peg has been
a mighty factor in guiding the destinies of Banana-
land, but its clay is nearly at an end.
The Privileges of Politicians.
In printing the subjoined The Bulletin has to
remark that the late Commissioner Eddy never
allowed his subordinates to be jumped upon:—
Ed. Bulletin, —On the 20th March last the undersigned
were fellow-passengers by the express from Brisbane to
Sydney, and occupied sleeping-berths. At Armidale entered
a familiar figure in N.S.W. politics—ex-Minister of the
Crown and present M.P. —who demanded a sleeping-
berth for his son, which was not available.
The son, it would appear, was not provided with
a ticket, but the father declared that, being an
M.P., he was entitled to a berth for him. Ask-
ing the conductor how he dare question his “ rights” as a
~ Member of Parliament, the distinguished personage took
the conductor by the throat, used the foulest language,
and created a disturbance which would have led to his
being handed over to the police at the nearest railway
station had he paid his fare as a mere ordinary passenger
and not been a Member of Parliament.
The next morning, on hearing much talk about this
outrage, we gave our cards to the conductor and offered,
if the Commissioners prosecuted, to give evidence. In
due course we were waited upon and urged by a gentle-
man representing the Commissioners to appear at Sydney
Central Police Court on the morning of the 10th, and
subsequently were subpoenaed, and duly attended that
court to our discomfort, personal loss, and inconvenience.
After waiting an hour we were told the case was post-
poned, and it was politely intimated to us by the solicitor
acting for the Commissioners that we need not trouble
further unless we heard from him.
It would be interesting to know how a case of this kind
down for hearing can be suppressed, and by what right
the magistrate can “postpone” a case without hearing
evidence, or having a reason publicly assigned. There is
a strong impression abroad that political influence is
again becoming paramount in the control of the N.S.W.
railways, to~ their undoing. Possibly those responsible
for the increasing mismanagement know their own
interests lie in holding the candle to the political devil ;
but if members of Parliament of this type are to travel
free, the Commissioners’ servants and the public should
be protected, and suitable trucks or cages be provided,
with separate sleeping-kennels.—Yours, John Horrocks
(Sydney), Oswald M'Master (Brisbane).

According to the Q. Govt. Press, if there was
one tiling that made the Q. Labor Party stink in
the nostrils of the Q. public it was that Party’s
alleged “ pro-Boer ” sympathies. And as the Q.
public had, for the time, no means of contradict-
ing this statement, a good many worthy people
accepted it as gospel. The Federal elections,
however, told a different tale. The party that
had so often expressed its belief that the war was
a most iniquitous one, the party that stank in the
nostrils of the Q. public, somehow contrived to
beat the ultra-loyal section into a cocked hat.
The Buzacotts, "the Thynnes, Annears, Bar-
tholomews —all men loyal to the backbone and
publicly effusive—were left out in the cold while
at least three Labor candidates who had been
flamingly denounced by the Jingo Press from one
end of the country to the other as disloyal and
traitors, were triumphantly returned. If the
Jingo Press doesn’t mind, folks will really come to
the conclusion that Loyalism isn’t half so rampant
or wide-spread as certain journals say it is.

The Country Press Conference, wdiich sat in Syd-
ney last week, seems to have been successfully
nobbled by the big Sydney dailies. Over -10
representatives of country journalism took the
bait of the Herald and Telegraph and plumped
for the free-postage of newspapers throughout the
Federation. It is admitted that postage would
mean a slight extra expenditure on the part of
country journals, but the “extra” would be well
compensated by the better command it would
yield the rural journalist in his own market. Free
postage carries into his town or village the big
penn’orths of Sydney day by day. His paper
cannot compete with them either in quantity or
quality. His topics are all exploited, and Ms
journalistic influence killed. Instead of producing
a paper, he is forced to produce a rag, and live
rather on the commiseration of the public than on
any intelligent service he renders. The nobbling
of him by the Sydney press should disentitle him
even to commiseration. But his fatuousness in
not seeing where his genuine journalistic interest
lies is aggravated by his claim that a Govt, which
charges other industrial products carriage-rates for
sending them hither and thither should not charge
the-particular product known as a newspaper. The
man who sends his wheat or potatoes across
country must pay if the Govt, carries them for
him, but, according to this Sydney-bossed con-
ference, the man who so sends printed sheets of
advertisements and news is entitled to have his
commodities carried free. The Commonwealth
Govt, will blunder egregiously if it hearkens to
any such rhodomontade. The newspaper-maker
must be taught to pay for the use of public ser-
vices as the farmer, the grazier, the artisan or
other producer pays.
The seamy side of the S’African war begins to
slowly turn itself to the gaze of the British
public. The official casualty-list gives the total
number of British killed from the outbreak
of war in October, 1899, to the end of March,
this year, as 17,572, of whom 14,728 are
dead from bullets and disease, and 775 are put
down as missing—many of them missed by the
searchers after the battle and left to be devoured
by the vultures. The remaining 2069 are put
down as Invalids. That is, they have been dis-
charged as unfit for further service, and will, in
the majority of cases, be confirmed cripples all the
rest of their lives. Over and above this holocaust
there are many thousands who will die premature
deaths ofving to hardships endured in the cam-
paign. Nearly 18,000 men and 100 odd millions
of money ! “ The price of subjugating my coun-
try will stagger humanity,” prophesied Kruger.
Was the subversion of the two little States
worth it, even to gold-hungry Britain? The Boer
Republics might have been swamped for half the
money, had it been devoted to planting British
settlers and their families on the land so that the
Boers would in time have been outnumbered and
simply died of acute minority. The British Govt,
could not have bought the land openly, but it
could have worked it all the same, and done its
conquering much more thoroughly and legiti-
mately; but Rhodes’ “apple-cart” was in a
hurry, and, as a consequence, 18,000 British
soldiers are dead or ruined, Britain burdened
with debt, and the seeds of an undying South
African problem sown.
The latest Melbourne Jaypee horror comes from
Flemington, where, sortie months ago, a young
fellow of 18 was charged with vagrancy. There
was no prior conviction for crime against the lad ;
and, though he was shown to have been in bad
company, the beaks suspended judgment, and
gave him a week to clear out, allowing him to go
free on his own recognisance. Meanwhile, the
youth remained in Melb., in the hope of getting
work, until six days expired ; then he took train
for the country. Next day, his case was called,
and the Solons who had given him a week’s
grace for the express purpose of leaving the city,
calmly proceeded to sentence him in his absence
to a month’s imprisonment. The conviction
was duly recorded; and when the young
man, losing the job he had obtained in the
backblocks, returned to the city recently,
he was promptly arrested and gaoled. After
a few days lie managed to see a solicitor, who
took immediate action. Nothing came of this,
however, until the youth had done the greater
part of a month in gaol, when the Crown Law
authorities magnanimously agreed to grant a
pardon, conditionally on his indemnifying the
magistrates who had convicted him against any
action. The boy’s best course would naturally
have been to serve his sentence, and then attack
the Bench for damages ; but the uncertainty of
things and the desire for freedom impelled him
to sign the required papers and walk out of the
gaol to which he had been illegally condemned.

D. R. Thompson, M.D., writes to The Bulletin
from Hobart
Ed. Bulletin. —l have just read the verdict given by
the jury in the Coningham divorce-suit; and I agree that,
as the allegations against Dr. O’Haran were not proved,
he was rightly pronounced not guilty. Here, then, is a
parallel case to that of Suffield. In each case it was the
firm and unchanging denial of the man against the un-
corroborated statements of the woman ; and in each case
the man was supported by alibi—but how different the re-
sult ! Suffield’s denial was ignored and his witnesses dis-
credited. The more influential Dr. O’Haran’s word was ac-
cepted. The poor man, unfriended but by poor ones like
himself, was sentenced to penal servitude for ten years, and
afterwards died—cannot it be said “was murdered”? The
influential man, proclaimed not guilty, was feted and
idolised and £l9OO, and more, was quickly subscribed by
priests and other friends, so that he should not suffer
pecuniarily to the extent of a single penny. In
short, one law for the poor man and another law for the in-
fluential dignitary. This, however, is not the only-
aspect of the Suffield case. To my mind The Bulletin
pamphlet showed that Suffield’s innocence could
be easily proved on every point of the recorded evidence.
As a doctor, I have studied the printed evidence
line by line ; and if an inquiry had been held, with the
experience gained during my professional practice of
about 40 years, I believe I would have had no difficulty in
proving that the woman’s evidence was fabricated. Yet
two N.S.W. ex-Ministers of Justice, A. J. Gould and B. E.
O’Connor, who closed their ears to Suffield’s plea for
inquiry, are to-day elevated to the Commonwealth
Senate !- D. ft. Thompson, M.D., Hobart.
N.S.W. Labor-party’s Federal programme con-
tains a demand for Constitution-amendment
to substitute the mass referendum for exist-
ing methods of amending the Constitution.
To the argument that the existing arrangements
are necessary to protect State interests they have
replied : “ Oh, but we are all Australians now.”
Something has happened lately, though, which
ought to hint to the Laborites' why the smaller
States won’t look at things that way. In the recent
Senate elections, though the Barton and Reid
candidates were Australians, just like themselves,
the Laborites nevertheless ran two candidates,
thus knocking out their own contention that one
Australian is as good as any other. Also the
N.S.W. Laborites are (like the smaller States) in
the minority ; and so, when the matter was re-
ferred to a mass vote, they were over-
whelmed by the bigger bodies—just as the
smaller States would be. And they don’t like
it —they say that the mass vote doesn’t give
their party, with its special policy, a fair deal.
But if the mass vote doesn’t give a comparatively
small party with its special policy a fair deal, how
do they justify the attempt to fix the mass
referendum on the small States with their special
interests ? The fact is, they have struck precisely
the same snag that they want to tip into the
stream for the smaller States to run up against.
♦
The Commonwealth Govt, will need to walk cir-
cumspectly in all matters relating to British
interference in other countries. Branches of the
Australian churches are, for instance, trying to
persuade Barton that he ought to urge Chamber-
lain to make the guaranteed security of lives of
missioners a condition in the overtures for peace
in China. None of the Powers, it seems, have
thought of this condition, or having thought of it
they all very prudently ignored it. Missioners in
any country should have only the same security as
others ; in fact, if their professions about laying
down their lives were genuine, they should not need
as much. But in the second place, to guarantee
missioners’ security would lay the foundation of
frequently recurring interferences with China.
An intemperate missioner hit on the head with a
pagan chopstick might set Europe and Asia
aflame. Thirdly, if the Federal Govt, softly
acquiesced in this clerical scheme and persuaded
Chamberlain accordingly, the Commonwealth
would be morally committed to stand with men
and money by England whenever that country
set out, on account of a broken-headed missioner,
to partition China.

At Sydney Tivoli nothing has yet been
heard from Billy M‘Clain about going on the land,
but if Rickards could see his way clear to start the
leaping man from Dixie somewhere down the
Swanee River, it would be a blessed relief to the
tired scribe. The brightest point about Dark
William is the diamond that glitters far back in
his cavernous mouth. After hearing him droning
about being merely a prominent coon, Marie
Lloyd and the bewildering Rosie Aquinafdo come
like a long, long, iced shandygaff in a weary land.
Marie is still telling how the costers laid Folke-
stone under contribution, and singing of the wife
who didn’t know if hubby took milk and sugar ;
and, like Millie, she’s “ all right.” The more one
gazes upon the amazing performance of Rosie Aqui-
naldo the more bewildering it appears. She glitters
and scintillates in many weird positions in a white
figure-costume frosted over with sequins, and her
black, curly hair tied up with one white riband.
Ferry the Frog is not in the same street, and Rosie
ranks with Cinquevalli as one of the wonders of
the world. Some of her contortions are pic-
turesquely horrible, and others are grace itself ;
but everything she does is marvellously clever and
seems to be done with ease. At the finish she
slowly unties the lover’s knot into which she has
twisted herself, gives three little bounds, and floats
away like the Lost Chord.
** * *
“The World’s Verdict,” at Sydney Ly-
ceum, is in this scribe’s opinion a most shocking
exposure of the taste of a huge hunk of Sydney
population. From start to finish it is a crude,
drivelsome horror. It opens with the poisoning
of an ancient party who dies in the limelight—for
which he can claim no pity : any man who drivels
as that ancient did deserves to be poisoned. It
ends with a woman being tied in a chair under a
slowly-descending guillotine, and shrieking for
what appears to be about 16 hours, till the funny
man shoots the maniac who is grappling with her
rescuer. Still, if people like to listen to a woman
screaming herself hoarse, manager Anderson is
wise to engage women to yell to their delight.
There are many other items in the melodrama
calculated to cheer up the morbid person who
revels in blood and bones—especially a London
hotel where a maniac is in the habit of dropping
in casually through the window and looking for a
victim. The fact that the various ladies and
gentlemen who have to perpetrate the dreadful
thing mostly do their work well, does not detract
from the fact that the alleged play itself is an
unlovely horror.
** m *
At the Queen’s Hall (Sydney) Entertainer
Rodney plays a lone hand for two hours with song
and story, and plays it very well; while pianist
Henderson gives valuable support. One-man
entertainments are calculated to pall, but this
scribe much enjoyed Rodney’s little show. The
entertainer has a pleasant manner, and a big fund
of stories, and gives as much variety as one man
can possibly give. He lifted up his serviceable
voice and sang a verse of the “Old Kent Road,”
first as it is written, and then as Handel, Gounod,
Offenbach, Arthur Sullivan, and Verdi would
have given it. It’s well worth hearing, and so are
his song “Round the Block,” the “Cockney’s
Description of ‘Hamlet,’” and several other
items. Rodney says he is an Irishman, but he
can talk Yiddish, Cockney, Doric, and Hibernian
in a manner that suggests that he has kept some
very mixed company in his time.
