A 6 the Palace, the German who formerly
kissed his supposedly soon-to-be brother-in-law on
one cheek now salutes both in true Teutonic style.
The German who loves furiously, but with strictly
honorable intentions, and retreats with a flourish
of trumpets, as it were, when he finds his adored
is—like Werther’s Charlotte—“a married lady,” is
thoroughly characteristic of that race whose chief
love-story depicts the hero committing suicide
because he has permitted himself to feel affection
for the other man’s best girl. Like other men,
the German enjoys being successful in love ; but,
unlike other men, his next-best pleasure is to be
disappointed and to retreat with a beautifully-
broken heart, either in the Wertherian method or
more prosaically by the big front door.

Florodora at Her Majesty’s makes a
further impression of a rather long-winded pro-
duction that harps a little too much on the
dependence of the British aristocrat on the Ameri-
can heiress. The dialogue is spotted with good
things as when the sprightly widow enunciates
the great truth that it is easy for a woman to be
iaithtul to a man when he-is dead. Nevertheless,
the opera seems most at home when it merely
curvets round and relies less oil the
book than on its many and voluminous skirts.
Ihe dancing embraces every form of gymnastio,
Miss Carrie Moore being occasionally flung up
towards the flies, both in her own person and in
that of the dummy which is whisked furiously
°J er footlights towards the orchestra. As one
of the girl makers of the mysterious perfume,
Carrie boasts a coppery complexion with a rather
metallic voice to match. Hugh Ward, as the illegal
proprietor of an ill-gotten perfume, has a Glad-
stone make-up, but shakes as gay and vivacious a
mg as any ballet girl. George Lauri is, as usual,
George Lauri, and nothing much better of him
can be said than that.
** * *
Two benefits loom up in the near future.
Tenor F. R. Woodhouse, who has struck a melan-
choly chord of ill-health, is to be tendered a
Night m Bohemia,” at Centennial Hall, on the
evening of Saturday, 27th. All the best-known
vocalists, instrumentalists, poets, artists, and the
salt of Sydney m general, will take some part in
the show, which is under influential patronage
that can put up the dollars if it feels that
way. The Mayor is chairman, and it is
sincerely hoped that Mr. Sun Johnson will
sing “Way down the Swanee River.” If he does,
it will be a raging success, for anybody who
heard Mr. Johnson sing at the Illingworth Night
would travel right across the continent to hear
him again. Mr. Woodhouse has been ordered to
take a sea-voyage as a last effort to recruit his
broken health, and it is “up to” artistic Sydney
to take itself, its family, the best-looking hand-
maiden and the back-blocker within its gates
along, and pay for admission at the door.
The other is a complimentary farewell
benefit to Mr. G. F. Pack, well-known in Aus-
tralia as a composer who created the music for
“Matsa,” “Djin-Djin,” “The Forty Thieves,”
and other Xmas attractions at Her Majesty’s.
Mr. Pack is at present conductor of Charles
Arnold’s orchestra at the Palace, where the benefit
Tn 1 if lie place 011 Thursday afternoon, the 25th.
AH the Sydney theatres are sending representa-
tives, including the Misses Nellie Stewart, Carrie
Moore, Grace Palotta, Nita Clarke-Leete and
other 4.7 guns in the operatic and dramatic world.
Mr. Pack leaves for England per White Star
liner Afric in May.
** * *
Melb. Age, wilfully ignoring the real
causes of the slump, said: “An opera which fails
to draw even a moderate audience on Easter
Monday may fairly be assumed to be about played
out, so let us hope that ‘ The Bohemian Girl ’ has
now been finally shelved at the Princess’s—for
the rest of the present season at least.” The fact
is that Musgrove’s public would have been quite
capable of crowding the house for this bank holi-
day performance of “The Girl” if they hadn’t
known certain things about it. Firstly, the low-
pitched orchestra spoils the effect of the music.
The choruses have never sounded so thin and
dull in any amateur performance of Balfe’s opera
that Melbourne can remember. Another all-
sufficient objection to “The Girl,” as given at the
Princess’s, is the weakness of the principals. The
Count is a short, squat Mooney, the Devilshoof
has no voice to speak of, the tenor hero of the
English ballad opera makes the silly lines more
ridiculous by his foreign accent and mannerisms.
All the faults of the work are emphasised in
this, the weakest of Musgrove’s entertainments.

At Her Majestv’s(Melb.) the Nance O’Neil
business looks satisfactory, though not boomsome,
as of yore. On Saturday the loud, emphatic lady,
who has certainly lost a little weight and vigor,
revived her Magda, and showed further signs of
overwork in her hoarse tones and halting elocu-
tion. Words that begin with a vowel appear to
trouble Nance more than ever. She pauses
at them as though she were confronted
by a phantom aspirate, and had to satisfy
herself of its unreality before opening her
mouth. Which mannerism is only one of many
that seem foreign to the character of Magda, and
cast a deep shadow of doubt on the allegation that
Schwartze’s prodigal daughter has soared to social
eminence on the wings of her warble. To-night
LOUISE MACK (Mrs. J. P. Creed).
(Thursday) and to-morrow Miss Nance plays
“Peg Woffington,” and on Saturday an Ib-
sen tragedy—“ Lady Inger of Ostrat ” —is to
be presented “ for the first time on the
English-speaking stage.” The advt. also states
gravely that Ibsen “might have written the
part of Lady Inger for the young American,”
and although Melbourne is to get first bite at the
histrionic feast aforesaid, “arrangements have
already been completed for the presentation
before a London audience of Lady Inger by Miss
Nance O’Neil, during her forthcoming visit to
England.” If the tragedy be half as impressive
as M‘Kee Rankin’s ad., the English-speaking
world is in luck.

At Melb. Athenaeum Hall the G.B. Bio-
graph is showing how Lord Roberts led Barnum
Powell across the camera’s line of vision for the
sake of the patriotic photographer who had lent
a light cart to the British army in its direst hour
of need, and was rewarded with special sittings
from the General. The “Bobs” pictures are the
pride and joy of the G. 8.8. owing to
the light-cart incident above stated, but they
don’t compare for clearness with the Queen’s
Funeral, and twenty other subjects. The Funeral,
by the way, is not quite identical with the film
originally shown at the Palace Theatre, London.
For one night, or for two or three nights only, the
apparatus which cannot lie revealed the fact
that the coffin bearers let their half-ton burden
slip to the ground as they were trying to
lift it from the gun-carriage. This thrilling
accident was cut out of the picture by order of the
Crown or the Police (which are one and the same
thing for suppression purposes), and subsequent
reproductions give no inkling of it. The G. 8.8.
is going big ”at the Athenaeum, in a showman’s
way of speaking, but The Bulletin regrets to
think it would go a bit bigger if the missing acci-
dent could be slipped into the Funeral ceremony.
Ihe public do so like to feel shocked in modera-
tion.

“ The best way to get good government,”
says Sydney’s Mayor, “is to give the people the
power to govern in all essential respects.” Just
so- And Dr. Graham, as M.L.A., resisted in
N. S. W. Parliament a clause of a Bill empowering
the citizens to elect the Mayor of Sydney, and
another clause conferring one-man-one-vote on
residents in the municipality.
State Premier See has strengthened the
Ministry in backblockers’ eyes by appointing two
honorary members of Cabinet, viz., Hayes mem-
ber for the Murrumbidgee, Riverina, and Bennett,
member for Durham, Upper Hunter. The first-
named is an old Party man and popular in the
House. Bennett has come quickly to the front
as this is his first Parliament. His return in ’9B
was one of the surprises of the General Election.
He defeated H. H. Brown, who had represented
the district for about 20 years. Report says that
Bennett will have to go bald to win the seat again
Ihese country contests are not swayed by the
burning questions of the day, as the metropolitan
press takes for granted, but turn on local issues
Brown has been working quietly and steadily
right through the electorate, since his defeat
building up his position in an unobtrusive way

" * ien Royalty is the guest, either of an individual or a

I. Slush.
There is no room for petty jealousy now, every man and
woman will be there that night to do their best ; every
singer, every musician, every attendant will be inspired
by the hour and the opportunity, from the keepers at the
gates to the chief magistrate of the city in his robes of
office—all will unite as one.
One city, one bucket, one Slush !
Before 8 o’clock invited guests commence to arrive,
each in evening or official dress.
One garb, one Slush !
But the concert commences, and under the spell of
music and song the audience, from the highest to the
lowest, are carried willing captives through the golden
gates of sound.
One set of golden gates, one sound, one Slush !
The silken union jack covering the golden souvenir is
lifted by the Mayor, and three cheers for the King aits
called for.
One souvenir, one king, one Slush !
J. D. Slush !
A London Daily Mail correspondent
writes from China :
For good behaviour on the march, in camp, and, in
fact, at all times, the Americans and English were about
equal. The Indian troops also behaved very well, as did
also the Ist Chinese ; although they were all, perhaps, a
trifle too keen after loot. The Japanese were splendidly
disciplined, but, of course, unlike our Asiatic troops, they
had no European officers, and at rare intervals their
Asiatic nature was bound to penetrate through their
artificial veneer of Western civilisation. lam sorry to
say I saw several revolting instances of the Japanese
torturing their Chinese prisoners on the march to Peking.
But the Russians and French were quite indescribably
brutal in their behaviour, and one can scarcely think that
the troops sent by the latter nation were truly represen-
tative of the French army. One prefers to imagine that
they were third-rate colonial troops grown “ rotten ”
through too long service in the East. It was the French
marines who were the worst offenders—if there can
be a worst when all were so appallingly bail. Throughout
the whole time they were in the country the French and
Russian troops left a gory trail of murder, rapine, and
pillage in their wake, until the thought that the allied
forces were civilised troops avenging the atrocities of a
barbarous foe seemed absolute farce.
The Russians had a great redeeming feature which the
French had not—they would fight. During the siege of
Tientsin, at a time when the railway station was being
hard pressed, the Russian soldiers practically saved, bv
their stubborn resistance and ultimate repulse of the foe,
the entire garrison from annihilation. For the railway
station was the key to Tientsin, and it was here, through-
out the siege, that the fighting was heaviest.
Minister Trenwith is having a bad time
with the Melb. unemployed. When plain Bill,
the Labor member, in a seedy coat and an old
belltopper, Trenwith frequently led the unem-
ployed to assaults on Ministers, but now that
William is a Minister himself he adopts exactly
the tactics of the gentlemen he used to assail as
enemies of the people and bowelless autocrats.
Trenwith tells the unemployed there is work in
the country, and they must go to the country for
it, and the cry of the lone farmer who wants
to give good wages for good work and can’t
find workers is supposed to be loud in the land.
Meanwhile the unemployed insist that some of
those who have tried the country have got chance
jobs and have been worked like goaded niggers
for Kanaka pay, and swear that once outside of
Melbourne you never hear of that anxious farmer
who is clamouring for hands. If Trenwith is
genuine in his expressed sympathy, let him get
the anxious agriculturists’ names and addresses,
also their rate of pay, and convince people that
he is not merely a decoy created in the hope of
clearing Melbourne of the obnoxious unemployed
during the Royal visit.
It is no longer a secret (says N.Y. Critic)
that Mr. Laurence Housman is the author of “An
Euglishwoman’s Love Letters.” Mr. Housman,
who is a young man of 34 years, is an artist as
well as an author. While he has written a num-
ber of books, the “ Love Letters ” is the first to
give him an international reputation.


The demand is still increasing for Wilfley Con-
centrators. Last week, both the Lucknow-Canoblas and
the New Dayspring mines added Wilfleys to their plants.
There is no doubt that since the inception of the
“ Wilfley,” those mines using them have derived great
benefit from their use. We understand that the
“ Wilfley ” will treat all the tailings coming from a ten
head battery. The capacity is from 15 to 30 tons per day.
This is as much as any four belt machines. N. Guthndge,
Ltd., the agents, report brisk business, having at present
12 or 14 orders in hand.*

Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “ big prospecting shows ” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject toinspection. Cable address: “Option,”
Melbourne.*

“Rats”: If the bush public were not long-
suffering they would effectively inquire why the public
advantage should be sacrificed for the private benefit of
the few, as it now is in the matter of closing the banks
and other public offices on the slightest pretext at times
when it would greatly convenience the public for them to
be open. Example : Last Federal election day thousands
of bush people rode long distances to the towns, partly to
vote and partly to transact business with the banks and
the Land and Mining Offices. Although these places
adjdin polling booths they were all closed, and the weary
bushie must come in again, perhaps a 40 or 50-mile ride in
and out. No one wants to deprive these officers of their
legitimate holidays, but surely such days as these could be
tacked on to their annual leave to the advantage of all
parties.

Westralians: Another small boom m the mar-
ket, though almost without exception the mines were
beyond their intrinsic value before it They
must come to that value eventually, though the higher
they rise the greater will be the fall—which may be ex-
pected to take place not later than next week.
Great Boulders, 255., a rise of 2s. in a few days,
meaning over £160,000-and the rise was probably more
iustifled in this instance than in any other. Golaen
Horseshoe, £ll, a rise of £1 (or 000 , representing'
almost the total gold-production of six months, although
it is over 15,0000 z. a month, and the greatest output
of the field). How monstrous these changes when
considered in this light! Lake Views, £9 18- _
week thev were £8 10s., and a few weeks before under £7,
but a matter of half-a-nnllion is nothing so far as the
W A market is concerned. The mine has next to nothing
to do with it now, but it can be only a matter of
time when it will have all to do with it; that must as
surelv be the case with Westralians as with the mines of
every field. Associated, 555., a rise of ss. £125,000 in a
few days. Mine working at loss as usual. No divs. for a year,
and no hope of one for another year. Intrinsic value pro-
bably under 10s. Northern Associateds, 555. This mine
is cursed by its name, and unless the writer is greatly
mistaken, it has more than double the intrinsic value of
its god-parent, with which it has no connection,
and never had, in respect of line of reef, &c.
Kalgurlis, 87s.—a rise of £1 in less than a week.
Ivanhoes, £9 55., another rise of £l, which represents
£200,000! Brown Hills, £4 55., which, but f ° r the all-
round boom, would foreshadow the success of the Diehl
process. Perseverance, £7l7s. 6d., an inflation of £175,000
in a week, which is not too bad for a mine considerably over
£lOO,OOO in debt, that must either issue more shares or work
for over a year ere incubus can be lifted. The few other
marketable stocks are also up. There has been no dis-
crimination—they all move up and down together, which
is inconsistent with the rapid changes that take place m
the mines themselves.

stringers mentioned in the last two reports have entirely

Values still remain very slight, and rarely show more
than a trace of gold.” How refreshing is the truth about
a mine, however cruel!

Ironsides North (W.A.) : “Grade of ore again
disappointing. Although free gold was showing in the
winze for several days, bulk samples showed the low aver-
age of 6dwts. to the ton. Several holes were drilled in
the foot and hanging walls, the borings from which gave
no better results. 60,000 gals. of water were sold during
the month.” Some little consolation even in that. As a
rule, over there the mine with the most water has the
least gold.

Golden Horseshoe, W.A.: “ Development work
on the lode continues to reveal large bodies of ore of good
average grade. The eastern crosscut at No. 7 level has
not yet intersected the boundary lode at the No. 6 level.
This body is 30ft. inside the boundary, but appears to be
taking another twist eastward into the Great Boulder
ground, therefore the exploratory work now proceeding
in this part of the G. Horseshoe is being watched with
keen interest.” The writer would think so ! In view of

gets the great bulk of its gold, going back to the mine
from which it came, it is rather wonderful that shares
should, at time this is ’ written, be at £lO
ICs. buyers, which means a market value of
£3,lso,oou—much the highest on the field. Last
month’s production over 15,0000 z., two-thirds of which
must have come from the lode which came into the mine
from the Great Boulder, which now stands a show of
getting it back again. t m 9

£ * * *
Cable: “Two men, Alfred C. Bailey and H.
de Stedingk, have been committed for trial in London for
alleged fraud in connection with the subsidiary com-
panies of the West Australian Development Corporation.”
These are two gentlemen who made an early splash in the
Westralian market in London. Bailey—“ Mr. Arthur
Crawshaw Bailey ” in those days—founded the Western
Australian Development Corporation, of which he was
managing director: also he floated the Armadale G.M.
Co. and the M’Kenzie Gold Mines. All these and others
are long since only memories. Mr. Henry de Stedingk’s
was a fine name for a prospectus, and full use was made
of it.

Kalgoorlie Chamber of Mines (about the livest
institution of the kind in the Australias), has issued its
first district figures for 1900. Ore handled (all ways),
491,943 tons; gold, 733,5780 z., worth say, £1,844,786.
Divs. (eight payers), £1,163,991.
Boiled down, this is how the Westralian Govt,
ended the strike or lockout of Collie coalminers : it
agreed to pay the owners 3s. sd. per ton above contract
price for the coal supplied to the railways (which are the
chief consumers) on the owners undertaking to pay the
miners 6d. per ton more than they had been getting.
Sixpence to the men, thirty-five pence to the masters—
and the Forrest Rump has the armor-plated front to
point to the bargain and ask the crowd to consider how
solicitous it Is for the workingman !

Unlike dozens of other copper shows that have
been pegged in Westralia, the Anaconda (Murrin district)
keeps going ; the output is £3OOO a month, with a good
solid sulphide lode just being developed at 160 ft.
Died at Melbourne, 14th March, W. J. Begel-
liole, formerly manager of Bayley’s Reward mine. Also,
some three weeks ago, plucky John Clark, of the Brilliant
Worcester, and Victory United, O. Towers.
The second section of the new Mundio
at Mt. Morgan mine is now complete at a , pqn non?
£6o,ooo(first section, finished 18 months ago, cost £90,000),
but cannot be started until the long-overdue deluge
arrives. As the entire crown of the rpater
nearly disappeared, and with it consequently g
quantity of the treatable oxidised ore, both high and low
grade, the tendency is naturally towards co ment
on treatment processes that will enable the » »
to profitably deal with the huge bodies of sulph
begin at the 370 ft. level and continue r >S h t down to
Heaven only knows where. The Linda level (650 .)
shown the body as large and as healthy as at tne top.
The process adopted in the Mundic Works has
been evolved by Metallurgical Engineer G. A. Ricnams,
who, by the way, started on the mine in the early ‘J,
an ordinary eight-bob-a-day man, but who is now in a -
lute command. When the mine was given a decent snow
after the era of £1,100,000 a year dividends, the low-
grade ores, both sulphides and oxides, were take
hand. The West works were built; and successfully
treated, at a cost of from 4dwt. to 6dwt., the lOdwt. re
that was pitched over the tip in the days of half-a-c
a month divs. The special mundic furnaces were b
two sections on Richards’ design to treat the low-gra
sulphides. These have a slightly larger average 12 t.
—than the oxides, and require more careful reduction
and roasting ; consequently treatment costs a little more.
In 18 months Mundic first section returned
40,0000 z. from 50,000 tons. Most important feature or
process is the height to which gravitation has neen
availed-of. Ore descends about six floors ; and from t
time it goes in to the first hopper, after being trucked
direct from the mine, up to the time the tailings are
thrown over the tip, it is only handled once by manual
labour. The receiving hopper feeds direct into powerful
breakers, and the ore, reduced to about ljin. cubes, tails
into a dryer heated to a high pitch. All extraneous mois-
ture is removed here, the ore only retaining the chemi-
cally-combined waters which are extracted in tne
roasting. An elevator picks it up, lifts it 50it.
and drops it into the mill bins, out of which it is fed into
the Krupp ball-mills below. The mills do the final
pounding, and the floury ore gravitates to the floor
underneath, whence it is picked up per bucket ele-
vator, and tipped on top of the reverberating furnace,
70ft. high. Each furnace has 13 inside arches, of which
four are power-worked, the rest being merely gravitation
chambers. Compressed air is puffed into the four check
arches, and the speed of the ore is thus regulated in
going through. From top to bottom takes 24 hours, and
ore is roasted so completely that hand-rabbling is alto-
gether dispensed with, the stuff being merely shot into a
calcine hopper, and run into the 150-ton vats for chlo-
rination.
The principal difference in the sulphide and
oxide treatment is in the fact that the sulphide is only
reduced to one-half the fineness, and is fed straight into
the revolving furnaces from the ore-bin instead of being
hoisted up on a tower. Only eight additional hands will
be required to work the new section ; and its capacity is
3000 tons per month 1 In the old top works, where they
still treat the 2oz. high-grade stuff, nearly everything is
done by hand —feeding, rabbling, trucking, &c. Experi-
ments are being made in the new works with a view to
minimising dust-inhalation by employes. The life of a
“ shed hand ” at the Mount is not more than 8 to 10
years ; arid the mortality in a quiet, steady way from dust
and gas has been very noticeable.

The Dawes Range Copper Co. This is the latest
introduction to the London market from Queensland, of
which one Warren White says that “ in no part of the
world has he seen such immense lodes of copper exposed,
not even in the American copper mines.” The mining
manager says 44 for richness and. magnitude the ore-bodies
are unsurpassed anywhere.” Another mining manager
says “ that if the ore is worked from the face it should be
mined for 4s. per ton.” Australian promoters: Foster,
Vic. ex-Minister for Mines, Hans Irvine, Winter Irving and
Orowe of Queensland, who are talking big about it in
London. Proposed to erect a smelting-plant to treat
8000 tons per month, which at 5 per cent, copper, with
the metal at £7O, is £28,000. £13,250 is deducted for
mining, smelting and all other charges, leaving an esti-
mated profit of £14,760 per month ; but that is on paper,
where it is so much easier to put it than to take it out of
the mine.
• * * *
“ J.A.J.”: That tin scheme is one of the big
things of Queensland if sufficient working capital and
equipment are provided, as well as development; but a
very large capital is needed and plenty of brains.
Mt. Lyell (Tas.): “ Owing to the large quantity
of purchased ores from N. Lyell and other mines, the
big mine is now only sending from 600 to 700 tons per
day to the smelters, and the various faces do not present
the same busy appearance they did a few months back.”
But there is another and more important reason. The
best part of the ore-body above the tunnel levels has
been taken away, and the great .bulk of that
which remains is too poor to pay, and can only
be used as a smelting-base. Taken altogether the ex-
ploratory work from the tunnels has not been satisfactory.
Where the ore was high in copper there was no great
quantity ; and where there was quantity, there was no
great value. A year ago the mine looked like having a
long life ; it has quite a different appearance to-day,
indicating how difficult it is to judge the life of any mine.
Nor have its troubles come singly. Last year much
was said and believed of the money that was going to
be made by the purchase of other mines that were
too silicious for individual working ; already it has proved
not an unmixed blessing. It has been found in smelting
the South Tharsis ore that, owing to -the fine dissemina-
tion of copper pyrites, the smelting action is not what
was anticipated, and much is lost in the slag. The public
need not be surprised to find that important changes in
the management may shortly take place.
* ♦ ♦
North Lyell mine is now in the hands of the
newspaper butterman. “ No. 2 bench, from which the
chief supply of ore for shipment is obtained, continues
to produce marvellously rich ore as the centre of the
body is neared, some of the material being almost pure
bornite. The eastern ore body is also maintaining its
value both as regards quantity and quality, and on the
hanging-wall side there is a body of decomposed ore carry-
ing a large percentage of oxide of copper, and soft enough
to be shovelled out like sand; there is now no doubt that in
this eastern ore body, quite apart from the rich bornite
mass, the co. possesses a magnificent and probably un-
rivalled copper-mining asset.” The public would do well
to remember the things said of the parent mine a few
short months ago, probably by the same hummer ; and if
they don’t, they are much softer than the ore that can be
“ shovelled out like sand.”

doned-after the co. has been drained of its resources.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction North’s reconstruction
scheme has been hung up for the production of a balance-
sheet, as some shareholders thought there wasn’t any
cause to reconstruct. But the figures snuff-out that hope.
In the seven months (to end of Feb.) covered by the
statement the co. appears to have lost £9125. At the end
of its last year (July 31, 1900) it had a surplus of liquid
assets over liabilities of £6764 ; its surplus of liabilities
over assets at the end of Feb. was £2361. And the lead
market is pounds worse now than it was most of the time
when this loss was being made.
The elaborate statistics issued by the Gernmn
metal co.’s, which are the biggest dealers in Broken Hill
levd sulphides, must give the man who bought shares on
an £lB lead-basis the shivers. Except in the last couple
of years, the average price of lead for 12 months has never
for 20 years past been above £l4 11s , and only five times
has the average exceeded £l3 ss. Moreover, values of most
commodities have fallen in 20 years ; but possibly lead
ought to be an exception, because silver (in the produc-
tion of much of which lead is a by-product) has had so
awful a slump. Still, in spite of that slump, silver pro-
duction has increased enormously, and with it, of course,
lead-production also. Therefore, there doesn’t seem
much of a reason why lead should escape the general
decline. At the last Broken Hill Proprietary
meeting, the chairman announced cheerfully that the
world’s production of lead in ’99 (’OO figures are not even
yet available) had decreased by 20,000 tons compared
with ’9B. These German figures support that; the de-
crease is here set down at 21,000 tons. But the chairman
didn’t announce what these figures also allege, that the
consumption had fallen away 14,000 tons, and that, in-
stead of exceeding production, as it has done for many
years past, production in the two years ('9B and ’99) ex-
ceeded the consumption by 33,000 tons. Metal statistics
are probably worse lies than a lot of other statistics, and
besides, these German cos. are big metal buyers (in fact,
most metal statistics are issued by buyers); but, for all
that, they don’t make pleasant reading.

* » * *
Glenfine South, Vic.: Shares were recently
over £lO ; they are now a little over £5, for which there
seems to be full and sufficient reason. “ The north drive
on the west leer at the deeper level has been stopped for a
time at least; the country has become broken, and what
little stone continues is of poor quality. The end of the
shoot of gold in this direction appears to have been
reached. Going south on the same leg the stone continues
to keep strong, about Bft. still in the face ; but the quality
has seriously deteriorated. The stone may improve
again, but the falling-off seems rather ominous.
* * * *
There has been trouble at the Waihi gold-mine
(Maoriland’s biggest), in the Upper Thames district, over
the dismissal of sundry men who were Union officers, for,
as the manager alleged, exercising a disturbing influence
in the mine. Numerous representations were made to
him on the point, with the result that, although he
claims the right to exclude all sentiment from business,
he has reinstated two of the men.
* » * *
Aproposoi current newspaper pars re prospecting
in Rhodesia, S’Africa, and the chances of success, it
must be remembered that the British S.A. Co. may, if it
thinks fit, claim 50 p. c. of everything found by a pros-
pector, and the possession of a “ miner’s right,” costing Is.
(for the stamp) renders the holder liable to be called
upon for active military service. Living is not
dear, but fever is as prevalent and deadly on the high
veldt of Rhodesia as in the swamps of the Mozambique
Territory on the coast, though slightly different in char-
acter. Prospecting consists of hunting up “old workings ”
supposed to have been worked by ancient civilised people,
many of which, however, present no more appearance of
age than the old open-cut, abandoned 30 years ago, of the
Royal Standard at Woods’ Point, Gippsland. It is the
“Romance of the Dark Continent” that keeps London
speculators’ interest up in Rhodesia, not the gold that
has come out of it, for one mine at Kalgoorlie has
produced more gold in one month than all Rho-
desia has turned out since its occupation by whites.
Barring an unpayable trace near Umtali, and a little
round Macequece, in Portuguese territory, no allu-
vial has been found, although the niggers dredge it up
from the bottom of the Zambesi at Tete, with a gourd at
the end of a long bamboo, from their canoes. There’s a
tip for another distant-dredging boomster 1

The Bulletin’s newly-discovered and very
peculiar Mining Poet once more :
The statement for the year ending 31st Dec.,
1900, issued by the Australasian Department of the
Mutual Life Insurance Co. of New York, has been for-
warded to The Bulletin, from which it appears that the
figures published in these columns last week were Jncor-
rect. This statement gives the figures which will appear
in the official returns to the British and Australian
Governments, and from it we quote the following:—
Year Increase
1900. over 1899.
Premium income _ «. £9,215,544 £557,681
Total income .. .. .. 13,018,126 85,453
Assets 66,889,764 4,909,366
Contingent Guarantee Fund St
divisible surplus .. .. 11,614,368 1,320,210
New business issued & paid for 36,140,869 1,387,918
Insurance & annuities in force 234,393,817 18,240,796
It is claimed that in our remark that it is a very expen-
sively managed institution, due allowance is not made
for the fact that premiums on new policies amounted to
over 27 per cent, of the total premiums. Of course, an
institution which is extending its business so rapidly
must incur a large outlay, and as in a life-office the ex-
penses are mainly incurred in obtaining the new business
a comparison of the ratio of expenses to income is apt to
be very misleading as between offices whose proportion
of old to new business differs. The point to be arrived
at is whether the outlay has been justified by the results.
In the case of the Mutual Life of New York there appears
according to the present statement to be no doubt it has
been so justified.
With respect to the Loans on Policies on ac-
count of Deferred Instalments of Premiums, we are
informed that the system is not adopted with a view to
swell the assets, but because the method of valuation of
policies and allotment of dividends found most suit-
able to such a colossal Company, tabulates the
reserve required on each anniversary of the policy
assuming all premiums paid to such anniversary.
Against this, must therefore, appear as an asset any
instalment of premium not yet due, less cost of collec-
tion. This asset, the co. contends, is neither “intangi-
ble ” nor “ unproductive.” When any payment has to be
made under a policy, the asset is perfectly tangible, ana
if a policy lapse, the balance taken credit for is more than
covered by the reserve. It is also productive, as an addi-
tion is made to the annual premium-rate in the case of
quarterly or half-yearly payments to cover interest and
extra expense. We are also informed that the liabilities,
which we assumed to be valued at 4 per cent., are in part
valued on a 3i per cent, basis.
• • * •
Maoriland is the first of the Australias to break
away from the silly custom of offering' the local investor
in State securities less than is reverentially tendered to
the gilded British creditor. Last year King Dick floated
£300,000 4 per cent. Treasury Bills on the London market.
This year he is about to issue £500,000 bills at the fame
interest on the Pig Island market. There is no doubt of
its success, and M.L. will have the satisfaction of knowing
that the £20,000 annual interest will be spent in the
country; and will also pay income-tax, and be available for
further investment at the same rates to pay off the
British gold-draining loans on which no income-tax is
paid to Maoriland, the gold being forever lost to the
country.

SELL BEFORE THE CLEAN-UP
Twenty heads of stampers—four mortars in a line,
With enough engine and boiler power to equip another
line.
The copper plates are silvered and ready to absorb
The golden metal from this very rich mine.
The wine is o’er the fly-wheel, and the starting valve is
turned;
The cams revolve, stampers drop, and pulp flows o’er the
tables.
With a swish, swish, and twenty heads are beating to the
tune —
“Sell before the clean-up—take your profits while you
may ! ”
Twenty heads of stampers lie idle at that mine.
The reef is large and easily won—but gold is hard to
find.
Mine samples are all very fine—but mills do burst a lot of
mines.
So sell before the clean-up—take your profits while you
may !
Kalgoorlib.

At the overflow meeting about the Conine-
ham case at Sydney Town Hall, it was resolved to
request the Attorney-General to prosecute certain
persons concerned in the Coningham-O’Haran
case. Perhaps it may be expedient to let the
Gonmgham case slide for good—possibly it may
be better to put up with a ease or two of periurv-
forgery or forgery-perjury than to further stir up
the black blood of sectarian hate. But the Coning-
ham-case perjury-forgery question certainly does
yell to Heaven with a loud voice for an authorita-
tive legal pronouncement as to who did the forgerv
and the perjury. Somebody has richly earned
seven years hard.

One point about Melbourne’s gorgeous
preparations, in gala, for the Duke of York is
the disrespect involved to the memory of Queen
Victoria. The State is supposedly plunged in the
deepest mourning only three months after the
good Queen s death. Yet here it is to be all High
Jinks. Hopetoun plainly doesn’t like it. He re-
fuses even to go to the play.

Haute nuance in humble life
* * « *
Some time ago this paper published “A
Plea for the Loving Woman,” in consequence of
some most uncharitable remarks
passed by a titled Maoriland lady on
the subject of maternity. Apparently
Mainland’s Chief J ustice shares that
lady s (his wife’s) ideas. Two girls
were charged before him with con-
cealment of birth. Both had borne
their children under sensational cir-
cumstances, one of them being con-
fined in an open paddock in the
early morning hours, without the
slightest assistance, proper or other-
wise. After the child was born the
unfortunate creature dragged herself
from door to door vainly begging to
be taken in. The other girl’s expe-
rience was equally dreadful, and both
had suffered a night of dantesque
horror that might have been con-
sidered to have wiped out their
“ sm.” Of course, both the children
died under the circumstances, and it
is wonderful that the mothers did
not also die. Found guilty of con-
cealrnent of birth, they applied for
probation, and the Chief Justice
asked were there any previous con-
victions. lhe police said there were
not, but tendered the information
that both girls had been illegal
mothers before, for which reason the
Chief J ustice sent them both to gaol
for six months. The C.J. empha-
sised. the fact that the previous un-
legalised maternity was his sole rea-
son for this course. When the first
magistrate of a country allows his
decision to be thus influenced, what
can be expected of the average bull-
headed J.P, ?

™ e T e ’ s nothing but White Australia in
this letter, anyhow :
~ . Bulletin.—Why let any correspondent chide
the Maordanders and South Australians for placing the
Indian officers of the Imperial contingent on a different
footing from the white men of similar rank? The black
man, whether African or Indian, is inferior to the white
man , and, if not treated as such, soon begins to think
he is superior. The fuss over Ranjitsinhii’s cricket made
him a conceited little darkey, who took every opportunity
f? ord , lt ° ver white Australians, considerably higher in
the scale of development. If Ranji had been treated
according to Ins color he would never have been
permitted to enter a cricket-ground with a white man.
“ - haVe b< * h compelled to travel in the
coloured carriage on the railway lines, and in the
Transvaal he would have been sjamboked had he pre-
®Uand6d k® fo , ot P aths instead of on the dirt
load. This distinction between African and Oriental
niggers is lllogically founded upon the fact that the
Indian has a better brain, by persons who forget he is still
socially and morally only the equal of the negro, who is
his physical superior. The matter is simply one of evolu-
tion. The proper place for the coloured man of all sorts
is half-way between the white man’s house and the
monkey-cage at the Zoo.
A.ll the same, Ran j i’s Indian ancestors were
probably scholars and gentlemen at a time when
this correspondent’s British ditto lived in a hole
in a rock, with a coat of blue paint for clothing,
and gnawed raw bones with indecent noises before
throwing them at the hairy female in the corner.

Which recalls how, when old Nicholas of Russia

flourishing villages, through which he would pass,
and turn the steppes into flower gardens for his
Imperial eyes to dwell upon. Then, when all was
over, there was a big sale of lumber, and much
com pouched by the transformation artists.

“Galen” writes re Melb.’s water sup-
ply :
Our “Queen City’’-men sit at homo in snug content,
and think with pity of the poor devils ‘ way-back ’ who
have to drink contaminated tank and well-water.
Suburban householders squirm at the thought of the
tank, whilst putting a truly pathetic trust in the reser-
voir. Meanwhile, a certain health-authority has been
making a collection of the things which come to folk who
drink at the tap, and that collection will be a national
asset before long. Surely it is full time that our highly-
trained Board of Health staff became officially aware of
the condition of the water-supply of Melbourne and
suburbs, which comes, God knows whence, and goes down
the gullets of the people, in such condition that an out-
break of Van Yean plague seems only a question of time.
The morning bath is a pollution, the mid-day drink is a
sin, and the evening tea an absolute crime before Heaven
and Hygiene. To wash one’s hands is to defile them, to
wash one’s body to shame it, and to look at the water one
drinks is to weep. There are many people who cannot
affoi-d a filter to strain cff at least a portion of the impuri-
ties,and whose babes’ milk is diluted with this muck. On
all sides are heard complaints, but no one moves ; so the
infant-mortality creeps-up, and the adult-mortality will
creep-up, and filth will continue to foul the land until the
very stones of the streets protest against being watered
from the Corporation carts.
* # • *
When two colliers recently arrived at
Adelaide from Newcastle (N.S.W.) with their
load-lines submerged, the opinion of the Crown
law - officers was that theS.A. Act applied only
to vessels loading within the province. Recipro-
cal State action in this matter is therefore urgently
needed. But even when proceedings are taken
the unfortunate skipper is always made answerable
instead of the man who regulates the amount of
cargo, and occasionally “stands by” on the wharf
to see that it all goes on board despite mild pro-
tests from the skipper (who has his billet to look
out for).
• * * *
Presented recently “ by the officers and
teachers of N.S.W. Public Instruction Depart-
ment ” to a teacher who had figured for a space in
S’Africa as a “ captain,” a medal “for gallantry.”
Now, though the recipient, for aught The Bulle-
tin knows, may have deserved distinction 100
times over, it should have come from his quondam
military superiors ; and there would be something
supremely farcical in the conferment upon him of
a medal by his colleagues and personal friends,
were not the absurdity also indicative of the rapid
growth of the decoration-disease in Australia.
Any trinket that can possibly be construed into a
“medal” is borne ostentatiously on the watch-
chain, and every chance of donning a uniform of
any kind is eagerly seized. And, during the York
visit, the epidemic will reach the acute stage.

“ Lean Scribe ” descants to The Bulle-
tin on some of the joys of country journalism :
I recently answered a Melb. advt. Editor and re-
porter wanted for a country bi-weekly.” and (things not
being too brisk citywards) asked for the modest screw of
£2 a week. Portions of reply—“ Would you accept less
than £2 ? I have several applications at much less. Do
you ride a bike ? Are you a comp. ? A few specimens of
your literary work might be acceptable.” In answer I
wrote agreeing to take 355., but didn’t get the job even at
that. Proprietor of this “ country bi-weekly ” (who, by-
the-way, in each issue has in the place where the leader
ought to be a verse of scripture) subsequently informed
me that two “journalists” would go for less than my
demand, one at 305., the other at 255. I presume the
latter was the “ successful ” applicant.
One N.S.W. soldier at least has been
stricken with plague at Capetown.
• * * •
The Chinese theory the Plague, it is
said, is that the disease has, all to itself, a special
devil, who is usually due about the month of June.
But as the time approaches for his arrival, an
Imperial Edict is issued putting the calendar
forward a couple of months. Then when the
plague-fiend comes, he says, “ Hello, I’m late, I’ll
have to put it off till next year ! ” Then —having
thus “got the bulge” on the fiend —they, the
authorities, rectify the calendar again. Of course
this is “miserable superstition”—but is it any
more so than our “days of humiliation and
prayer ? ”

The Bulletin will shortly have to make a
big effort to start a Foundling Hospital, at which
children will be received “and no questions
asked.” At present, conventional respectability
is an ogre that battens on the bones and flesh of
the unauthorised infant. The police are eternally
fishing up incidents that prove how dreadfully
neces ; 'ary such an establishment is. One came to
light in Victoria the other day, when a woman,
arrested on suspicion of concealment of birth,
admitted that she had slain her infant, and had
been pregnant eleven times, but had only given
birth to three unfortunate mites of whom but
one is living. And this is the outcome of
Respectability !

knocked down by tram. Conductor hails cab and

getting fares out of tram co. Woman carried to

cart dashes up. Finding services not required,
ambulance is sent for and, after 20min., arrives.
Woman, a mass of torn clothing, dust and blood,
eventually carted off. And this is Marvellous
Melbourne !

“ Science ” on “ Female Modesty ” :
The lady who covered her face when surprised by the
bath-attendant (B. 23/3/l/’01) was by no means anomalous
—as might possibly be inferred. Very many women act
like the ostrich, fabled to hide its head in a bush in order
not to be seen by pursuers. That the idea is instinctive
may be seen by comparing- the common impression of
young children that when their eyes are covered or shut
their bodies cannot be seen. Doctors will tell you that
women under examination often find satisfaction in
covering the face with the hands. Casanova was told in
Constantinople that the most reserved of Turkish women
blushed at nothing when their veils were on. George
Moore, a good observer, in “ A Modern Lover,” represents
a modest girl-model as enduring the nude pose, but
shocked beyond bearing at sight of the resultant picture.
Incidentally, it has been observed that the centre oj

modesty among some Hottentot tribes, where steato-
pygy is considered beautiful, resides at the rear. For
some curious notes on these topics see Ellis’s “ Psychology
of Sex,” vol. ii, p. 46.
* e • *
“ Alba. D.” talks Art arid Psychology :
The chasteness of sculptor Mackennal’s “ Circe ” is
probably due to the absolute subordination of the ab-
dominal lines—a treacherous task to all but genius. In
denuding the figure of hip and abdomen, in the interest
of chastity, the painter or sculptor is liable to lose the sex
lines —the feminity of the figure. To the ascetic all flesh
is gross, and for centuries the Lean Woman has been held
up as the type of spirituality and purity. Psychologists
are fearless image-breakers, and they aver that the Thin
are, in numberless cases, sensual to a degree approaching
ferocity. It is the slim, wistful woman who is
the veritable man-eater, yet it is the slim, wistful
woman who is chosen to represent the angelic
type—praying lips and upturned eyes. The heavy
young damsel known as the full-blooded Hebe —heavy
bosomed, broad-beamed and lazy, is supposed to be the
embodiment of wickedness. As a matter of fact, she is not
fond of man, but of his dinners, his money. When she isn’t
eating she would prefer to spend her time in long, brilliant
periods of sleep. She predominates in our pictures, sur-
rounds our beer-machines, simply because Fat is synony-
mous with vice and ease. Rich men pass gold bangles to
her over the counter-lunch. And the Thin Girl—who is
all that the Fat Girl ought to be —hits a type-writer in a
stuffy office, or frets out her sensuality in a slop clothing
factory.
* • * »
The riiie-range habitues of Randwick,
despite the Supreme Court injunction obtained
against the practice, still keep blazing away at
the targets. Residents of Coogee at rear of range
forecast that someone will be hit shortly. The
rapid-volley-firing folly, which wastes much am-
munition that the taxpayers pay for, is chiefly
responsible for stray shots. Lined up in 50’s or
more, with instructions to fire within a time limit,
how can raw recruits help “ strays ” ? The rifle-
man who aims slowly and carefully, however new
to the game, cannot send a bullet over the hill.

A casual stranger of meandering mind,
taking a constitutional near Williamstown (Vic.)
lately, wandered into the quarantine ground at
Cut Paw Paw. He saw nobody, and was strolling
homewards when an enthusiastic official spied
him, and he was promptly secluded, vaccinated,
and kept there a few days till his indignation
cooled off. They have a smallpox patient there
just now, and as it isn’t often Victoria gets a case
of that sort she likes to make the most of it.

“Phormi”: Par. in B. 9/3/’Ol, re jury-
disagreements, doesn’t state the whole case.
Whilst many, do dodge jury-duty in the way
stated, there are others who are afraid to trust
the judge with a “guilty” verdict. I remember
alO days’sittings of a M.L. Supreme Court, in
which only in the first case did the jury find a
“guilty” verdict —expecting that the accused (as
one of the jurymen told writer) would get six
months at the outside. The judge, however,
dealt him out a savage three years “ hard.” Re-
sult, no more convictions that sittings.

“The 20th-century woman,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “is easily
going to ‘out’ her fin-de-siecle sister, according to
a life-taking case just concluded. Maud
and John were lovers. Maud, a fine-
looking, upstanding filly, coming on
rapidly, and with enough physique and
affection for half-a-dozen Johns. The
man, a respectable', four-meals-a-day, go-
to-bed-at-ten shopman, had no right to
indulge in such Indian curry as Maud.
Clouds in the shape of relatives arose ; a
storm brewed ; and Maud, ‘ heeled ’ with
a loaded six-shooter, arrived at the shop
of John and dropped him dead in his toy-
tracks. Arrest—jury. Maud explained :
had gone to shoot herself before her cold
lover—struggle, and he was accidentally
shot. All evidence, even her avowals after-
wards, pointed to murder. Nathless, the
usual petticoat-blinded jury acquitted Maud.
But the Crown intervened and prosecuted. Mur-
der evidence still more damning, but jury again
acquitted ; • then the Crown charged Maud
with attempted suicide. Of course she must
plead guilty, and Justice Phillimore ladled
her out 15 months’ hard labor ! Now, what
will the Commonwealth pay for that gentle-
man’s services for 12 months? Maud’s de-
meanour in court excelled that of a veteran
Old Bailey barrister.”

Advt. in an Ipswich (Q.) paper :
WANTED at once Three Aldermen for the Muni-
’ ’ cipal Council. No Gray or Bald-Heads need
apply.
The Ipswich satirist is slightly at sea. It isn’t
the gray or bald-head that is the trouble with the
average alderman—it’s the paunch. Prohibit all
aldermanic candidates of more than a certain
girth and you’ll soon find an alteration in the
municipal policy.
RECESSIONAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wandered, crowded by the crowd
Which, at the Show-time, Sydney crams,
And all the time I cursed the loud
Procession of tumultuous trams.
At least ten thousand times a day
I had to waltz out of their way.
As flooded streams sweep round their curves
They thundered ceaseless, car on car,
Sure only he who has no nerves
May know exactly where he are,
Or where he art, or where he am,
When every moment means a tram.
What bells and bawls, what squeaks and squeals,
What screams and shrieks, and how they shake
Themselves, when to the grinding- wheels
Is hard applied the hoarse, harsh brake.
A Bishop would say many damns
(Nor e’er be heard) amongst the trams.
And now, returned to the grim gum
From spree and street and shop and show,
Amongst the tramless paddocks dumb—
The Station’s pianissimo —
You’d think a rare relief I’d find
At thought of trains left far behind.
And yet, amid the untongued tracks,
The soundless scrub, the silent; plain,
Whence comes no noise save when one whacks
The bush, I long for trams again !
You’d say the contrast could but soothe—
Alas! the opposite’s the truth.
And frequently—thus far from town—
The reins upon my neddy’s neck
I drop, and dream of knocking down
For ever an eternal cheque.
And then my heart leaves ewes and lambs
And listens to the longed-for trams.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ „ . Sydney, April 16, 1901.
Mr Dear Arini,—
, A.J.C. fourth day’s racing was close and excit-
ing and the day itself was close and depressing.
With 75 per cent, of moisture in the atmosphere,
and wearing its new winter clothes, Society must
have reminded itself of a steam-jacketed vat.
Every woman over nineteen looked wan and
hollow-cheeked.
A few women wore all black hats with gold
buckles. Nellie Stewart’s raiment was among the
most noticeable in that crowd of 12,000 odd. It
was of lovely cream silk with yeasty chiffon ; and,
with a pale blue hat, the effect was lighter than a
syllabub. She brought her little daughter, who
was in deep mourning-for a favorite doll.
Nellie introduced other infants to her own,
and the small child responded with quaint
gravity and invitations to view the dead doll
which is still lying in state. Grace Palotta,
I forget her frock, but I remember the big red
rose tucked into her hair (nestle is, I believe, the
suitable word), after the fashion O’Neil. Mrs.
Claude M Donald (once Miss Ethel Greene), wore
a startling frock of ficelle lace with a pink over-
dress cut like asparagus, and a hat with black
feathers which would have made her comely face
look creamy instead of yellow, but for that 75
per cent, of moisture.
Boleros and zouaves were plentiful at the races.
The bolero is first cousin to the zouave, and both
are simply wicked unless their cut and your figure
are slenderly perfect. A large-hipped woman in
a zouave ! There’s one line of demarcation above
the waist, then the waist-line and the light strik-
ing straight down ! O, the Artist does hate the
zouave ! I once saw so good a figure as Mrs
Brough s get quite a hunch-back effect out of a
short black velvet zouave worn over a light bodice.
Such very tall women, and such a lot of them
were at the races. Some one told me that they
came from the Hawkesbury. I know a woman of
five feet eleven, and yet she wears cork arrange-
ments in her shoes to lift her another half-inch.
We usually associate the position of Mayoress
with rotundity of figure and a thick neck. Most
of the Mayoresses I’ve known got very hot and
shiny-faced after their three-thousand’th conse-
cutive hand-shake. But Mrs. Graham, gay and
girlish-looking, accomplished four thousand hand-
grips (assuming that everyone was thoughtless) at
her first reception on Monday last, and finished
looking no hotter than the ice she ate. And the
long, long smile (long in point of time, please)
really looked to be her own, and not merely an
official acquirement taken over with the Town
Hall suite of rooms and other municipal per-
quisites. Mrs. Graham wore a black-and-silver
frock with heliotrope and black hat, and received
in a foliage bower that would have made a good
aviary had it had wire outside its walls of green
Decorations and refreshments were all that the
artistic and hungry might desire, but the crash-
bang of the organ was too loud. I did not notice
this but myself. A lady whose double-
barrelled name has r’s and b’s in it couldn’t find
her card when it came to having the name
announced. So she spoke it in low, incisive tones
to the usher. He, raising his voice against the
organ s, announced Mrs. Augustus Johnson.
“I could forgive the Augustus and the John-
son, she said, “but not the missus.” A number
of race-dresses came. Also a lot of dresses built
to make you scream.
An odd thing about this reception has been the
rarnd spread of excitement since it was anouneed.
u 8 Somebodies, °f course, were ready for it. Then
the Anybodies, even from away back, began to
rouse up, and finally the Nobodies, from far and
near, poured into town, and from 3 to 5.30 p m
fairly assaulted the Town Hall. Wild-eyed, de-
termined-looking, they were intent upon greeting
the Mayoress ; but it was with a bigger ambition
—they were famishing for York ham !
The rational way to enjoy a Mayoress’s recep-
tion is to go with a party of, say, four. Go early
and secure a sofa or five or six chairs close to the
foliage sanctuary, syndicate your skirts to keep
the whole of the lounge or unoccupied seats, get a
little table m front of you with a large stock of
assorted pastry, and say “more tea, please
waiter, every time one passes. Hundreds of
persons go to Mayoresses’ receptions in the
rational way, and so trained are some of their
faculties that by the time the reception is over
they have an accurate knowledge of about 3000
toilettes from the skirt-binding up to the quality
of the toque crown. And their watching has not
affected their preying either, or their lien on the
unoccupied seats.
One of the frocks at the Mayoress’s reception had
a vest collar and cuffs of that beautiful ribbon-
work. which, at a little distance, looks like hand-
painting on satin. The ribbon, about the tenth
of an inch wide and shaded from palest to darkest
violet, was fashioned into flowers, rosesand bunches
of lilac, on a satin background. It was wonderfully
cunning ” and becoming, and it has a historiette.
Ihe damsel who wore it—a brunette of about 19
—saved it from being made into a tea-cosy. Her
mother had seen, admired, and acquired it for
that purpose.
Trebelli Dolores’ second warble came off last
Saturday. Toe singer still sticks to the classical
programme Handel—Purcell—and composers of
the 15th and 10th centuries, and high-toned
moderns such as Massenet. By the way, the old-
tlme < r ora P osers just mentioned had a good idea of
their business, their music seems unusually fresh
and spontaneous—is the local article too vain to
take a hint ? All the same, going back to the
programme, it’s very much an open question
whether a lest exalted programme, musioally,
would not bring better houses. Australia
is not very bright at its music, and it likes
3 tnnll to be homely for instance on
Saturday I m Ower Young to Marry Yet ” and
, n ®, Laughing Song ” were rapturously received,
oth being old friends. Mr. Clarance Newell
played several things on the piano in a nice, kind,
inoffensive way, and was encored once or twice.
, A Politician’s wife, who is known to
difficult with her domestics, lately issued
invitations for a select little dinner-party; but
when the evening arrived, and the guests were
assembled, the hostess was horror-stricken to dis-
cover that cook, _ laundress, housemaid, and even
coachman had disappeared, leaving their mistress
and her daughters to struggle through with the
dinner-pa rty as best they could. This is the lady
who once expostulated ” with her coachman bv
turning the hose on him.
Doctors of this State are giving a dinner and
reception at Hotel Australia to-night to profes-
sional brethren who have served with the forces in
AA- D you have a medical degree and thirty
shillings you can be there. This was the heaviest
faster the Australia has known. For the second
time in its history its tower was densely inhabited.

Did Everybody read Mr. Hennessy’s prose-
poem in last Monday’s S.M. Herald? If you
i d “7^ cry body—didn’t certain days of your child-
hood flash back upon you—days when you had
played the wag and had eaten nine immature
quinces with six ounces of brown sugar? Spots of
the letter reminded me of Dan O’Connor. For
instance: “Biff the concert commences, and,
under the spell of music and song, the audience,
rom the highest to the lowest, are carried willing
captives through the erolden gates of sound.” Mr.
Hennessy was “induced”—he should have said
inspired to write that letter for the inform-
ation of the community,” and, from the apparent
accuracy of his knowledge, he must have enter-
tamed Royalty, probably in that lane off Hunter-
st., where his modest brass - plate clings to a
dingy wall. Strong men read that letter breath-
less and bare-headed. Reverential grovel exudes
from every full-stop. If we took Mr. Hennessy’s
advice, and if by-and-by the Ancient of Days
himself came down with the Seraphim and Cheru-
bim in attendance, in what greater spirit of self-
abasement could we receive the Divine and
winged beings ?

“S’Rupert’s stepmother, vastly popular with
Victorians, as a rule, awakens much envy among
the more ambitious dames of her own set. These
high-steppers bitterly resent the Widow’s sort of
semi-official standing, attained to some extent by
her own liberality and. geniality in periodically
welcoming to her own house societies of various
kinds, en mane, backing up bazaars, and putting a
helping finger in every pie. Handsome Mrs.
What’s-her-name, who gets credit for wanting to
oust the Baronet’s relict from her pre-eminence,
seldom spends a penny or wastes a smile on
the world of nobodies. Once in a way,
she condescends to boss an exclusive Holy of
Holies at some charitable function, where she
dresses down her colleagues and tactlessly cold-
shoulders would-be customers. Then she wonders
why the public plumps for the Widow of Clive-
den.
“The Melbourne Tramway Co. must have
spent many thousands in completely relaying the
St. Kilda Road track. This is another bit of
work done in view of the Royal visit. The track
has also been elevated 18 inches. This was made
necessary by the St. Kilda Road improvements,
the whole road having been re-made above its
former level. It is now quite the finest and
longest boulevard in Australia.
“ A hot controversy rages among C. of E. folks,
about the erection of grand-stands on the Cathe-
dral Close, facing Swanston-street. William Cain,
a lay member of the chapter, defends its action in
making hay while the Jook shines; Parson
Hindley leads the attack. Very nasty things
have been said on both sides, and, of course, much
Scripture has been quoted. Even Adam and Eve
have been dragged into the controversy. The
tem per displayed suggests Cain and Abel. Hindley
is Abel. J
That Charity fair at Como (the Armytage
place) was less chic than in former years. It
happened, not in a nice garden, but in a paddock,
as if it were a Sunday School picnic or a Salv’-
army encampment. One approached the concert-
room through what appeared a fowl-run, and hens
are hens all the world over.
“Mrs. Vallange, young and pretty as ever, in
pale grey trimmed with cream lace, grey purse-
bag slung to her belt, white chiffon ruffle with
tuoked scarf-ends and black hat, sang agreeably.
Both she and sister Ruby (who contributed a song
m a pretty little twitter) sport the newest
coiffure— i.e., puffed-out but very smooth, hardly
waved at all. The front hair parted to one
side, ends on the forehead in one, two or
three smooth stiff curls rather like Carmen’s
lovelocks’ or a Coster’s ‘kiss-curls.’ Not
so pretty and light as the one loose little centre
curl of yore, they look smart enough unless the
hair is very dark and massive. Some people, of
course, will travesty the idea, and pile over one
eye a cluster of short, solid sausages. Gold purses
and lace, lisse or chiffon boas are epidemic. Veils
are prevalent again—fine, black ones, with an
occasional big dot.
At the fair, S’Rupert’s liege lady provided a
refreshing streak of variety among many mincing
fashion-plate women—all studied mannerisms and
intonations. Nowadays she is rather a hand-
some woman, with expressive eyes, marked fea-
tures, and a so-so complexion. There was nothing
notable about her Como costume except a droop-
ing hat of coarsest magpie straw, but she stood
yarning with a white linen apron round her neck
to keep off the sun, while her neighbors posed
and simpered round her with one eye on the
photo, man.

Ella Russell is not unlike Miss Lorraine
in looks, though rather less bulky of figure
and not so fresh and pretty in face. She wears
her own nice golden hair, has a large mouth
and is very dramatic. Indeed, as the tragedy
thickens, her vigorous acting rather knocks the
wind out of her voice department. Her first
gown, of creamy satin, has a brocaded front, stole
sleeves, &c. She sits out to take the veil in
frosted white brocade and hair down her back
and sings outside the fortress in decollete black
velvet and black chiffon head-wrap. In this
mournful scene, sharp eyes spied gaping placket
or pocket, and a glint of white between bodice
and skirt, but one can forgive a trace of untidi-
ness in the toilette of a heroine whose whole world
is tumbling to pieces. The pleasant, plump trou-
badour seemed slightly flurried by the impetuous
caresses of his new Leonora, who ruffled his hair
for him, and bustled hihi generally Salvi
always makes such an ideal ‘ Mammy’s boy.’ In
Carmen,’ remember how he bursts into audible
blubbering when he hears of Mamma’s illness and
bellows Omy poor muzzer ! ’ In the ‘ Trova-
tore prison-scene with his gipsy parent he looks
quite the model lad, in juvenile black velvet tunic
and knickers, nicely braided with yellow.

“The census people really should be more
careful. In the paper left on the respectable
householder they give a specimen form to show
how the document should be filled up. Here is one
or their specimen entries:—
Anne Campbell, servant, aged 27
Janet Campbell, servant’s daughter, aged 12.
So that the imaginary Anne had a daughter at the
age of 15—a somewhat early age, even in this
country, surely. Also the governess living in the
house has an infant son !!! * 8

No Waste of Words.
Evidence Which is
Right to the . . . Point, and Reliable.
If pain attacks you, or you notice any deviation
from regular health, try and locate the cause, and
discover which organ of the body is weakening.
If the kidneys are at fault—and in nine cases
out of ten when health fails they are—look well to
their restoration to health and strength.
They are the filters. The purity of the blood
depends upon their cleansing powers. If the kid-
neys get weak the blood soon becomes impreg-
nated with impurities. As they reach a more un-
healthy stage a scalding or irritation is felt, while
a dull ache or pain in the back makes you mise-
rable. If the water, when allowed to stand 24
hours undisturbed, shows a sediment, or is cloudy,
you cannot afford to ignore it. If allowed to go
on, your face will get pale or puffy—especially
under the eyes—the feet or ankles will swell, or
the heart begin to act badly.
Kidney disease weakens thousands daily. An
eminent scientist once said : “Take care of your
kidneys and your body will take care of itself.”
Mr. T. J. Castner, of 8 Victoria Parade, Pad-
dington, N.S.W., wrote, on 28th August, 1900,
saying : “ Some five years ago I was under medi-
cal treatment for severe pains in the back ; in
fact, I was completely done up. After giving a
fair trial to the doctors, who then wanted me to
undergo an operation by crushing a stone in my
bladder. I was advised by a friend to try if
Warner’s Safe Cure would benefit me. After
taking three or four bottles, I felt better. I con-
tinued the course for about five months, and am
now, thanks to Warner’s Safe Cure, free from any
trouble whatever.”

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The new King’s title: Edward the Caresser.
There is a Melbourne impression that
National Treasurer Turner’s ill-health will pre-
vent him continuing in office.
Madden perhaps consoles himself with the
idea that Chamberlain was afraid of his accom-
plishing a Napoleonic coup d’itat.
David Syme is hanging a temporary
portico of pine and stucco on the front of the
new Age building in Collins-street, to gratify the
Yorks.
Dean Farrar, whose serious illness was
reported last week, schoolmastered Primate
Saumarez Smith in Marlborough College in the
latter’s Latin-syntax days.
Kingston and Holder both confess that
they have no committees, no secretaries to thank.
In the Commonwealth election they left them-
selves in the hands of the electors with very good
results.
“N.” : I never saw it in print till just
now that Clarence and York were rivals for the
hand of Princess May, and she preferred York all
the time. One might have imagined it was
coming.
J. Wilson, fertile third time “ runner-up ”
for M.L. rifle-championship, is the sole sur-
vivor of the Wilson family, who—father, mother,
sons, and daughters —were murdered in the
Poverty Bay massacre by Te Kooti’s followers in
’6B.
“ Melb.” : Demise, Ben Teesdale, aged
88, the handsome, white-bearded, florid, fur-
capped veteran who since time began lias been on
view as he sunned himself at the polished brass
railing in front of Cleeland’s Albion Hotel. For
years uncountable he had been Cobb’s agent.
In the Austral Light (R.C. mag.) one of
the cloth tells how lie was travelling with a doctor
who was expatiating on a successful operation for
trephining. The priest asked “Where is the
patient?” Doctor: “ I don’t know. His cere-
brum is in that jam-tin, and his cerebellum in that
cigar-box.”
The Duke of Clarence, eldest son of King
Edward, was born very prematurely, about six
weeks before the expected time. He was brought
forth after the Princess had been skating, at
Windsor. The layette was at Marlborough
House, and the youngster had to be packed in
cotton wool.
Is there a Boodlewraith Curse 1 Since ’93,
MTlwraith, Drury, Byrne, Palmer, Dickson, and
Chataway, all in the forefront, have gone to final
audit. Two alone have soft temporal seats —Sam.
Griffith, Chief-Justice ; and Nelson, President Q.
Leg. Council; and one is a.lawyer and t’other a
Scotchman.
Gregor M‘Gregor, the S.A. Labor senator,
got into politics through his capacity for telling
anecdotes with a humorous twist. He, unlike
most of the Laborites who have come to the front;
in politics, was an unskilled laborer—just a pick-
and-shovel man, and L not ashamed of it. It is his
great misfortune now that he is well-nigh blind,
having to be led everywhere. His memory is,
however, remarkably retentive.

Tom Bent (Brighton, Vic.) was in a Govt,
money-order office t’other day and saw a police-
man helping to count the coin. Bent was amused.
“First time I ever saw a ‘ Bobby ’ doing duty as
a bank-teller.” “Almost as strange,” retorted
the policeman, “as seeing a cabbage-grower in
the Speaker’s chair.”
The Syd ney women who have started a
special purse of sovereigns for Dr. O’Haran would
behave better by merging their contributions in
the general fund. Further, Dr. O’Haran, who
has just had a lot of not unflattering likenesses
of himself printed for circulation, would lose
nothing by being just a little less effusive.
Of the three new N.S.W. State Ministers,
R. G. D. Fitzgerald, member for Robertson, is
the least known. R.G.D. is a solicitor up Single-
ton way, and a good Federalist. His electorate
is merged into the Federal “Hunter,” and he
gave great encouragement to Barton when he was
deciding on a constituency.
Of the ten N.S.W. Conventi<mists of 1891
only two, Barton and M'Miilan, have won seats
in Federal Parliament; of the ten who sat in the
Convention of ’97, six—Barton, Lyne, M Millan,
O’Connor, Reid, and Walker—have won seats.
Three of these latter ten did not stand, and one—
Wise —stood and was defeated.
Walter Bennett, M.L.A. for Durham, ap-
pointed lion, member of John See’s Cabinet, is a
native of Maoriland. He has been 10 years m
N.S.W., almost all the time engaged in country
journalism. Mr. Bennett, who edits Dungog
Chronicle, has lately been trying hard to estab-
lish a weekly supplement for the general country
press.
One very much defeated S.A. candidate
for the Senate unblushingly, and with a bland and
child-like smile, told the electors at. the calling of
the poll that he had merely come forward to find
out where he was best liked in the State, so that
he might present himself there for election to the
local Parliament. The roar of derision was a roof-
raiser.
Poor old Mint-sauce Jones, the almost-
forgotten back-number of Vic. politics, was a can-
didate for the Fremantle (W.A.) House of Repre-
sentatives seat; but he didn’t get as many single
votes as the winner got dozens. A miracle of
the elections over there, by the way, was the
oppressive silence of Newcastle’s windy warrior,
Tommy Walker.
To cite Critchett Walker as a man ap-
pointed to an under-secretaryship on the same
basis as Fegan was a bad Bartonian “bloomer.’
Mr. Walker was a quarter of a century in the
Civil Service before he reached his Under-Secre-
taryship. He went in a youth, and climbed and
climbed. Actually, he is a perfect type of the
growth and bloom of the competent civil-servant.
Sunday Times, of Perth (W.A.), now
seems to be owned by a speculative R.C. priest.
Tommy Walker, erst of N.S. W., is editor. Vos-
per, when he started the paper, was agnostic and
died a penitent R.C. The padre held a mortgage
over the property, so it fell into his hands. And
now Tommy Walker —spiritist, freethinker, in-
fidel—runs the journal for its clerical proprietor.
It was interesting to watch Slangdon
Bonython’s election ad v ts. in his self- Advertiser.
The address occupied just a column ; and every
issue it was propped up hard against the choicest
news-stuff of the day. It leaned oftenest upon
the bosom of the Coningham case ; but when
divorce was “ off ” the farmer was invited to pass
from a description of the prize bull at Adelaide
show to a vivid description of what Slangdon had
done for the State’s producers. But he didn’t
stop at that—he was, of all the candidates, by far
the most liberal advertiser in the country papers.
Altogether, his was the most strenuous bit of
electioneering the dust-province has known, and
it resulted in Slangdon being sent in far ahead of
Premier Holder.
Lately the Supt. of Public Watering
Places prepared for the Pastoral Land Com-
mission a long, interesting, and valuable re-
port on the artesian bores of N.S.W. —the
best thing of the kind yet produced in the
State. To it the modest Minister O’Sullivan
prefixed a page of hifalutin and not too
grammatical Sullivanese : and then it was
' all issued with the Supt.’s name printed in
small type on the title page, and with the
O’Sullivan’s in about as big as the Govern-
ment Printing Office could supply. No
wonder his officers love him so !
Judge Onslow, the retiring Chief Jus-
tice of Westralia, was a Cambridge contem-
porary of present British Lord Chief Justice
Webster ; and, both being crack athletes,
especially on the long - distance running
tracks, they were thrown a good deal to-
gether. Onslow represented his ’Varsity
one year in the annual contests with Oxford.
He still retains his fondness for athletic
sports, but has more diligently cultivated
his classics ; indeed there were few finer
classical scholars at this end of the earth.
Varney Parkes, appointed to the
Sydney Harbor Resumptions Board, is the
second member of the late ex-Premier Parkes’
family to accept Govt, employment. A
daughter was recently appointed visitor to
asylums for the insane at a small salary.
Pai'KC.s often asserted that while he lived no
member of his family would fill a Govt, billet.
Musical critic Guenett, who has just
severed a 15-years’ connection with Melb. Argus,
has probably passed through as many accidents as
any man living. An Englishman of French ex-
traction, Guenett possesses a Gallic temperament,
which leads him into paths especially conducive
to casualty. From his youth onwards he has been
accustomed to being thrown off horses and out of
buggies. He has twice had his skull fractured,
and was given up by his medical advisers on both
occasions, but it was with the advent of the
bicycle that he was afforded full scope for his
Brassey-like "faculty of “falling-off.” Ever
since, he has been half his time either with his
arm in a sling or hobbling on a crutch.
