Leo Cussen, the Melbourne barrister, who
got “plucked” at the Wannon election for the
Federal Parliament, had a brilliant University
record, taking first-class honors in all his years.
A clever man, as well as an able lawyer (a civil
engineer, too), Cussen is not a good platform-
spouter. And how is a polished representative of
higher culchaw from town going to win a country
election on the Tory ticket, if he can’t stir ’em up
with his tongue ?
Frank Fox, the new chief of Melb. Age
staff, started life at 1G as a private in a line regi-
ment ; but, being bought out by his parents after
six months, drifted into presswork, and eventually
came to Australia for his health. He was on the
defunct Standard, and afterwards started the
ill-fated Sporting Standard. He joined the
Age about five years ago, but Syme’s staff has
had such rapid changes that there is not now a
man on it senior to Fox.
Oscar Bailey, the pushful “ infant ” of
Federal Hansard staff, and Adelaide’s only con-
tributor thereto, learned his lightning shorthand-
ing in Sydney. Soon, while still a lad, he wanted
to try the S. Aus. capital; and, other means fail-
ing, he tramped right across, via Gobar, Wil-
cannia, and Broken Hill. He got a chance to
report a rather intricate j udgment of Chief Jus-
tice Way’s, and the little bigwig was so pleased
with the result that Bailey was put on his feet at
once.
John Kidd is again a State Minister. A
quiet little man, he is liable to be overlooked
when forecasts are being made ; and none of the
cognoscenti tipped him for a place in the recon-
structed N.S. W. Govt. In Dibbs’s last Ministry
the selection of Kidd was a surprise, and he was
supposed to be a makeweight for the R.C. Tom
Slattery—Kidd was to keep up the Protestant end
of the log. Kidd’s Orangeism is perhaps the
main reason now for his selection for a second
term of office.
“Tom” Hassall, M.L.A. for up Moree
way, has left the N.S.W. Ministry, in which he
held the Lands portfolio, and is going to England.
For months it has been assumed by everyone that
the burly Thomas was to be appointed Trade
Commissioner, or something of the sort, to the
State of N.S.W. in London. Premier See
strenuously denies that Hassall is to receive any
Govt. job. And he ought to know. Still, every-
one is wondering why Tom Hassall unmurmur-
iugly abdicates £l2OO a year.
Poet Arthur Adams didn’t meet many big-
gun “specials ” during the China war—they were
mostly in S’Africa. He came across Lynch, of
the Sphere, who got away from the siege of Lady-
smith by the simple expedient of walking out, but
was afterwards captured by the Boers. The
Sphere, according to Adams, gives as much as
£5O for a photo. Whitall, Reuter’s agent, dropped
in for loot in the shape of £lOOO worth of silver,
in boxes. The most noted pressman was, of
course, Dr. Morrison, who, despite his long
residence in Cnina, can’t speak the language.
When he walked through China, years ago, Mor-
rison could just cackle 12 words, and he hasn’t
learnt many more since.
Frank Anstey, the .ablest Vic. laborite out
of Parliament, was originally a sailor, and in the
middle ’Bo’s, as a lad of 18, was staying between
voyages in a Sydney seamen’s boarding-house.
A strange tar, a thick-set man of 35, blus-
tered into the room one night and tried to
bully young Anstey into bringing his sup-
per. Anstey declined, upon which the
stranger made use of expressions which
impelled the future Labor-man to saunter
up and push his face hard. Taken aback
by the onslaught, the newcomer picked
himself up, and, announcing his inten-
tion of eating Anstey, rushed on him,
but in a couple of minutes was left
stretched on the floor. The pair
never met again until 15 years later,
when Anstey, being in Bourke-st.,
Melbourne, one day, with Marshall
ILyle, observed a man whose appear-
ance, despite Pharisaic respecta-
bility, somehow reminded hnn of
his old life. As the man turned his
portly figure Anstey saw a mark on
his cheek, and, like the true artist,
recognised his own touch. “ See
that man?” he said to Lyle, and
related the incident. “Do you know
what he is now?” asked Lvle.
“You’d better take care he doesn’t
have his revenge for that thrashing,
after all ! He's the hangman ’. "

cences, was one of the genuine pioneers
of Western Queensland, and in the
long, long ago established himself on
the Diamantina —then a veritable Never
Never. De Satge was along, lank, seri-
ous man, of strong Tory feelings, in
which, unknown to him, several Radi-
cal sentiments flourished. In Parli-a-
menthewas admitted to be able, but he could
never form a party or hold on to one.

Cassells’ new “ History of Woolwich Mili-
tary Academy ” mentions that Khartoum Gordon
and Adam Lindsay Gordon were thereat the same
time. Then it discounts its own credit by the
statement that A. L. Gordon was accidentally
shot on a moor in Scotland! The assertion is
made that he was expelled from Woolwich, for
“ horse-stealing,” with extenuating circumstances.
He owned a racehorse, but it was detained
for debt. Gordon broke open the stable, took
the horse, and won a race with it.

The death is reported of Sir Harry Pais-
ley, who for many years earned a breezy living as
a “ poker ’’-sharp on the Atlantic steamers. He
made at least one successful trip to and from Aus-
tralia and the East, but Sir Harry’s best hauls
were accomplished between London and New
York. The profession of always winning at
cards, and winning most persistently when the
stakes are highest, grows difficult for even the
nicest of men when he gets to be old and notori-
ous, and Paisley died in poverty, sad to say. An
overdose of morphine shuffled the gentlemanly ill-
dealer off in New York, and there he will sleep
in the best of card-sharping company until
Gabriel’s Trump calls him to be euchred.

Aboriginalities.
“ Matelot ’ l tells of an interesting person-
age :
He was a fellow passenger of mine recently from
Hongkong to Port Darwin—a sexagenarian Frenchman,
well-known up north as a bird-catcher and taxidermist.
Came to Australia first in the ’so’s, and since then has
been all over the Northern Territory, W.A., M.L., and
Polynesia, besides “collecting” in America, India,
Egypt, China, and the Straits Settlements, for London
Zoo and Jamrach, of Hamburg. Once, when he was
smuggling about six dozen poisonous snakes on a French
steamer from Australia to Marseilles, some one cut a hole
in the snake-box. Result—the crew were promptly
“’way aloft,” and the passengers locked themselves
in their cabins. The old fellow and his bird-tender killed
a lot of the reptiles, and then he was put in irons, being
allowed out each morning to attend to. his feathered
charges. At Mauritius he jumped overboard, swam to a
British vessel and claimed the protection of Gov.
Broome, who refused to give him up, and the
French captain reported the matter to his
Govt. A sentence of three years gaol, and a
rousing fine was registered against the snake man.
The collection of snakes represented two years’ hard work,
and old Louis stood to draw £7OO had he got them to
London. Another time, taking home “ dickies” in a
German liner for Bartlett, of the Zoo, he found that the
crew were looting his cages. A remonstrance to the
chief officer was laughed at, so Louis mounted guard one
night with a shooter, and hearing a rustling amongst the
birds, fired in the dark and shot in the knee a marauding
fireman. The man lost his leg, and a big row ensued at
Hamburg. Bartlett came over, and eventually Louis was
released, whereupon he gave the fireman £2O. When I
met him he was on his way home after more adventures.
With a big shipment of birds he had taken passage for
Europe via China in the Futami Maru, which was wrecked
on Mindoro in the Philippines, and when the passengers
and crew left, Louis stayed on to try to save some of his
property. Single-handed he boated ashore a lot of cages,
and his larger stock—cockatoos, wild geese and ducks,
cassowaries, emus and kangaroos—he liberated on the
island, where he thinks they will become acclimatised.
After fearful hardships and exposure, he was taken off by
a steamer and brought to Manila, went home, sold his
salvage stock for £3OO, visited Paris Exhibition, and came
out again. In the early 70’s he went to Europe, joined
the French army, and was taken prisoner at Metz. The
old boy, who is still as hard as nails, now contemplates a
run to Papua to catch birds of Paradise, the Prince of
Wales and the King of Belgium being named as buyers at
£2OO per pair. This man’s real biography would beat
Rougemont’s romances hollow.
* * * *
" Trotter ” descants on one aspect of Far
Northern agriculture :
On the Tully River (Cardwell, N.Q.) the late James
Tyson owned a large tract of splendid scrub-country,
which he intended for sugar-growing. This land was put
up for auction lately, but had no buyers. An enterprising
"speculator” bought 10,000 acres recently at 10s. an
acre, and intends cutting it up in sections and leasing it
to Ohows for fruit-growing 1 . The Chows have made an
appearance in this district—as in all others in N.Q.—and
mean to stay. The meat-works people intend starting a
“ jam-factory,” and the heathen comes along accordingly.
The Tully and Murray Rivers are suitable for fruit-grow-
ing ; and tropical fruits of all descriptions—such as pine-
apples, mangoes, oranges, &c. —will flourish there ; but
the middle-man comes in and fleeces the cocky. One
pumpkin-squatter sent 60 cases of oranges to Townsville
and lost 6d. a case on the transaction. Tried Normanton
and lost a florin ; now he sends to the Chows, and gets
a return. A Chinkie bought a man’s crop for £2B
and cleared £lOO. The shark won’t let the producer
live.- Saw a man in this district putting up house, sheds,
fee., for Ohows. He told me he had leased the land to
the oily mystery for 10 years, first two years rent-free,
and afterwards £1 per acre per year. So it goes on.
Every man who owns land in the North will tell you that
he’d sooner have a Chow for a tenant than a white man—
" He’s more reliable ! ” But to show what this country
will do: There's a man on the Tully who cleared £4OO
this year from oranges alone. He took them to Towns-
ville in his own boat, thence to the Towers, and sold
them himself. This man employs niggers. By the way,
what is wrong with the idea of growing maize and
pumpkins, &c., on a big scale on the Northern scrub-
lands and feeding them to pigs—for which there is an
almost illimitable market now at Brisbane at remunera-
tive prices ?
How is this for “ strength ” ?
said youth : “You can’t beat the
Lane Cove sharks. There was a
dead cow in the river one Satur-
day, and three or four 15-foot
‘ tigers ’ had been nosin’ round all
mornin’, so when we pulls up at 3
o’clock there was about 40 other
boats there for the hunt. Some
1 captain ’-bloke started bossin’.
He got us all in a ring so’s they
couldn’t get away with the meat,
and then every time one ’ud
make a bite he’d let go with his
harpoon. He missed twice, but
the third try he got the iron into
the biggest. Cripes ! you orter’
seen the rope hum ! and in about
three shakes the boat gives a jerk
and begins to slide. Then he
yells for us to hook-on, an’ as he
goes past we all hooks-on in a
string an’ away goes the whole 40
boats down the river like blazes. .
Crums ! we did slide ! and some
of the blokes was just going to
cut loose when the ‘ tiger ’ runs
slap into a mud-bank —just two
mile from the start. Strong ?
Well, no not for a Lane
Cove-er ! ”
* * *
It is now six years since
the Maranoa River, Q., ran right
through from its source to where
it joins the Balonne.
“Yes,” said Appy, the pit-sawyer, “I
was one of the sailor-crowd that served all through
the worst of the Wanganui (M.L.) war. One
fight we were in the Friendlies (Maoris) were sent
round one way to take a pah in the rear, while we
were sent round another way to take it in front.
We never saw the pah, but we met the Maoris.
“Here’s the niggers!” we yelled. “Give ’em
what for ! ” We dropped 14 of them, and chased
the rest right into camp—and blessed if they
weren’t the Friendlies ! There was a row, and the
boss sojer wanted to know how we came to do it.
“Well,-” said we, “what did you want to send
them round on the port-tack for—and us on the
starboard and no pennants flying.”

“ Imray ” : Can any Bulletin reader
give an idea (understandable by the ordinary
bushman) as to the evolution of the blow-fly.
What is he before he is a blow-fly ? and what be-
comes of the maggots ?
Several fishermen have lost their
lives recently by being washed off the
rocks on the coast near Sydney. Such ac-
cidents are of common occurrence, and
are usually caused by ignorance. . The
seas along our coast, especially when
there is a swell unaccompanied by wind,
are most treacherous. The waves will
wash to a certain height for perhaps
half-an-hour, when suddenly, without
warning, a number of enormous breakers
will come along, sweeping the unwary
fisherman off the perch he has selected
as being well out of reach. The fact
that a rock is dry is no guarantee of
safety, for there is often time for the
water to dry between the breaks. Old
hands have always one eye on the water,
and if caught lie down and hang-on
hard.
“F.S.S.”: At Broad Arrow ail
old miner—a hatter -kept, a tamo snake.
He saw the snake go into his hut, and
could not find it for three days, during
which there was a complete clearance of
vermin. So the old chap, giving the
snake the credit, allowed it to stay on
after he had found it. He was tempted
to kill it once when he found it coiled
up in his bed ; but, on second thoughts,
contented himself with giving it a shove
with a whip. The snake did not go on
the. bed again, and for three months it
stayed, becoming at last quite tame.
But one day two men who came to the
old man’s tent while he was away,
despatched his pet, in ignorance of its
domestic status.

‘ ‘ Werrimee ”: Travelled lately from Tiboo-
burra to Broken Hill. Crouched up in a shanghai
from noon on Saturday till Monday night, one
interval between meal and meal, 18 hours;
another, 11 hours; then three two-bob meals in
nine hours. Horses poor and weak, whipped from
stage to stage, and driven full walk up to each
“mail change.” And for an exhilarating trip of
this kind you part with six lovely sovs. each way
—besides 4d. per lb. for luggage .over 14 lb.
At Sandy Creek we met the up-coach bogged in
midstream, the coachman wading through mud
and water, and whacking into the weary brutes.
The mail must be up to time at the Broken Hill
end; but at Tibooburra it is often from four to
forty hours late. Common thing to see the mails
served out at midnight, and even at 1 and 2 a.m.

of the lyre-bird must be very ineffective. The

“ Dave ” on a certain bush-worker’s
yearning for company :
“ Call at the humpy when yer out thet way,” said Bilk
“ I never see nobody all th’ blanky year ’cept th’ ol’
woman an’ th’ kids. Queer ol’ gal she is !—blowed if she’s
looked at me th’ lars six munoe, and th’ kids is racing
about thet big paddick like brumbies. A ’ell of a life fer
a man, y’ know.”
Caught in a storm, I ran into Bill’s one evening (he was
paddock-minding).
“Mary Jane!” Bill shouted, “no supper to-
night?” Mary Jane, a coarse girl with big bare feet and
dirty faded hair, banged a couple of pannikins and a
damper and some cold mutton pn to a greasy slab-bench,
and grunted. A clumsy, big-mouthed boy of 16, with his
pants half falling off, came and stood at the door and
gaped at the mutton.
Bill searched in a back crib for sugar. “ Now ain’t
thet enough ter drive a man off his ’ed ? ” sorrowfully
shaking dust out of a bag. “ On’y the other day thet
wuz full. By G——! I’ll break some of yer -- necks,
one o’ these"times ! Will yer put a bit o’ honey in yer
tea?” he asked me, but I tried to assure him that I
wasn’t hungry.
“Dammit, leave somethin’in th’ house; don’t be
worse then a dingo.” This Bill addressed to another
youngster, who stood himself beneath a bag of comb sus-
pended from the rafters, waiting for the strained honey
to drip into his,open mouth.
“ Now wire-in ; don’t be afraid ; ’elp yerself,” said Bill,
setting me example.
Here Mrs. Bill appeared, with a blanket round her,
and a bandage over both eyes, so that she wouldn’t see
Bill, and sat down and groped for the mutton.
It did seem “ a ’ell of a life ! ”
“ Scotty ” : Bulletin (9/3/’01)
report that a Prahran J.P. gave a man
“24 hours” for putting on his hat. If a
Hindoo wears his shoes and his turban
he is directly insulting the court. He
must remove one or other. If he removes
his turban and his shoes he is again in-
sulting the bench. All nigger (Indian)
pleaders chuckle over this. But Plow-
den, Chief Commissioner at Lahore, who
threw up his appointment in order to
become a pleader (barrister) was master
of the situation. In ’65, I think, a cheeky
Hindoo was pleading before him, when
he remarked, “There are natives in the
court with their shoes on.” The pleader
explained that they were European shoes.
In fact, they were patent-leather over
very loose white socks (all niggers are
spindle-shanked). Again the court said,
“There are men in court wearing both
shoes and head-gear. Mehtur Bolao.”
Which is “ Call the sweeper.” The latter
appeared with his broom, which Mr.
Plowden would have ordered him to
apply, thereby sending their souls to
hell (I must quote Homer) and their
bodies “ Kunessin oionoisi te pasi.”
(That’s not bad for a Murrumbidgee
whaler)> The lawyers ran to the door and
quickly returned minus their shoes. If
we all —all whites —made the turbaned,
fezzed, and lowest of all smoking-capped
Indians, remove his boots or turban at the
door, we should soon have civility from
the Hindoo hawker and an exodus of the
Mussulmen. But we haven’t the pluck to
do it. I write this for the benefit of
“White Australia.”
* * * *
Man entering bar : “ Give us a beer
and brandy.” Publican, amazed : “Beer
and brandy?” Customer: “Yes; what
about it?” Publican: “Well, blessed
if I ever heard of such a blend before!
Might I ask why you drink such a strange
mixture?” Customer: “Oh, well, it’s this
way : If I drink beer alone I get full long
before I’m drunk, and if I drink brandy by itself
I get drunk before I’m full, but when I mix ’em I
get full and drunk at the same time, and that’s
what I call getting the worth of your money.”

“ Werrimee ” : In that town of flies, clust
and drought, Tibooburra, the butcher had to
shut-down because the surviving stock in that
part weren’t strong enough to travel to the
slaughter-yard. The town’s supply of pigs also
gave out, and tinned-dog at the stores followed
suit. The only baker fell ill, and everybody lived
on scones, damper, and other people’s fowls. The
pubs, got meat occasionally from Broken Hill per
coach (200 miles). Butter, cheese, potatoes, car-
rots, come per parcels-post; and at the present
rate of going saveloys and prime ducks will be
early on the list. Letters often come now in an
advanced stage of grease; and when love-epistles
arrive mixed up with sausage-meat it will be, time
to take a last long look at the “Red Hen and
quit.

“Buzz'”; Be the Queensland drought
which, after all, has not broken-up out West:
One mail contractor has during the last 12 months lost
in the execution of his contract 200 horses. Day after
day he had to walk along beside the four miserable
creatures that dragged the coach or buggy with the mail-
bags. On five separate occasions all four horses died at
the end of the stage, and many dropped on the journey
and were left to perish. When the mails were packed the
poor brutes would stumble for the slightest cause, and
fall, when the whole load had to come off to lift them up
again. It has been the cruellest time for both man and
beast ever known, and the pity of it is that it is not over
yet —for though there have been a few inches here and
there and isolated storms in a few parts, nothing short of
several feet of good rain will do any permanent good.
Until that comes the western country is done. By the
way, the other day 9000 breeding ewes were trucked
back to the west. They were the remnants of two flocks
of 18,000 and 11,000, which left the station some months
ago in search of grass.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: Asshowingthe wonderful change
that a shower of rain makes in Sturt’s country:
For many months not. a vestige of grass or herbage
had been seen between Broken Hill and the Q’land
border; in fact, the ground looked as if nothing
ever had grown there, or ever would. Then came
patches of heavy rain, and within a week the
whole country was as green as emerald. What
price a system of irrigation here ? At Sandy
Creek, erstwhile the barrenest spot of all, grass is
now high enough to mow. Birds are returning to
the long silent country ; and Cobhain Lake, for
months a dry bed, is calling back its feathered
denizens. Settlers, too, tired of the surly Afghan
and his camels, hail the return of the teamsters,
whose vocabulary of expletives sounds now like
“ prayers after rain.”
* * * *
Nearly all the leading Australian papers
have discussed the book of the hour, “An
Englishwoman’s Love-Letters,” but none have
challenged the passage (on p. 208) which Aus-
tralian papers might fairly have pounced upon.
This : “ As I went by a bird opened his bill and
sang a note, and down, down, down, down he
went over a golden scale ; pitched afresh and
dropped down another ; and then up, up, up over
the range of both. Then he flung back his shabby
head and laughed. It was the laughing jackass.”
If the guttural gurgle Jacky makes is meant by
the “ golden scale,” the description is oddly inapt.
Similarly, Jean Ingelow wrote a poem called
“ The Bell Bird,” commencing, “ Toll ! Toll !
The bell bird sounding far away ’’—whereas
nothing more unlike a toll than the note of the
bell bird could be imagined.

An Old Boundary Rider
When a reporter' called to interview Mr.
Henry Gray, of Singleton, he
anticipated an interesting conversation for
subsequent publication in the Singleton Argus,
and in this he was not mistaken.
“My age is fifty-six,” said Mr. Gray, “and I
am one of tne oldest inhabitants of the Hunter
River District, having lived there for nearly fifty
years. For twenty-one years I was a boundary
rider, and went through all the hardships
inseparable from such a life, often camping out in
the cold and wet all night without a fire, and
riding all day in the pitiless rain. I well remember
one experience—l had got a thorough soaking,
and being unable to change, my joints got chilled,
and for l ine weeks I was bed-ridden through rheu-
matism, which cause excruciating pain in joints
and muscles. I was powerless to do anything, and
took to crutches when I rose from bed. The
cold weather aggravated the pain, and then
my flesh swelled and became so tender that I
could not bear it to be touched. My arms and
hands were beyond my control—l staggered
when walking, and was intensely miserable.
Many remedies, liniments, embrocations, and
electric belts were tried, and the doctor’s orders
faithfully followed, but with no beneficial result.
Two years ago I read a pamphlet concerning
Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people, and
began taking them. After using a few boxes the
pains became less excruciating. Wince then I have
had no return of my ailments —have regained my
old strength and activity, and have constantly
borne testimony to the wonderful curative
proprietors of Dr. Williams’ pink pills. If other
sufferers will give these pills an honest trial they
will not be disappointed.”
Physicians say that vitiated and impoverished
blood is the real cause of rheumatism, sciatica,
and rheumatic gout, and that only by purifying
and building up the blood can they be shaken off.
By this method Dr. Williams’ pink pills have
cured thousands of cases, not only of rheumatism,
but of anaemia, debility, skin diseases, rickets,
premature exhaustion. By toning up the nerves
they cure excessive nervousness, St. Vitus’ dance,
neui-algia, paralysis, etc. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers, and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per
box, six boxes fifteen and three, post free.
Special instructions sent free if you write describ-
ing your case.*

Townsend Macdermott, aged father (or say,
Awful Dad) of the Victorian Bar, could probably
write a book of reminiscences quite as interesting
as that of the late Montagu Williams, the
English barrister and county-court Judge. Long-
time the chosen advocate of Melbourne’s leading
criminals, Mac’s most remarkable client, perhaps,
was Billy Barnes, the expert burglar and lock-
picker, who in a weak moment brought himself to
grief for the murder of J. B. Slack. Slack, of
South Melbourne, was a lonely old crank with a
mania for legal proceedings. Everybody took it
for granted that J. B. Slack had “ rats in the
garret,” so when the pertinacious litigant was dis-
covered lying dead of a cut throat in his dusty
house, clutching a razor in his right hand, a
verdict of suicide naturally ensued. Some months
later Billy Barnes got caught in the act of climb-
ing up Dr. Beaney’s verandah-post, which meant
another long term of “chokee” for the burglar.
And this time Billy’s conscience played him a
scurvy trick. He was moved to babble in his
dungeon cell of “ poor old Slack,” and the wicked-
ness of killing him. The police got an order to
exhume the ‘ ‘ suicide’s ” body, and it was then
discovered that his throat could not possibly have
been cut by the hand which held the razor. Slack
was a left-handed man. Moreover, the wound,
viewed in the light of Billy’s confession, bore wit-
ness to truth of it. Put on his trial for murder,
the weary burglar pleaded guilty ; all the same,
Townsend Macdermott made a powerful de-
fence, contending that the jury had
come to the right conclusion re Slack’s de-
cease, and that Barnes’s babblings and sub-
sequent “ confession ” were mere theatrical
follies, born of criminal vanity. A hesitating
jury speirt some hours in finding the prisoner
guilty, but being safely convicted, Billy didn’t
have to wait a minute over the appointed time for
his execution. The Law hanged him with enthu-
siasm. Mention of litigant Slack (a now some-
what forgotten character) always recalls the name
of Townsend Macdermott to the barristerial mind.
To think of the old pleader—erstwhile famous
as a mining lawyer —is to quote some of his smart
sayings. Mac.’s repartee was essentially quota-
tional. “Hot, isn't it, Mac.?” said a younger
practitioner, one blazing north-wind day. “My
dear boy, it’s hot enough to dissolve an injunc-
tion,” was the reply. < ice, on his way to Court,
wily Mac. was accosted by a seedy person with
the peculiarly confidential manner of one who
“does the touch.” “ Good morning, Mr. Macder-
mott ! Many’s the time you’ve appeared for me.”
Quick as thought the answer—“ Run away, my
good man, and rob a bank, and I’ll appear for you
again.” In criminal-assault cases Mac. was a bless-
ing to his own sex. Appearing for the Man
cm one occasion, his cross-examination of the
Woman was very warm, and to the point. “While
the unfortunate prisoner was trying to do what
you have told us, didn’t you say to him, ‘ A-ah !
TOWNSEND M‘DERMOTT, THE VETERAN
VIC. BARRISTER .
Photo, by Richards & Co., Ballarat .
don’t ’ ? ” inquired Mac. in a lingering, beseeching
tone. His way of putting the question, and the
girl’s feeble declaration that her “Don’t” had no
“ A-ah ” before it, settled the case in his client’s
favor. The old battler was on one occasion con-
fronted by a technical objection, which prevented
him from going into the merits of his case. Bar-
rister Isaacs had raised the objection, and Chief
Justice Higinbotham remarked that it was fatal
unless Mr. Isaacs would waive it. “ Then must
the Jew be merciful,” said Macdermott.

the Kaiser were a “bit-off” when we find him
(with reference to the recent Constantinople
earthquake) praying that “the Lord would con-
tinue to hold the Sultan ” —Abdul-the-Damned,
to wit!—“in His gracious and holy keeping.”
“Locust” While on election-tour re-
cently on behalf of a Bartonian candidate, I struck
a little gold rush where about 35 men were making
tucker. The men had just finished dinner (half-
East 11a.m.). One of "them stood on a mullock-
eap and coo-eed, waving his arms to the north,
south, east and west, like a clay-covered Merlin
the Enchanter. The men began slowly to come
in. A chairman was appointed, who lit his pipe
and squatted on his haunches on a big mullock-
heap. The meeting likewise lit and squatted.
I began. Knowing that for some inscrutable
reason many N.S.W. miners are Freetraders
(though highly protected by the State pros-
pecting-vote bounty) I thought I would nave
a hard job—and so it was. Pegging away
desperately, at last, to my surprise, I found
that one shot told. I said that the Free-
trade tariff proposal as propounded by the
M‘Million was to raise £4,000,000 from duties on
narcotics and stimulants, and that the consumer
would have to pay extravagant prices for his beer
and tobacco. Every man’s face lengthened ; each
one slowly took his pipe from his mouth and
looked into the bowl for a minute with pensive
glance. Then the pipes were replaced, and I
knew the battle was won. We got 34 to 1 there,
and I am credibly informed that there is one
man on the field who neither smokes nor drinks.
So that must have been his vote.


A DRESS REHEARSAL.
G. JENKINS, C.M.G. (stage-manger) : “ That position will do very nicely. Please understand that when you are not bowing to His Royal Highness you will bow to me.”
AUSTRALIA: “O, Sir George — I mean Mr. Jenkins —don’t you think I’ll find it awfully tiring?”

A BURST OF GRIEF.
When the news of Queen Vic-
toria’s death and of the King’s
accession was heliographed to the
town, Major B. J. C. Doran, who
commanded the garrison, trained
minute-guns on the Boers, and
fired the salutes with live i
shells. i
A recent American visitor when asked his opinion
of Australia—“ It’s a grand country,” said, he ; “ but
it’s not finished. You
haven’t put the fence
around her yet! ”
Bombala, Friday.—Mr. E. J. Crooke, M.L.C. of
Victoria, yesterday visited the proposed site for the
future Federal capital, with which he expressed him-
self well pleased.
Wonder what back Melbourne street Mr. Crooke
“ visited ”on his way to Bombala. And, by-the-bye,
wonder who Mr. Crooke is, and
does M.L.C. mean “Man Look-
ing for Capital 1 ”

RATIONAL REFORM IN TEACHING
FRENCH.
Teachers must bear in mind that it is a gross mistake,
as well as a mere waste of time and labor, to land pupils
on the grammatical and literary stages of the study before
the first stage (Phonetic and Mechanical) has thoroughly
been mastered. (Dr. Henry Sweet, Oxford.)
Therefore use PERIER’S FRENCH METHOD (Is. 6d.)
and its PHONETIC COMPANION (Is. 3d.). All book-
sellers. Copies are sent, post free, by sending postage
stamps to A. C. GAERTNER, Sole Agent,

POLITICAL
POINTS
If there is any paper which wishes to
politically stay Senator Simon Fraser, let it report
him verbatim.
It has been decided that the official
designation of the House of Representatives is to
be “ not “M.H.R.”
In democratic Broken Hill and district, of
5497 votes cast for the Senate, Mary Jane Gould
polled 297. They put him 23rd on the list.
The other day a compositor in a Mel-
bourne Tory paper substituted “Theft” for
Thrift in a leading article. The poor printer
isn’t always a fool.
If J. N. Brunker had stood aloof when
Reid tried to form his first Government in Burde-
kin’s drawing-room, the Govt, would not have
been formed. Brunker stood aloof when See
attempted his coalition coup, and the Coalition
Govt, vanished.
Mr. Barton no doubt read his colleagues’
minds correctly when, without any consultation
with them, as he says, he instructed the Governor-
General to inform Chamberlain that the Common-
wealth will not have Boer prisoners interned
within its boundaries.
N. S. W . Attorney-General Wise describes
reviving the fiscal issue in State politics as
“putting money on a scratched horse.” This
doesn’t prove that Sydney D.T. is a fool, but it
strongly suggests that it is a rogue. The Daily
Telegraph owns the brute !
Solicitor Crick, if the See Ministry lasts a
few years, is expected to make a name as Lands-
Minister as conspicuous as that of Solicitor
Jack Robertson.. But, then, Robertson put
all his brains into his Ministerial duties, whereas
Crick will probably still stick to the lucrative law
realm.
Millionaire Geo. Brookman, and wealthy
tobacconist, Hugh Dixson, are already out in the
National Ass. interest for S. ii. Parliamentary
seats. Ihe S.A. delegation to the Commonwealth
includes five lawyers, yet an equal number is
already out for local vacancies. Australia is a
happy hunting-ground for the lawyer.
Dear Bulletin,— Of the 16 votes cast at
Ashford (Gwydir Federal electorate) not one was
formal. Yet there’s only one pub. there. Any-
how, 16 is not half the actual voting strength of
Ashford. The inertness of country electors is
answerable for much evil—the present N.S.W.
contingent in the Senate for instance. — Wal-
laroo.
PUSS-PERVERSITY.
“ You naughty boy t don't pull the pussy's tail.”

In the meantime, we shall utilise the State officers.

Snow’s Tram Grab is to come before next
session of S.A. Parliament, backed by Adelaide
Municipal Council and a large majority of local
bodies. Holder, now resigning as Premier, pro-
mised that, if the local council couldn’t agree on
a scheme, S.A. Govt, would go for nationalisa-
tion, but no more has been heard from the shifty,
lean politician. With Kingston, Batchelor,
Glynn, and other strong opponents absent, the
Snow crowd, which is gaining (or “securing”)
much support from politicians, has a splendid
chance of getting its unspeakable grab through
S.A. Parliament next session

At the calling of the Federal poll, in Ade-
laide, there was something akin to an apotheosis
of Josh. Symon, K.C. The man who had been
boycotted by the National Ass. had to acknow-
ledge the cheers of the democracy which had placed
him at the head of the Senate list. Just before
Symon rose to thank the electors, th.e sun peeped
through the side window, lighted on the dis-
tinguished counsel’s egg-bald pate, and illumined
the whole room. To hundreds, Josh. Symon will
now always seem to have a halo.
J. H. KEATING, who headed the Senate poll
for Tasmania. . *
Poynton, one of S.A.’s Federal members,
had the unique distinction of receiving the block
support of squatters, shearers and laborites gener-
ally. When elected to S.A. Assembly six years
ago Poynton was considered c, firebrand, but he
was the main author of a Pastoral Act which lias
done much to revive the squatting industry.
Poynton is unimpressive-looking and a poor
orator, but his head is properly screwed on.
Awful admission from the Kalgoorlie
Miner, Westralia’s most vigorous paper, run by
J. W. Kirwan, M.H.R. (he had been charged
with making his opponent pay for speech reports
in the Miner). “His (Kirwan’s) opponent is
charged neither more nor less than he (Kirwan)
is. The practice followed by the Miner has been
pursued by it for years; and is identical with
that of the Adelaide and Perth dailies. It applies
to all municipal and Parliamentarv candidates.”
Minister N. E. Lewis, who retires from
hon. membership of the Barton Cabinet, saved
the Adelaide Convention on Federation from a
sudden collapse. John Forrest, was, on one occa-
sion, in a vefy obstinate mood, and insisted on the
Senate’s absolute equality with the House of
Representatives. Lewis held the same view in
fact, a, majority of the Conventionists were for it.
Lewis, however, recognising that the Convention
would rise if the motion were carried, went over to
the other side, and saved the situation.
Thus characteristically N.S.W. ex-Minis-
ter Fegan, after appointment to a permanent civil
service position : “I am now in a position in
which I don’t give two straws for Mr. Reid or his
opinions.” By the way, Lyne (no doubt with a
view of .softening things, down) avers that Fegan’s
salary as Under-Secretary for Internal Affairs
will not exceed £750 or £BOO a year. So that a
Commonwealth Under-Secretary is to be (say)
£2OO a year at least worse off than a common
Under-Secretary for the State of N.S.W. !
Now that the plague has caught the Cape-
town army, wHat price the British yearning for
peace? They cable bravely that the Boers are
not entitled to peace on the refused terms ; but if
the Boers keep up the fight a little longer
and the plague gets going, Britain will be glad to
make peace on almost any terms. Britain is
thoroughly sick of the war ; and the Boers stick
to it as grimly as their old Dutch forbears stuck
to the fight against Spain. And Judas Chamber-
lain may well be praying for peace or compromise
as Wellington prayed for night or Blucher.
How absurd a political fetish is Brunker
of N.S.W. ! Brunker’s character is set forth in
his handwriting, which is of the copperplate
order affected by tailors’ foremen. The depth
of political ignorance in which the amiable
placid, respectable cow-merchant wallows was
illustrated in his estimate of the annual cost of
Federation per head of population (didn’t he sav
f 27 l r t( l people of N.S.W. ? Strangers coming
to JN.S.W. naturally ask “Whence Brunker ?”
or “ How?” Much of Brunker’s howness appar-
ently consists of the fact that he is related to
S.M. Herald.
Fleming, spokesman for Melbourne’s un-
employed, told Lord Hopetoun the other day that
ne (His Ex.) had come from “a country where the-
people are much more prosperous than they are
here.” “Which country?” the tired Hopetoun
wanted to know. “England,” said Fleming.
“The cause of the misery here is the bad manage-
ment of the country.” And the Gov.-General had
to look as though he were listening to words of-
historical truth and wisdom. Finally, he said he
was very sorry, and would see whether “ anything
could he done”—to put the Melbourne unem-
ployed on level terms with London’s submerged-
tenth, did he mean?
G. H. Reid, in his speech before N.S.W.
Federal Freetrade Association; cites as positive
proof of the sterling qualities of Freetection that—
The fact that the mother State of the group, the State
which had a much riper political experience than- any
other, gave such an overwhelming majority for Freetrade
was a very significant circumstance. Going to the other
extreme of the continent, they found the youngest State
also voting Freetrade.
Then lie goes on to say that the fact that the
youngest (and most inexperienced) State also
voted Freetrade only strengthens the belief that
the Freetrade poli'y is the best. What can any-
one do with such a logician ?
“ Malle ’ : The N.S. Wales Protectionist-
leaders have always systematically overlooked
their supporters when distributing loaves and
fishes, and, seemingly, the Federal Govt, means
to do likewise. The Federalists were pretty
unanimously uninvited to the Commonwealth
shiyoo, while “Long Bill” lavished illuminated
invitation-posters on the Pile’s-dog crowd. Now
the first Federal appointment of note goes to
Fegan, the Freetrader and anti-Federal hustler.
If this sort of thing continues, Barton’s estimate
of the House of Reps.—supporters 40, opponents
29 (equivalent to a majority in N.S.W. State
Legislature of 29)—will soon need revision
Precedent, and the manipulation of poli-
ticians, having made the weary old fiscal question
the touchstone of Federal politics, the State
electors may well consider whether all minor
politics are to be governed by this dreary fetish,
m the same way as minor politics in America are
defined by the Republican v. Democrat cry, and
in Britain by the Liberal v. Conservative cry. If
the State Parliaments wish to do things, they will
cast aside this fiscal demarcation once and for
ever, and—if we must have Party Government-
range themselves after the fashion of the London
County Council, as Progressives and Moderates.
I hen can we differentiate the sheep from the
goats and ascertain where we are.
The fate of the Barton Government will
largely depend on the votes of Labor-members.
Barton has been cute enough to get to work early
with this fact in view. Kingston, in South Aus-
tralia, and Turner, in Victoria, have been quietly
angling for the support of the men who will be
likely to join a distinct Labor-party in the Federal
Parliament. Two better members of Cabinet for
this work could not have been selected. King-
ston justly stands high in the estimation of the
intelligent Laborites in his owii State, while Tur-
ner has the assistance and good-will of men like
Trenwith, Beazley, and Mauger in Victoria. If
the Labor vote m the Federal House is to be
secured for the Barton Government, then Turner
and Kingston will get it.
N.S.W. Premier See has harnessed up his
new team. Six out of the nine Ministers were
members of the late Lyne Administration, so
piactically it is the same old Govt. Sydney D.T.,
which assumes most of the credit of stopping the
leading men of the old Freetrade Party (Brunker,
Ashton, Carruthers and Garland) from joining the
Ministry, is developing symptoms of violent dis-
taste of the new Govt, for the avowed reason
tnat the State Premier has selected men from the
_ I rotectionists. ” As if the poor man could have
done anything else. The D.T., 18 months ago,
received the announcement of Lyne’s Ministry
with loud acclaim, and has consistently supported
it since, but, having made it up with G. H. Reid,
is now prepared to accomplish its extermination.
Lively contests are expected for the Pre-
sidency of the Senate and for the Speakership of
the House of Reps. The personalities of members
being unknown to the majority of their fellows
the fights promise to run on inter-State lines.
For the Senate Presidency, S.A. and Victoria
will run the Presidents of their respective local
Leg;. Councils—Chaffey Baker, and W. A. Zeal.
And the choice seems to lie between them, though
if Sargood adheres to his decision to stand, the
contest may end in Baker slipping in. S.A. and
Victoria also supply leading candidates for the
Speakership m the other House—Holder and
Quick respectively. As there is some idea of
Holder helping Freetrade and G. H. Reid, the
Sydney press is crying to “ give N.S; W. a show ”
and suggests Bruce Smith. The Govt, nominee
will probably win the office, but Barton is silent
as to whom he will -select. With the Victorian
vote solid, Quick seems to have a good show.
If Premier Peacock is nab more cautious
he will says Melbourne gossip, awake one morn-
ing to find the Age sitting heavily on his political
Ihe reason will be that he has not been
sufficiently respectful to the Age proprietor.
David Syme wanted the property at the corner of
Collins and Spring Streets, recently bought by the
Vic. Govt, for the housing of the Federal
Ministry. The place belonged to Dr. Rudall, and
is m a splendid position. Syme wanted to get
hold of it for his nephew, that rising surgeon, Dr.
Syme. It was dirt-cheap, and just as David
was trying to work the last point Peacock
stepped m and bought it over his head, planking
down the deposit on the spot. Peacock is at pre-
sent disinclined to disclose the price paid by him
—but the figure was about £12,000. Considering
that the property was sold sometime ago (not in
boom time) for £30,000, the Vic. Government beat
Syme for a real bargain.

VITADATIO
After Years of Suffering.
CALL AND SEE FOB. YOURSELP.
Paddington, August 22, 1900.
Mr. S. A. Palmer,
184 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Dear Sir. —My mother has suffered for many
years with Dropsical Swellings in her legs and
feet, and for weeks at a time has been laid up and
could not move about. She is 77 years of age.
My brother has also suffered for a long time with
Poverty of Blood, Weakness, and General Debility.
Last January my mother and brother decided to
take a course of VITADATIO, and the result
was marvellous. My mother got better, and is
now able to go about anywhere, and has no pain.
My brother lias regained his strength again, and
has put on a deal of flesh, and has now a healthy
color. I myself have suffered Tor twenty years
with Fibrous Tumors, General Debility, Weak-
ness, and Poverty of Blood, and noticing the won-
derful effect the VITADATIO had on my mother
and brother I decided to take a course. I got the
medicine from your Paddington Agency, 377
Oxford-street, and after I had taken six bottles
one tumor came away. I am still taking the
medicine and expect some more to come away. I
am getting much stronger, and I have gained five
gmuds in weight since I started with the VITA-
ATIO, and now I have a much better fcolor.
We have persevered with VITADATIO, and it
has done wonders in our family; and we will be
very glad to give any further explanations re our
case on application for our address, which can be
obtained at 184 Pitt-street, Sydney, and at 377
Oxford-street, Paddington.
[Note. —As the writer of the above does not wish
her name to be published through the press, I
have refrained from adding it to the above
testimonial, but the name and address can be
had (as stated) on application as above. —S.
A. Palmer.]
Fnr further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Mrs. J. M. Nicholl writes to The Bulle-
tin from 4 Clifton-Park Road, Clifton, Bristol,
England, a hopeless-looking letter. But it the
persons inquired for are alive and can be found,
The Bulletin is the paper to find them :
If there are now living in Australia any children of a
certain John Rowse (born in 1807 in the Parish of St.
Columb, Cornwall), and they wish to help him, he is still
living in Bristol and in great poverty, and can be heard
of through me. You will know best whether the notice
should appear in the form, of an advertisement or in a
paragraph to catch the eye of working people. Ihe old
man is industrious (an old farmer) and would work; as
long as he could stand. He occasionally earns a few weeks
livelihood at a crossing or tidying-up, but lives, of neces-
sity, much in the workhouse. He fancies his children
are ail in Australia, but has lost sight of them for very
many years. I am quite a stranger to him, only lam
very sorry for him.
This paper does not, as a rule, print
parodies, but the following, from London Morn-
ing Post, is sufficiently true to stand any amount
of repetition
“ RECESSIONAL.”
Three years after Rydyard Kipling.
God of our fathers ! known of old,
Lord of our far-flung battle line,
Beneath whose awful hand we-hold
Dominion over palm and pine—
Lord God of Hosts ! Oh, judge us not,
We have forgot—we have forgot !
The shouting and the tumult dies,
The banners and the troops depart ;
Still stands'thine ancient sacrifice,
A humble and a contrite heart.
Lord God of Hosts ! Oh, judge us not,
We have forgot—we have forgot 1
For we, with Empire drunk, let loose
Wild tongues to speak, base pens to write
Slanders and lies, and boasts whose use
Should stir ttie blood, should rouse ttie fight :
God of mankind ! condemn us not,
We have forgot—we have forgot !
For heathen hearts that put their trust
In beaus of gold, in hosts of men,
To friends untrue, to foes unjust,
So it may seem to England’s gain—
For grasping deed, for vengeful word,
Have mercy on thy people, Lord.

The Borough Council of Dunolly, a back-
blocks Vic. township, lately decided that a
memorial to her late Majesty should be erected
locally. The stodgy councillors then shelved a
proposal to re-excavate the old reservoir. It was
stated the town had only a 10-days’ supply of
water left—but what did that matter so long as
they got their blessed memorial? Is not York
laden with presents, and has he not the power to
bebauble the wearers of white waistcoats?
Bunny lias a poor time of it now—quoted
in the London market as flat at 71,d., he is bundled
out of existence in Victoria with the assistance of
M ‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., the big iron-
mongers in Melbourne. That firm supplies guns,
ammunition and traps, and, in cases of dire need,
can turn on a full blast of destruction with Dixon
Gould’s Vacuum Destroyer. This machine is the
Jim Dandy of its kind, is beautifully made,
certain in operation as a Waterbury watch, and
counts its victims by thousands. Poor bunny !*
An interesting leader in The Bulletin,
13/4/’Ol, on “ The Theory and Practice of Drink”
wisely proclaims—“ We only desire perfect health
as a means to perfect life, and perfect life depends
upon the quantity and quality of possible brain
sensations. ’ In other words, man is governed to
a great extent by the brand upon his bottle.
D.C.L. supplies the best possible sort of brain
sensation. Soft and nourishing as milk, yet cheer-
ing as fizz, it gives the maximum of brightening
results for the minimum of vital cost. Therefore
is D.C.L. ever known as the Whuskyof the Wise.*
After the Brunner (M.L.) mine disaster,
a fund was raised for the widows and orphans,
out of which every widow has been allowed 12s. a
week. Since then several of the widows have
married again, and a question has arisen as to
whether they are still entitled to the weekly
allowance. In one case the public trustee, who
administers the fund, refused to pay ; but the ex-
\yidow issued a writ, and he parted without bring-
ing the case into Court. He now proposes to
allow each widow £65 on her marriage; but the
younger ones “aren’t taking any.”
The statists have evidently a poor opinion
of John Chinaman’s marital relations, as “Chinese
are to be set down as ‘Never Married’ unless
they have wives in Australasia.” And what a
time the unfortunate sub-enumerators must have
had filling in census-papers in Lilly Boulke-street
and other Chow quarters ! Also, do the Statists
expect to get correct particulars from those
harassed women who let rooms as to the status of
certain lodgers—the married couples, the woman
with a baby “ whose husband is away in the
West,” but nevertheless calls to see her about
once a week or so ?
Mr. Curry, Under-Secretary for Lands,
taking leave of N.S.W. Minister Hassall,
observed: “We (officers and clerks in the Lands
Office) have no political opinions.” This rather
strained the fact; but numbers of highly-placed
Civil Servants in Australia really deplore the
liberty to vote which the law accords them. Two
under-secretaries in Queensland, on their own and
other officers’ account, recommended, a few years
ago, the disfranchisement of all Civil Servants.
The high officials say that, having the vote, they
must use it. If they didn’t the fact would be
noticed and used against them. If they use it, as
likely as not it will be against their existing
Ministerial head. One under-secretary admits it
has been his bad luck to have to conscientiously
vote at five different elections against Ministers
with whom he was in the closest official intimacy.

by Albert Sharp of Sydney, entitled, “ Piles, and

free, on application to the Dep6t, 257 George-

Melbourne.*

Sydney big Tatt.’s Club continues to flourish.
It was announced at recent annual meeting that last
year s revenue topped that of all before. Bank-liability
paid off and £2500 invested in Treasury bills. Profit oil
year reached £3419. Writer remembers that the new
P ace was built at the instance of the late Mr. George
Hill, and w-hen the club shifted from George Adams’ long
room opposite, pessimistic predictions were plentiful that
the load of debt w-ould never be shunted. Members re-
signed at different times fearing future liability. The
most sanguine, however, never dreamt that the overdraft
would be wiped out so soon. It all shows what big money
there is in the concern, especially when the beneficent
a.j.u. allows an extra day at Randwick.

i? ® a H a , ra t Miners’ Turf Club meeting on
Sister Saturday the Selling Race provided some good
sport. When the field went to the post a man on a
black pony accompanied the starter, his business being
to stop, if possible, the wild career of Coronella, after
that confirmed bolter had completed his four-furlong
task. Coronella won the flutter (odds on), and then the
trouble began. The nag bolted, as usual, and had gone
about half-a-mile past the winning post before the man
on the pony made a first desperate attempt to catch him
by the bridle. No good. Coronella went on rejoicing
for another round of the course, and another, and an-
other. Some of the jockeys who were waiting to weigh-
m made unavailing snatches at the bridle of the mad-
headed steed, and the man on the pony became almost
frantic in his periodical efforts. At last Coronella, being
almost spun out, consented to get caught. He was sold
ror £26 to a Westrahan sport who said that his jockey
would know how to reduce the horse to subjection. The
Bulletin will be glad to hear how Coronella shapes in
the Ooolgardie district under the new management.
* * « *
Onkaparinga Club, which races on Easter Mon-
da .V at Oakbank, in the hills behind Adelaide, gave
L 2700 in stakes this year. It has a £5OO Cup, Hurdles of
the same value, and an £llOO Steeplechase—£7oo to win-
ner. A smart three-year-old, Royal Artillery, kept the
Cup m the State, but Howie took the Hurdles with
Leiroo, and Allan the Steeplechase with Wooral (Hayseed,
The African, Bethnal, and The Duke, nowhere). It is
expected that the big event will be increased to £l5OO
next year.
» • * *
The total isator had bold advt. at the big one-
day Onkaparinga meeting. £45 17s. was the best div. of
the day. In the chief event, the Great Eastern Steeple-
chase, J. J. Allan, on his own horse, Wooral, captured an
£ll div. ag well as the stake. The second horse would
have paid exactly £297 for a sow if successful ! During
the afternoon over £22,000 went through the machine,
the Racing Club taking 7£ per cent, of this. What per-
centage the man with the bag would have taken can only
be conjectured, but it’s pretty certain he wouldn’t have
handed back to his customers over £20,000 as the machine
d , besides which, the money that the club keeps goes in
stakes for next year. Three races were won by the abso-
lute favorites, another by a very close second favorite,
two more by thirds—Leiroo was third in demand when he
paid 10 to 1 ; and yet a man who should have blindly put
on every horse that started all day would have been
only about £l2 out on balance.
* * * *
S. A. Tatt’s Clip meeting drew a great crowd.
Tote money totalled £10,894, £3OO ahead of last year’s
amount. Five jocks experienced a bad time, one (E.
Hodgkins, the State’s top-notcher) on Corral, in the Wel-
ter, died subsequently, consequent upon several broken
ribs and perforation of the lungs. M'Cann (riding St.
George in same event) sustained a broken collar-bone,
while Calnan and Walker were badly shaken. Ebb, with
H. Hardy up, made a mess of the last jump, and, rolling
over, broke his rider’s left arm.
* * * *
Mr. J. A. Buckland’s Valour (by Vancleve)
notched a double at Ashburton (M.L.) trots, cutting out
the Winchmore mile in 2.25 from scratch, and Dash
Handicap Mile in 2.27 2-5 from 2sec. behind scratch.
Buckiand s St. Simon (scr.) was downed by Irene in
Laghmoor Trot, and trainer Piper protested ori ground of
the of the mare’s performance being far
better than at a meeting held just previously. He dealt
it out pretty strong to the committee, and’the officials
writhed, but said nothing, merely dismissing the objec-
tion. Deposit was, however, returned to Piper.
• • * •
Trudge ” : Never saw a tougher swimming
struggle (I have seen hundreds of hot ones extending over
20 years) than that on Saturday between Read and Cavill
for the Australian Mile Championship. Former set the
pace at straining tension and sustained it from end to
end. Meanwhile boy Dick responded marvellously, never
allowing his bigger and older rival to get more than 3yds.
away till after ICOO yds. had been thrown behind ; but
thenceforth greater maturity and consequent superior
stamina told its tale, though Cavill was only shaken off
inch by inch while frenziedly-excited hundreds screeched
vigorously. Additional years are likely to improve Cavill
for some tune yet, but Read was possibly seen at his best
on Saturday, as the achievement is truly a remarkable
one. In the light of this and other occurrences it seems
to present scribe that ’tis just as well for their reputa-
tions that Fred Lane and Jack Hellings have, at least
temporarily, retired, though neither is nearly out of the
twenties yet.
* • • *
A , f ? w / ears a £°> the mere suggestion that it was
possible for a man to swim the double over-arm or
Trudgeon ’ stroke a mile would have caused the sug-
gestor to be thought “dotty.” This method of propul-
sion is named after the man (J. Trudgeon) who brought
it from South America to England 28 years back. Not
long since, the list of remarkable swimming feats included
a quarter-mile by someone “ using the Trudgeon stroke
all the way. _ When Balmain (Sydney) veteran Peter,
Murphy swam m the Australian half-mile cham., at Fitz-
roy Dock, Cockatoo Island (Sydney), and adopted the
double over-arm stroke, ’Ome writers thought it wonder-
ful. Now, the old side, single, and breast strokes have
disappeared altogether in the Mother State, as every
schoollioy and champion affects an improved Trudgeon.
.Both Read and Cavill utilised this method over every inch
Saturday ' , '° Urney in Mother State Mile Championship on

swimmer Read’s performance in the
State Mile supremacy Saturday last is much the finest
ever put up. He finished 35 or 40 yds. away from pre-
vious holder of the title and hitherto invincible Dick
Cavill, and was clocked to do 24min. 46 4-ssec.—26 3-ssec
better than the previous world’s “ best,” created by the
Ome top-notcher (J. A. Jarvis) at Leicester (Eng.), Aug.
8, two years Cavill also touched inside Jarvis’
figures (25nnn. 13 2-ssec.), putting up 25min. 12 4-ssec.
Last years ‘ all schools” premier “ Snowy ” Baker swam
every inch, of the mile in 28min. 38 l-ssec., just missing
standard time (28min. 30sec.)by 8 l-ssec. What prompted
fo oJs th , man Mealy) to start is a mystery, as less

Wednesday last week, crack N.S.W. vro
sculler, H. Pearce, who won the big sculling event at
recent Anniversary regatta, shipped ’Ome by Orient-liner
Omrah as a deck-hand. He purposes taking on some of
the second-raters “ down-under,” but will leave the front-
rank men alone yet awhile. Harry is a son of one-time
crack oarsman, “Footy” Pearce, who, if memory serves,
visited England years ago in something the same fashion,
but did little good. Pearce fils promises well. He is not
yet 22, scales 12st., is strongly built and as hardy as a
draught horse. The youngster shaped publicly first
when under 17 at Balmain regatta, and won. He is at
least as good as the best we have in Australia now.
• * • - •
M.L. crack pair-oar combination, Calnan and
Crawford (Wellington R.C.) has been broken through the
departure of Calnan (stroke) for S’Africa with the M.L.
Contingent. The career of Calnan—best known in Aus-
tralia as a slashing forward with last visiting M.L. rep.
football team—is unparalleled in M.L. rowing annals.
Beginning absolutely raw in ’96, he rose with a bang
through the maiden and junior classes, and at end of
season rowed, with Crawford, second to the .Picton crew
in the Champion Pairs. Since then the two have com-
pletely knocked the bottom out of M.L. pair-oared rowing,
winning this event at every regatta of note from Auck-
land to Dunedin (including four championships) without
ever being pushed. They have always favored Che “ biff ”
stroke—very slow up on slide and a quick, sharp swirl
through the water, Cheir combination being equalled
only by their magnificent watermanship. Two curious
points are that they never used a rudder on their shell,
and that Calnan has always rowed stroke on the usual
bow-side.
**■ * »
An Australian long-distance rowing record was
put up by F. M. Armytage, the Tasmanian sculler, in Port
Philip, the other day. Armytage rowed from Geelong to
Port Melbourne in an 18ft. skiff, completing the 45 miles
in nine hours. The sculler, a well-to-do’amateur, use
a boat of his own designing, and aspires to make a record
crossing of the English Channel in the same skiff.
• * • *
The recent death of. Sydney pug Bryan Byrne
(whether what occurred at the Golden Gate A. C. or the
Engine-street “ two-up ” school some days later was re-
sponsible or not) should cause Sydney fight-promoters to
drop their cheap-and-nasty system of match-making and
pit none hut properly trained men against each other.
Often now “ pick-ups ” from the tough section are matched
with at most a week’s warning, and dubbed champions of
this or that locality or “ new arrivals ” from England
or America. This is the class of contest that provides
the deaths when they do occur. • It isn’t on record that a
well-trained man has ever been killed in the ring.
There’s too much grab about these so-called clubs, who
want to pay as little as possible for the attraction so that
the margin of gain may be big. Men who have to train
can’t meet for small sums.

middleweight Graeco-Roman man M‘Dermott, at the
Democratic Club (Melb.) Monday night, though
genuine enough, turned out a huge disappoint-
ment. M'Dermott had little hope from “ go.” Buttan
got first fall—a neck-hold—in 18min., and 4mins. later
M'Dermott withdrew through an injured arm. Unusual
course of appointing a judge for each wrestler, as well as
a referee, brought about the rare and laughable spectacle
of five men floundering over the mat at one time, and
caused an unseemly triangular dispute. Writer never
thought M'Dermott had a chance with the turbaned top-
notcher, and wonders whether M'Dermott ever really
looked upon his prospects as at all rosy.
* * * .
N.S.W. Gun Club’s big £lOO Open Handicap
shoot last week went to J. T. Campbell, who grassed 52
straight, and carried home a string of 75 through his two
noms. Boy Montgomery, of Warren (N.S.W.) evidenced
better form, but had hard luck with his 49th, leaving
ultimate winner to kill. Campbell is 47 years old, and
oughtn’t to have a whole bone in his frame, but he owns
many. While talking to this writer he told of a broken
shoulder and arm, and seven surgical operations. Screens
were drawn round his bed four different times through
doctors insisting that he had reached the end of his
tether. Campbell is one of the lean, tough sort that
couldn t be killed with an axe.
* * * *
Herb. M‘Kell, in such a desperate hurry to
leave for England to “fulfil an important engagement ”
12 months ago when Otto Cribb cornered him, never got
further than S’Africa, and now news comes of his first
engagement (March 17) at the Cape, where he and Yankee
lightweight, Everhardt, supplied a howling fiasco before
a highly wrought-up (beforehand) house. The referee
called the thing “ no fight ” at the end of the 11th round.
The purse (£200) went to local charities, and when the
mail left sports and scribes were bitterly deploring the
heavy knock out the game had received. This recalls—
several things.

Invincible plugger Martin literally paralysed
em in the last 100yds. of Melb. Druids’ Great Wheel Race
t other day, and went home with £165 worth of spoil.
Later, the 10-mile supremacy of Victoria fell to him, at
Bendigo ; with Tasmanian Wilksch second, and Frank
Beauchamp third. Seeing that the Bendigo track is
much below first-class time, 23‘5 is remarkably fast.
Beauchamp has been either second or third in every
championship this season.
* » * *
Half-caste M.L. pug. Ruenalf’s method of
attracting attention at recent N.S.W. Agricultural
(society s show was amusing. He’d pound away at a
punching-ball fixed outside his booth and gather a lot of
admiring urchins in front, and then, in between bangs,
bashes, thuds, and gasps, would offer a free ticket for the
booth to the lad screeching loudest at a given signal.
„ ■ nooah ” went one punch stiffer than the rest.
Now ! cried the bruiser ; and the youngsters would
cheer uproariously. “That ain’t loud enough !” came
from the platform. “ Try it agen—now ! ” and so on
hour after hour throughout the day and right through
the week. &
* * * *
interesting example of the superstition
of the racing cyclist. One big firm in the trade in
Australia employs a number of track-men, and a clerk
who is cross-eyed. It was noticed that when there were
races on in the afternoon not a man of the riders would
step up for his usual Saturday pay. Investigation dis-
closed that the cyclists were scared off by the “ evil eye ”
c^ each declaring that he would have
devil s luck on the asphalt if he encountered that cocked
optic.
* * ♦ *
As evidencing what a tough specimen world’s
champion Jeffries is, he never has been knocked down in
any of his fights. Only once did he show signs of
and then not till Corbett had literally
showered blows on his head. Both Fitzsimmons and
Sharkey got home squarely on the huge ex-boilermaker’s
jaw without much visible effect. Though incessantly
pounded about the ribs not once has he been doubled up,
nor, as far as indications went, seriously inconvenienced
thereby. Hence it is generally conceded that any other
man in the business to-day can be much more easily hurt.
It is rumored that Bilmartin, the famous Red
Bird champion who holds all the Australasian records up
to the hour, is going to be married shortly.
At Emmaville (N.S.W.) Easter Sports Massey-
Harris riders gathered four first and one third—in fact,
the whole programme.

A pretty story comes from Auckland (it
ought to have come from Otago). The dread of
the Black Death was on the place, and a war or
extermination had been declared against the rats.
The Counoil had placed a price on the head of
every rodent in the city, and the grocer s boy
became a perfect Nimrod and slew about 30. At
the risk of contracting the plague, he carted his
dead along, obtained his scalp-money, and
came back jubilant to the boss and told him how
much he had made. The boss cast upon him an
eye of Aberdeen grey and then remarked quietly :
“Weel, weel, ye’ll just pay the money to oor
cashier, for ye ken the rats is mine, not yoursj
and ye munna try to rob yer maister like yon.
The bov’s remarks are unprintable.

Public Notices.
To the Members of the A.M.P.
Society.
Ladies and Gentlemen,
With a view to guard against any recurrence of the
Board’s action in connection with their London, &e., Ex-
tension proposal, and at the same time to provide for an
infusion of fresh blood in the Directorate, I desire to offer
myself as a Candidate for your votes at the forthcoming
election to the Board.
The insight into the Society’s affairs I obtained last
year, when helping Mr. Want and others to defeat the
Extension Scheme, satisfies me that, amongst other
things, certain changes in our mode of government are
needed in the interests of the Society, especially :
1. More effective representation of members in Colonies
other than N.S.W. on Head Office Board, e.g , by adding
to it a locally-elected resident Director from each of such
Colonies, who should occasionally sit with the Board in
Sydney to settle important matters of policy, &c.
2. Provision for taking a binding Referendum vote in
serious cases of disputed policy.
3. A limit to the number of Bank Directors (at present
four) on our Board—the huge financial interests of the
Society necessarily making it a rival of the Banks.
To carry out these and other desirable changes I would
propose the appointment of a Committee of Directors
and Members to prepare the necessary amendments in
the By-Laws for your approval.
Your obedient servant,
GERALD R. CAMPBELL.
Chambers, Sydney.
TO THE MEMBERS OF
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY.
Ladies and Gentlemen,—
Permit me to intimate that I am.one of your Directors
who, in accordance with the By-laws, retire on this occa-
sion, and, being Eligible for re-election, I am again a can-
didate, and have the honor of respectfully soliciting your
vote and influence at the forthcoming election.
My interests in the Society are large, and I think I may
claim that my long experience in connection with the
commercial and producing interests of the Colonies has
not been without benefit to the Institution.
I fully recognise the importance and responsibility
attaching to the position of a Director, and should you
therefore again do me the honor of returning me, my
earnest efforts shall, as heretofore, be given to promote
the welfare of our Society.
Yours faithfully,
JOSEPH ABBOTI
(GOLDSBROUGH, MORT & 00., LTD.).
Sydney, 10th April, 1901.
At Poverty Point.
If George Musgrove wants to finish up his second Mel-
bourne opera with anything of a bang he will need to
shorten the waits between the acts. “Mignon” had to
carry the awful burden of 40min. of intervals, and a lot
of the public naturally stayed away from the later per-
formances. An explanation of this tiresome policy was
suggested by the too frequent appearance of Madame
Slapoffski’s name on the bill. Last week, for instance,
the conductor’s wife sang in “Mignon” or “Tann-
hauser ” on four successive nights, so it was only to be
expected that the masterful Slap, would give her voice
as much rest as possible. And because the weary
audience didn’t vigorously kick against the imposition,
Slap, took their contentment for granted, no doubt. He
reckoned without a knowledge of Australian audiences.
Madame Slapoffski, it seems, was not professionally
known by her husband’s name until she appeared as an
operatic artist in Melbourne, or, rather, until she sang
“ Elizabeth’s Prayer ” at a Marshall Hall concert in the
Town Hall, prior to the arrival of Musgrove’s Co.
Madame Slap., the prima-donna, is only an Australian
identity thusfar. In England she sang ballads under her
maiden name—Miss Lillie Williams.
Theatrical-manager George Darrell, who had intended
to stage “The Sorrows of Satan” in Sydney, left for
Yankeeland per Sierra on Tuesday week. George is
nearer 60 than anything else, but he seems to have dis-
covered the Fountain of Perpetual Youth, for, seen on
the street, he has the joyous and jocular appearance of a
boy of 31.
Barron Berthald, whose slight Franco-German accent
doesn’t at all mar his singing of “ Tannhauser ” in English,
makes up very like the George Rignold of yore. Seated
in the centre of the stage, with his face resting on his
hand, Berthald would pass for a twin brother of our royal
friend, Henry Vee.
Frances Saville has scored a particular success, at
Vienna, as Elsa in “ Lohengrin.” If the fair daughter
of the Simonsens has not grown much older and stouter
since she sang the name part in “ Marjorie ” for the Firm,
she ought to make a charming Elsa.
It is known for certain that the Williamson Italian
Opera co. will have a fine principal tenor in Larissa, the
best of all. the tenors who were available. Probably a
certain famous soprano has been secured, but the recent
cable-message which announced the engagement of a
complete co. didn’t specify this lady’s name. Anyway,
there should be a strong combination of artists, judging
from the fact that the salary-list will go considerably
higher than the management’s original estimate.
George Allan writes from Milan, in March; “ Italy may
be bright and sunny in the main, but my experience has
been one eternal landscape of snow. I begin to think I
must have struck the North Pole.”
Nance O’Neil’s re-appearance in Melbourne, where she
was “ born to the Imperial purple ” 11 months ago, is going
to provide some further humorous complications in news-
paper criticism. Last year Nance was “ perfectly
natural ” and “ spontaneous ” in all the daily-press
statements of the case. She got one rather rough notice
from the Age —that was for her Lady Teazle —but the
offending journal took it all back as the season
advanced. The idolators contradicted one another
with their selections of Nance’s superbest points
in certain characters, but, as to her talking,
her gestures and her facial expression, there was
a complete unanimity of praise. She didn’t seem to act
the parts, she lived them, according to the press notices
of last year. And now the Evening Herald has recklessly
put its foot into last season’s butter. “The fair Ameri-
can has made great strides in her art since her last appear-
ance in this city. Her elocution is much less labored than
of yore, gestures freer and more attuned to the purpose,
and play of features less immobile. La Tosca’s acting in the
third and fourth acts reached a high standard of ability,
the actress refraining from the tempestuousness which
marred several of her former tragic efforts.” This of the
Nance whose acting was so spontaneous, unconstrained,
“ instinct with truth ” and altogether beautiful 11 months
ago ! And the Herald is just as far from the truth as it
was last time. Nance’s elocution has not altered in the
least, nor are her gestures more “ attuned to the pur-
pose,” nor is she less tempestuously disposed. But she is
considerably thinner than of yore, and her voice seems to
have lost some of its old strength. When she plays
Elizabeth again it will be found that her royal roar has
diminished in volume.
Grewar, the man who induced Marshall-Hall to lecture
in Adelaide, and went down heavily thereby, went to
S’Africa with the last S.A. contingent as a sergeant.
Grewar, a Methodist parson’s son, has go and brains, and
was cut out for the managerial trade, but he had very
bad luck from the start. He made a bid for “ Banjo ”
Paterson, but the veteran Smythe got there first.
Among the articles recently stolen from Mrs. J. R.
Wood (formerly Essie Jenyns) at the Hotel Australia,
Sydney, was the diamond pendant presented to her by
citizens of Sydney at the hands of the late Mr. Justice
Innes on the occasion of her forsaking the “boards.”
This loss much grieves Mrs. Wood.
“A cold-blooded admirer of Nance O’Neil” writes:
“ I want to express sympathy with Nance O’Neil, and com-
passionate her because of her magnitude ; for her weight,
moral and physical, has made her many enemies. She
came to Australia weighing 14st., more or less, looking
the opposite of the ideal feminine, and fell amongst
critics whose every idea is based on the old romantic con-
ventions. These critics think of the stage heroine only as
the “womanly womanand the womanly woman', as
cherished in the minds of the romanticists, is a
creature that never existed off the boards of
the theatre, and outside those of the moral
novel. Nance O’Neil was not a bit like the
cherished ideal; she looked a hale, flesh-and-blood per-
sonality, with a fine capacity for taking care of herself.
There was no appeal to the protective instincts of the
male—“ protection ” is the poetic ifarne man irives
to his desire to take over and possess. Hos-
tility was the natural outcome. The romantic and
chivalrous man is always anxious to screw the
neck of the woman who laughs at his romance and has
no need of his chivalry. The O’Neil played Magda on
these lines, the only possible lines, Sudermann having
built his heroine that way. Some writers shrieked
desperate charges of “unwomanliness”; some were dis-
gusted, they didn’t know why, missing the appeal to their
Lord of Creation magnanimity; some praised. The
praise was not flattering in nine cases out of ten, and
gave painful suggestions of being provoked by half-col.
advts. rather than an honest appreciation of good work.
Now, practically the whole of the critics refuse to find
tender and womanly touches in anything the O’Neil does,
because she has shown an “unwomanly” attitude of
mind, and ignores the ideals. Yet this actress is capable
of most bewitching tenderness, the tenderness of a strong
woman, a kind of tenderness always much appreciated by
men in real life where the pagan feelings get their work
in. Now-a-days, every man has a caul—romanticism. A
few strip themselves of it for good and all, and contrive
to see life clearly ; the majority put it aside in conducting
their every-day affairs, but draw it over their heads when
in the presence of life as depicted by art. With his caul
on, the literary and artistic person becomes unhumanly
stupid, and challenges the ass in its own field.
Some of the tricks of the music-teaching trade are
strictly dishonorable but rather amusing to people in a
position to spot them. For instance, the prof, who
“ took over ” Amy Castles from Mme. Marchesihas stated
that he will have to devote a tremendous amount of
tuition to the middle register of Amy’s voice. Which
means, as a matter of course, that he means to claim
credit for work already accomplished. Fellow pupils
with the Bendigo girl at Marchesi’s academy can testify
to the fact that Madame wrought a very great improve-
ment in the aforesaid mid. reg., but had not begun to
educate Amy’s higher notes before her mother came and
took her away to the new school.

THE LAST BOER.
(Translated from the Bavarian Liman Blatter.)
The last little streamlet of blood
Has soaked down through the sod.
And thiTV” th ? land of the Boert) >
And the last musket is mute
Heaven hath no pity !
Long since are the crops down-beaten,
And round the shot-shattered farms
The flames lick and quiver.
Two sweat-bathed steeds
V” th . e b ™ w of the hill are tethered,
And darkly on the veldt
An old grey-beard crouches,
Holding, with agonised sorrow,
bunk on his horny hands,
The flaxen-locked head of his bov,
Whom the bullet has just found.
As the lad’s eves glaze,
And he sighs 'his last breath out,
There comes from the old man’s lips
.Nor word nor prayer.
But he raises up liis child,
His hair blood-matted and tah°ded
Lightly before him on to the mare,’
As though he still lived.
Trotting along the narrow path,
With the flame-glow gleaming before him,
He rides through the evening—
Silently rides he home,
And the stars laugh cheerfully down.
His tears blind his eyes,
And, whispering low, the rider
Speaks to his dead child :
“We are riding, Pieter, riding
Through our little world,
For the last time we two together
tor God has willed it so. *
But where this night from the saddle
Thy life-blood trickles down,
Cursed, in earth and in rock
Cursed he the gold !
And though they soon hew it out
And seize on it lustfully,
The blood of my boy cleaves to it,
And fastens my curse thereon
From woe unto woe,
Crazed, it shall drive them :
Their men it shall slay,
And their wbmen it shall lure.
“ It shall deprave their race,
And rob them of peace ;
And their sons shall die—
But. none so nobly as thou !
And what, with lustful greed,
They seek in our land to-day,
They shall come to curse—as we
My dead child, curse it!
“We are riding, Pieter, riding
Through our little world,
l’or the last time, we two together
For God has willed it so.
But where this night from the saddle
Thy life-blood trickles down,
Cursed, in earth and in rock
Cursed be the gold ! ”
