WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising: is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large s urns of money in studying- that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate trie knowledge universally. We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment may
be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands, of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run not in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Kegistration by Government
Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, stouth Australia,
Great Britain and America Open to Inspection.
Late Consulting Staff, Homoeopathic Hospital, Melbourne;
Uate Medical Staff, British Army.
HOME TREATMENT If you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive you
——i———> of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new system
of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a day
passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a personal
visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings and symptoms as clearly
as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of writing,
and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to them-
selves the monstrosity of giving counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, and who
have nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult.
Although liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND
WALLACE ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall
receive the advice of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and
unparalleled success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

Fitzpatrick, chief traffic-manager of Vic-
torian Railways, lias been appointed Acting-Com-
missioner until Parliament comes to some decision
as to future control. There is a strong feeling
among the more crooked politicians, in Victoria,
that the Act under which Mathieson ran the
railways successfully will ha,ve to be altered. This
means that they want it altered to suit their
own ends. These creatures found they could
not squeeze Mathieson as they wished to do
when he began to work in real earnest in the State
interest, and there have been rumblings in the
House during the last three sessions, which
go to show that an effort will be made to make
the future Railway Commissioner amenable to
political influence. Alteration or no alteration,
Fitzpatrick is not a big enough man for the
permanent job.

TWENTY-SIXTH REPORT, presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary general Meeting on 26th April, 1901.
rpHE DIRECTORS have pleasure in submitting to the Shareholders the annexed Balance-sheet and Statement of
x Accounts for the Hajf-year to 30th March, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £6,121 Os. od. brought from last half-year) amount to £12,608 12 6
out of which it is proposed
To pay a dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum £3,750 0 0
To transfer to the reserve fund ' 6 000 0 0
- 8,750 0 0
And to carry forward the balance £3,858 12 6
The Directors point with pleasure to the considerable increase in the figures appearing in the Balance-sheet,
which shows progression in every direction, enabling them to recommend with confidence an increase in the dividend,
which will no doubt be considered satisfactory by Shareholders.
While the Directors will continue to use every effort to extend the business on safe and profitable lines, they trust
to have the assistance and co-operation of the Shareholders, whose services in the past they desire to thankfully
acknowledge. If each Shareholder will do what he can to introduce desirable business to the bank the results cannot
fail to be gratifying to all interested in the institution.
BY ORDER OF THE BOARD,
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

School-teachers’ grievances :
Dear Bulletin.— N.S.W. Public-school teachers are
complaining— with regard to the annual examinations—
q{ the lack of a syllabus of the scope of questions to be
set for the various half-yearly enrolments in each class,
particularly in grammar and arithmetic. Desirable also
would be the notification to teachers of the approx-
imate date of the inspector’s annual visit—each teacher
to be required to have as nearly as possible the full
number of his pupils present. As things are an inspector
m av appraise a teacher’s year’s work when all his dullest
pupils are present and his brighter ones absent ; while a
[ acher in the neighborhood, hearing of the inspec-
lor's approach, can fix his school up for the visit by
•etting his, dullards to stay away till after inspection.—L.
This looks like a genuine military griev-
a 'aTQueanleyan (N.S.W.) were recently interred the re-
mains of Trooper J. Swan, who went to S’Africa with the
Ist Contingent, and returned to die in Sydney military
hospital. Swan, who had belonged to N.S.W. Permanent
Vrtillery, had to buy his discharge before he could join
the contingent. But the man was not really discharged;
he was merely “ transferred at his own request”—a thing
done every day in the British army-from one regiment
to another ; and the proof that Gen. French is perfectly
well aware of the practice lies in the fact that none of the
P A. commissioned officers who have gone with the contin-
gents were required to send in their papers. All were
‘‘attached for duty” and “seconded.” Even the bur-
lesque “Citizens’ Bushmen,” though N.S.W. Govt,
professed to have nothing to do with them,
were supplied with a CiO. and an adjutant
from the P.A. Now N.S.W. Govt., has, in decency,
to choose between repaying to Swan’s represen-
tatives the money it meanly obtained for a bogus-
discharge, and striking off the strength of
the Permanent Artillery, from the date of joining the
contingents, every P.A. officer who did so join. What is
sauce for the goose is sauce for the gander. If a private
bad to buy his discharge to join a contingent, an officer
should have had ri resign his commission to do so.
THE BOERS.
Unschooled in Letters, and in Arts unversed ;
Ignorant of Empire ; bounded in their view
By the lone billowing veldt where they upgrew
Amid great silences ; a people nursed
Apart, the far-sown seed of them that erst
Not Alva’s sword could tame ; now, blindly hurled
Against the march of the majestic world,
They fight and die, with dauntless bosoms curst.
Crazed, if you will; demented, not to yield
Ere aU is reft! Yet, mad though these may be,
They have striven as noblest Englishmen did use
To strive for freedom : and no Briton he,
Who to such valour in a desperate field
A knightly salutation can refuse.
William Watson, in London Daily News.
The world moves ! Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board complains of the excessive con-
sumption of water —not whisky—by Sydney City
Council.
Armv Chaplain liose, recently returned to
N.S.W. from S’Africa, says that “in no case had
he seen or heard of an ambulance party having
been unfairly treated.” This testimony, from a
man who was on the spot, is worth tons of the
wild hearsay statements that have got abroad
about wanton Boer attacks upon the men of
mercy.
The Great Northern S.S. Co. (U.S.A.) are
having built, at New London, several ve -sels
630 ft. long, 73ft. beam, and 56ft. depth-being
thus wider and deeper than the biggest Atlantic
liners, though inferior in length. But, for bulk,
everything afloat will be surpassed by the 20,800
ton boat which the White Star line is having built
at Belfast, Ireland. This vessel —the first to top
the 20,000 tons—will exceed the once-famous Great
Eastern’s capacity by 2000 tons.
GENERAL
Two Years’
A IFIRIZEUSriD
DEBILITY.
Sufferings.
I ZEST UsTimiEaD.
The C afe of
Mr. ROBERT GRAHAM.
(Reported for “ The Star.”)
The increased facilities for recrca'hu
afforded to shopkeepers and their em-
ployees uuder the provisions of the Eatly
Closing Bill are often appreciated by people
who do not come directly within ihe
operation of the Act. It gives “ outsiders ”
an opportunity of meeting folks enjoying a
half-holiday in the middle of the week who
are not at liberty at any other time, except
after business hours. Knowing that Mr.
Robert Graham, of No. 11 Wattle-street,
Chippendale, was always disengaged on
Wednesday afternoons, our reporter
th ught it an opportune time to obtain an
interview in 1 elation to his indisposition,
and was rewarded by finding the gentleman
mentioned sitting on his verandah.
“I have called to ask a few question
about, your illness,” began the scribe.
“ You are quite welcome to ask as many
questions as you like, and I will freely
answer them.”
“ What was your complaint, and how
long were you ill ? ”
“I suffered from debility, in conjunction
with indigestion, for fully two years.
I had been engaged iu sewer work for
some time, and the bad air played havoc
with niy con-ti tut ion. My health had been
very for a long time before I
paid much attention to it. ai d then it was
compulsory, for the disease had worked so
slowly and so stealthily that I did not
give in till it had mastered me completely
I thou| ht that the unhealthy feelings would
go away w hen the sewer w’ork was finished ;
but I was greatly mistaken. I could not
go on Working through the violent pains in
my head and chest, which afterwards ex-
tended all over my body, and 1 got very
feeble in the legs. My appetite became
fickle, and gradually died away altogether.
Anything I did succeed in getting down had
a nauseous taste, and proved quite unpalat-
able. I could not sleep well at night. I
went to bed feeling tired and weary, and
after sleeping an hour or so I had to put
in the remainder of the night wide awake.
When it was time to get up I had to drag
myself out of bed with much reluctance, not
having enough energy to dress. I could
not look at my breakfast, and would start
the day with only a cup of tea on my
stomach. My strength gradually caved in,
as I could not take any food to sustain it,
and I lost all animation and vitality. Water
wws my principal drink, being always
thirsty. My stomach was too much upset
to allow' of my taking anything else, so you
may imagine how debilitated I was. There
was not an hour of the day or night that I
could say I was free from a depressing
languidness that made me melancholy be-
yond description. A sign of my w-eakness
was the way I used to perspire without any
exertion, before I W'as taken ill I was as
hard as nails, and could do a good day’s
work without raising a sweat. In other
ways the debility made itself manifest,
notably by the symptoms of extreme
nervousness from which I suffered. I
used to think I had not got any nerves at
all, but now the least thing would startle
me, and set me all of a tremble. I fought
hard against my affliction, and often forced
myself to eat when I had not the slightest
inclination for food. The result was usually
very disagreeable, my enfeebled digestion
constantly turning on me. I experienced
dull, aching pains I etween the shoulders
shortly after these forced repasts, and it
was not long before the food came up again.
The retching involved in the operation
made my headaches, from which I was
never free, increase in violence. My
tongue was coated with a tlvck, slimy sub-
stance, which made my breath disagreeable.
The fellows with whom I worked used to
remark how awfully miserable I looked,
and in truth I was frightfully thin and
pale”
“ What medicines did you take during
your illness ? ”
“I think I must have taken every
medicine under the sun. At all events i
visited the hospital for six weeks, am
brought back all sorts of physic and pills,
but they did not benefit me in the slightest
degree. I did not get any relief from pain,
neither did they promote an appetite. At
the end of six weeks I gave the hospital
best, and consulted a doctor. He advised
me to take Clements Tonic. I felt rather
doubtful about his advice. After having
taken so many mixtures before, I became
very incredulous about medicines, and so 1
sought the advice of another doctor, who
also recommended me to take Clements
Tonic. Without more delay I procured a
bottle, and the effect was most remarkable
I had believed that my nervousness couh
not be cured by any medicine, but to my
unspeakable delight I felt my nerves m<m
restful in three or four days after the intro
duction of Clements Tonic. Sound refresh
ing sleep came la k quite naturally, and
my terrific headaches were amongst the first
of my tormenting symptoms to be subdued.
The bodily pains left me one by one, and
my strength ielurued as my appetite g ty
better, thus enabling me to eat the foil
that my worn-out frame so much needed
There was an entire absence of pain in th
stomach after eating, and I was never after
wards in doubt about my meals bein.
properly digested and doing their wort
thoroughly. Clements Tonic lestored my
bowels to healthy action, and my tongue
lost its obnoxious roughness and became
clean and sweet again. The excessive
perspiration no longer remained to remind
me of my weakness, as I became strongei
and stouter every day. I took quite a
different view of life under my altered con
ditions, and the feelings of melancholy
troubled me no more. W hen I had taken foui
bottles of Clements Tonic I was quite strong
again, my nerves were as sound as a bell,
and my digestion was all that I could wish it
to be.”
“Can I report these particulars in the
papers.”
“You may with pleasure. I owe my
restoration to health solely to Clements
Tonic, and if any people should happen to
read about my case who are suffering in the
same way they may be glad of my experience.
Publish how and when you like.”

Enteric Fever, Malaria, Typhoid, Dysentery,
Small Pox and all Contagious Diseases.
The War in South Africa
JEnteric Fever has claimed fa/r more victims than the
bullet during the Great Boer War. A guard to the health
of our soldiers, is, therefore, of the highest importance
for the success of the campaign. The following impressive
letter, giving the experience of one of the medical staff of
the Princess Christian Hospital, South Africa, is most
interesting on this subject:
Tan-y-Bwlch, Chester Road, Erdington,
February 4th, 1901.
Dear Sirs. —l have recently seen your advertisement and free offer in the London
Morning Leader, and shall be glad if you will send me a bottle of your Carlsbad Powder
to use in my family.
I first heard of your preparation from Sergeant Gardener, R.A.M.C. (St. John Ambu-
lance Brigade, Birmingham [City] Division), who has just returned in charge of invalids
from Africa. He was for some time stationed at Princess Christian Hospital,
Pine Town Bridge,- Natal, and had also served at several other base hospitals. His first
duty on arriving in Durban, in April last, was to assist in burying four victims from
Enteric Pever. As I was about sending out nine more men (they sail on the Lismore
Castle on the 7th), I asked him to address them. In the course of his remarks he urged
them to lay in a supply of Kutnow’s Powder. He himself had attended to Hundreds of
Cases of “ Enteric,” and the only medicine he partook of was KUTNOW’S POWDER
twice or thrice a week, as instructed on the bottle. He had never been “SICK OR
SORRY” for an hour, and he attributed this fact to the bi-weekly use of KUTNOW.
In following Sergeant Gardener I suggested that the men should lay in a stock of
KUTNOW, and I trusted they would benefit from the advice given, and would return to
England, looking as strong and hearty as he did.
Yours faithfully, J. R. STAMP,
Supt. Sec., St. John Ambulance Brigade,
Messrs. Kutnow & Co., Ltd., London. Birmingham (City) Division.

Along the Castlereagh.
Bill Primrose’s Brewery Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
About a mile from the township, on the road to
Conroy’s Flat, I came upon a huge decaying
wooden structure. Scattered about its rotting
floor were broken barrel-staves, bung-corks, and
rusty hoops. It was an abandoned brewery.
Poets have sung of deserted mines and deserted
homesteads; but no one has immortalised the
naked temple where the dead beer-froth has
stained every beam and rafter.
Looking out through the chinks Of the wall, I
saw a mob of sheep spreading from the dusty flat
towards the thin pasturage of the river-bank. The
wind came through the doorway, and fretted the
gray webs where fatted spiders drooped in mid-
air. In fancy I saw an empire of casks arise from
a malt-strewn floor, with a naked god astride a
bulging tun singing “ Hey for the beer that’s
bitter ! ”
• * * *
Arriving at Conroy’s Flat I came upon the at-
tractive legend of the The Swag and .Pannikin,
swinging over a newly-painted pub. Entering, I
saw Uncle Jim asleep in the corner. Behind the
bar, to my vast surprise, who should stand but
mine host Bill Primrose, late King of Sydney
Domain ! He knew me at once, and greeted me
with one eye half-shut and a moist, beery hand.
1 stood back while two teamsters slouched in from
the road—dry-lipped, smelling of bullock and
long, slow distances. The thirst of a teamster is
a chirk-brown thing with seven throats —as was
o.ice more made evident.
When the teamsters had gone, and their whips
were cracking in the bend, publican Primrose
leaned towards me over the bar. He breathed an
odor of prosperity and good spirits. We shook
hands again and again.
“Ah, sir,” said Bill, “afore I came up here the
Salvation 1 Army wanted me tew jine, but I wud
sooner sleep in a hempty cask than hit a drum
what never did me harm. Me brother Joe died in
Perth, sir,' an’ left me a lirrle money, an? I thought
oi'tlve place where I had been happiest, and so I
took this pub.
“I like the , Castlereagh : me thoughts have
alwuz been up here among the sheep an’ the
drovers what lend you their money tew mind. I
like mindin’other pipple’s money. Mother used
tew say I’d die a banker or a brewer. I should
like tew die a brewer. There wuz a company
started a brewery down the river, near the town-
ship, but they wuz alwuz in trouble—coopers,
most time's.” A .
I intimated that I had seen the company’s pro-
perty ; and watched Uncle Jim snort, stretch his
legs, and open his eyes about the width of a hay-
seed.
Publican Primrose settled on his elbows ; ex-
perience had taught me not to speadc when Bill
settled on his elbows. I sipped what Bill called
whisky, and waited. . "
* * * *
“ I suppose, sir, you’ve never studied breweries ?
A brewery is like a sheep-station—blokes rushin
about brandin’ casks an’ chuckin’ ’em on drays
while other coves tallies every pint what leaves
the place. If yew go tew the end of the brewin’-,
shed you’ll see a lot of fellers shearin’ the wool olf
the beer in the vats. It’s a pity them artists don t
paint a nice brewery wiv its ’underds ah’ ’underds
of casks piled up tew the roof, an’ the tubs an’
vats an’tuns, an’the lorrymen (big as giants) heavin
an’ rollin’ the liquor across the floor. What a
place tew lie, in, sir, when the hot wind seems tew
rip away yOur froat an’ the sun is eatin’ intew
your bones ! Yew snuggle wiv your back against
a tub listenin’ to the gulp, gulp, an’ drip, drip,
drip of the beer up above. Ah-h-h-h !
“ Some of the vats are bigger’n a Guvimint
tank ; when yew sit near yew can hear the froth
washin’ up against the sides —the big loose froth,
sir, what heaves like the sea. I used tew hold me
waistcoat an’ sigh, ‘ What is the wild beer sayin’,
Bill ? what is the wild beer sayin’ ? ’
* * * »
“ It wuz coopers what killed the brewery down
the river, sir. A cooper is a man what builds a
close fence round a selection of drink. These
coopers are terrible guzzlers, sir. When I wuz in
the brewery down the bend there wuz a cooper
there called Swanky Joe ; he never drank out of a
glass or bottle; he jest walked across the brewery
an’ stuck his head intew a 10,000-gallon vat o’
beer. On a warm day he’d let it stay there,
leanin’ over wiv the froth pilin’ up round his hair.
He wuz a fine man, sir, what liked his bath.
‘-‘A man worlcin’ at a brewery has a lot o’
friends. Swanky Joe wuz very pop’lar wiv the
jockeys and loafers from the township. They’d
slink in froo the front gate and say, ‘ What oh !
Swanky, eny ’ope of a wash ? ’ Swanky wud look
up from his hoops an’ staves wiv one eye on the
manager’s office. ‘ ’Olloa, Bunny ! ’ he’d say.
‘ ’Urry up ; the boss is about. Get it out o’ the
little tub there, an’ for Gawd’s sake don’t get yer
boots in it! ’
“ It wuz a beautiful sight, sir, tew see Murphy,
the policeman, come intew the brewery on a visit
of inspeckshun. No sneaking about him ; he’d
jest walk right up tew the big vat, lay his helmet
on the steps slow an sorrerful, an’ then undo his
belt. It’s a grand sight, sir, seein’ a six-foot
p’liceman undoin’ his belt before a 10,000 gallon
vat o’ beer. I used ter think of that chap fiajax
defyin’ the lightnin’. Murphy ’d take a long
breath, put both hands on the edge, an’ lift him-
self slowly, very slowly, until he hid half of him-
self in the big, white froth. Only his boots, sir,
wud be vissible from the beer. Then you’d wait,
an’ wait, until you began tew fink that the beer
wuz beginnin’ tew feel ashamed of itself. Scheiner,
the German head-brewer, wud rush out shoutin’
‘ Murder! Dhieves ! Yire ! ’ Then you’d see one
of Murphy’s boots wriggle, an’ he’d get down
.very slow from the vat, wipin’ his mouf an’ lookin’
wiv a friendly eye at the brewers. ‘ Ah-h-h-h !
bhoys, yez are alwiz thryin’ tew Spile me dhrink.
’Tis good beer, no less, an’ me trote wuz as dhry
as-a camel-thrack.’
• * ' ' * * *
“One terrible hot day, sirj I stood in the
-brewery gateway lookin’up at the big white vat
o’ beer till I couldn’t look at it no longer, so I
made a rush in, ’opin’ tew reach it afore I wuz
stopped; but Scheiner collared me by the coat.
‘ Yat you do ?’ he sez. ‘ Vas you dink dis is a
svimming-bad ? Yere’s dyour dowel ? ’
“ ‘Herr Scheiner,’ I sez, ‘I on’jr want tew see
me reflection in the beer. I helped yer brother
on the Bareoo wunst. Let me see me reflection
in the beer ! ’ He wouldn’t let me, but he gave
me a drink but of a lirrle cask. On’y Murphy
the p’liceman wuz allowed tew go up tew the big
vat.
» * * *
“ Arter a bit a reg’lar school of whalers an’
tramps took tew hidin’ behind the vats arter dark.
They drifted up from the Murrumbidgee an’ Gawd
knows where. Every man on the track must
have talked about the brewery along the Castle-
reagh. There wuz a man amongst ’em called
Sailor Ben ; he wuz a hairy man, sir, wiv a hoarse
vice, knotted hands an’ warts all over him. Me
ole friend, Stinker Jones —you remember, Stinker,
sir? —he wuz captin’ of the gang. The watchman
knowed they wuz hidin’; but bein’an’ old Murray
cod-fisher he was soft wiv the boys. At night, sir,
when the brewery wuz closed, these whalers an’
tramps wud come out of hidin’ an’ sit round the
vats dippin’ in their billies an’ drinkin’ like
gen’lemen. Sailor Ben wud sit in a dark corner
on his own, talkin’ to himself, an’ sayin’, ‘ Ha, ha!
avast there, ye lubbers ! avast! ’ An’ all the time
he’d be spittin’ a ter’ble black juice over the floor.
He had a few mice in his hair : he wud sing in a
awful vice about desert Inlands an’ mermaids.
“Well, sir, arter he’d been in the brewery about
a month he got queerer. One night he crawled
out of his corner an’ collared a small mash-tub an’
floated it on the beer in the big vat; an’ afore any-
one cud stop him he jumped intew the tub and
pushed off intew the middle of the vat. The
boys scrambled round watchin’ him like kids at a
pantermime. Ben sat still for a long time, wiv his
hand over his eyes as though he was lookin’ across
the sea. ‘No ship in sight,’ he sez, hoarsely,
‘no one tew ’elp a perishin’ sailor. Heaven, hear
me prayer, an’ send a sail,’ he sez, spittin’ out
the terr’ble black juice, ‘or I perish on the deep.’
His hairy arms wuz like a gorilla’s, an’ he kept
one over his eyes lookin’ for a sail. ‘ Heaven
hear me prayer,’ he sez again, ‘ an’ send a ship.’
“Stinker wuz nearly sober; an’he jumps up
an’ collars a lot of brewers’ corks what wuz fas-
tened together wiv wire, an’ tied ’em round his
belt. Then he got another mash-tub an’ floated it
on the big vat. ‘ Keep your ’eart up, Ben !’ he
shouts. ‘ Yew shan’t perish for want of a helpin’
hand.’ Wiv that he jumped intew the tub an’
pushed off intew the middle of the ragin’ beer.
“ Sailor Ben sat up in his tub, sir, an’ scowled
at Stinker floatin’ towards him. ‘ Avast wiv that
lubberly craft !’ he roars. ‘ This is the ship Lon-
don, wiv 700 souls aboard. Keep tew starboard,
d’ye hear ?’
“Stinker skimmed up alongside the London
an’ seized her by the ribs. ‘ Come ashore, Ben,’
he says, ‘ an’ no larkin’.’
“ Ben the sailor stood up like a bull, collared
Stinker by the chest, an’ frew him intew the beer.
It wuz a awful sight, sir, tew see Stinker floatin’
about the beer wiv his corks, an’ Sailor Ben
cursin’ him for a lubber an’ a shark.”
* * * *
I heard a groan from Uncle Jim. He was wide-
awake and listening. Publican Primrose con-
tinued.
“ One day the manager told Herr Scheiner that
the customers wuz complainin’ about the terrible
flavor of fish an’ prawns that went wiv the last
5000-gallon brew. That was froo the whalers
catchin’ their tucker in the river. Arterwarc’s
the manager goes up tew the big vat and says
there wuz a tremehjus leakage of 700 gallons a
week. Scheiner he said somethin’ about Murphy
the p’liceman, but the manager said ‘ Bah,
nonsince ; ’tisn’t possible for one p’liceman tew
surround a square acre of beer’—for that’s what
the leakage amounted tew, sir. .
“ Suddenly somethin’ came intew the manager’s
face. ‘ Surround the vats !’ he shouts. In ’arf a
minute all the brewery hands wuz around the tubs
wiv staves in their hands, shoutin’ an’ cursin’ about
the dark corners. Stinker Jones surrendered first;
then arter a desperit fight they got Ben the Sailor;
the others rolled out like the blokes from Cronje’s
laager. They wuz a dirty crowd, sir. One old
wh der began tew cry ’cause lie lost his swag in
the beer; another went on his knees tew the
manager an’ sez ‘ Gimme a photograph of the big
vat what’s got the white balm on top ; it
will be something for me to look at
when I’m perishin’ in the dry country!
The manager wuz at his wits’ end, sir ; he
couldn’t prosecute a dozen whalers for sleepin in
his vats ; it wud give the beer a bad character, sir.
So he let ’em go.”
* • * 1 *
Maloney and Brannigan came in and asked for
beer. Uncle Jim looked as if he was going to say
something, but changed his mind. Publican
Primrose wasn’t the man to spoil a good story;
he drew the beer with as much froth as possible,
and proceeded.
“ Arter a bin, sir, drinkin’out o’ the big vat be-
came fashionable: it wuz better than takin it
from a glass or tap. You could stretch yourself
across it, burrowin’ froo the white balm intew the
cool red beer. The swells took tew cornin’ up
from the township, an’ tipped heavy tew bo
allowed tew get their chins in. One day a ker-
ridge drove up, an’ the Guvnor himself stepped
out at the brewery gate. There was a lady in the
kerridge. ‘ Sir ’Erbert,’ she sez, ‘ where are you
goin’?’ ‘Deer Lady Anastasia,’ he sez, sof ly,
‘do not worry ; I’m goin’ tew see a man about a
lirrle poodle for yew, Lady Anastasia.’ He
stayed in the brewery, sir, longer than a mort-
gage : when he came out Lady Anastasia jumps
up in the kerridge an’ sez, ‘ Oh, Sir ’Erbert,
there’s a lot o’ froth around your neck ! ’ ‘ Lady
Anastasia,’ he sez, ‘it is a hair of the dawg what
bit me.’ ”
Publican Primrose smiled with enjoyment.
• * » *
“There wuz an old cooper named Sam what
got a job at the brewery. He wuz a slow work-
man, an’ wore spectacles. Never hurried himself
like the other coopers ; jest plugged along, makm’
a cask now an’ then. He wuz alwis losm his
specs ; sometimes they fell into the 5000-gallon
vat, an’ he wud pass away the mornin’ tishin for
’em wiv a long piece o’ wire. Sometimes he
hooked a bit of crayfish or a bit of an old sock.
One mornin’ he pulled out a singlet belongin tew
Stinker Jones. It wuz a fair day’s work findm a
pair o’ specs, in a 5000-gallon vat, an’ the manager
didn’t like it. One day a smart young cooper
named Manners came along and asked for a job.
The manager started him right away. Manners
wuz a quick worker, an’ wud knock a cask toge-
ther while old Sam was huntin’ for his specs. So
old Sam got the bag.
“He took it tew ’eart, but he got another
job down South, an’ plugged away for nearly
12 monfs makin’ casks an’ huntin’ for his
specs. Well, one day, as he was strainm a
hoop on a tub, in walks Manners and asks for a
job. Seein’ that he looked quick an’ handy, the
boss gave him a show right away. Old Sam got
the bag next day ; an’ he never said a word, jest
walked out o’ the gate wiv his kit o’ tools under
his arm. Next ,day he left for Sydney an’ gorra
job—coopers wuz scarce them. days, sir. I hey
paid him ten bob a day for fishin’ in the vat; he

damp, and this affected my health. My trouble
came with a bad cold in my kidneys which
caused terrible pains in the back. I did my work

with these pains when I saw Doan’s Backache

name of the remedy that it should be good for my

The nature of my work caused me to have slight
returns of the pa n, but I take a dose of Doan’s
Backache Kidnty kills, which never fails to cure
me. lam never without them in the house.”

ALBERT DOERINTON.
The Olden Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN
Sbal coTd e f iVe UP the fight because some have grown
Shall we furl up the flag for the moth and the
mould ?
Shall we turn a poor back on the foe in our fear,
nd slink with the prudent who slip to the rear?
", If i' VG truly believe in the prophets who tell
if there’s Heav’n for the brave, for the craven
there’s Hell ” ?
Shall we falter to-day, in the heat of the fray,
Or battle it out in the Olden Way ?
O, Hands that are weary and Hearts that are sore,
Ye labor but now as they labored before;
The Heroes who strove and the Warriors who plied
iheir terrible trade on the field, where they died.
JLhe I lag that we cherish—by them was it flown,
Our Harvest of Freedom-by them was it sown.
Did they part from the fray ere the finis!) ’ Oh
nay !
iliey battled it out in the Olden Way !
And if these were our fathers and We are their
seed,
How shall we bring shame on the Fame of the
breed ?
How shall we tell Ages that follow : “ We came
Of men who were mighty in spirit and frame ;
But We, we are puny, and little we dare,
And fain would we fatten on delicate fare :
But our Fathers (they say) had a love for the fray
And would Battle it out in the Olden Way 1 ”
k or it matters no whit if the fashions be new,
1 here is only one test if the metal be true, —
Do we lend a stout arm till the vict’ry be won,
Or pray in the kirk with our fighting undone ?
Do we shrink in appall ? Do we answer the call
Of the bugle that pealeth : “ O Brothers, come all,
Get you girt for the fray ; be ye manful to-day,
And Battle it out in the Olden Way ! ”

P. LUITIG.
All Melbourneites, Imperial and other-
wise can mute to thank the poor little Jook for
recent loyal improvements to the city. The
I I’ince s Bridge approaches would have remained
or ever in the same state of abject ugliness had
not the announcement of the York visit impelled
the awful councillors to crawl towards the goal
they should have run to years ago. Now they are
walhng in the newly-widened thoroughfare with
rockeries and foliage, while the Queen’s statue
committee intends taking over what was a horrible
fetid swamp at the rear of the Immigrants’ Home
and transformnig it into a new park, fitted out
with kiosks and band-stands. At present, how-
ever, one great difficulty faces the City Engineer
m his work on St, Kilda-road, near the bridge.
The land recently filled in continues to sink
rapidly and shows no signs of stopping. The
surface beautifications are accordingly postponed
but enough has already been done to make the
toughest Republican admit that Grovel is not
always an absolutely unmixed evil.
A Census-Collector’s Experience.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was a comparative stranger, so the district-
collector put all the papers in order for me, and
we went over the list together. “ Jones;
he’s the cocky on the right-hand side, half a mile
after you pass the pub. De Lisle’s just opposite.
Mrs. Anderson—she lives about a mile off the
road; you’ll see her track after you get over
the rise from De Lisle’s. John Harris-next house
.” And so on (I have altered all the names).
It was getting near sundown as I turned my horse
into Mrs. Anderson’s cart-track. In the dark I
would not have seen the way, for the road was
overgrown with brush and seemed little used.
The sun was just setting as I came out of the dark
messmate forest and viewed the house and clear-
ing. The enclosure originally intended for a
garden was invaded by prickly-moses, wild hop,
bracken, and other rank undergrowths. The low,
two-roomed house was almost hidden by dense
clumps of whitish gum saplings, the tender
suckers of which grew up to the door and sprouted
between the boards of the verandah floor. The
smoke issuing from the paling chimney, and a
well-beaten path winding from the back door to
the creek through the thick, malodorous dog-
woods, were the chief signs that the place was in-
habited. The surroundings seemed to breathe an
air of loneliness and abandonment.
A slatternly bush girl answered my knock, and
ushered me into a room whose neatness and order
were in sharp contrast with the general aspect of
negligence without. In an armchair in the corner
sat a woman, prematurely old and grey. Her
face was pinched and sallow, and her eyes, dark
and sunken, had a wild, abstracted stare.’ She
was sobbing spasmodically, and her hands, which
twitched violently at times, clutched at a portrait
in her lap. I explained my mission, placed the
census-paper on the table, and ventured a word to
the effect that I was sorry she seemed ill, and
hoped to find her better on my return in a week’s
time. She turned her gaze upon me for a moment
but made no reply.
The girl was sweeping the verandah as I passed
out. She seemed determined upon extracting all
the relief from monotony which I could give ; for
she stood right before me and talked with a
levity and familiarity strangely in contrast with
the scene I had just witnessed.
“What’s the matter with your mistress’” I
said. ‘ ‘ Baby dead ? ”
“Oh, he wasn’t a baby when he died—he was
20 and more ; but that portrait she’s got was him
when he was a baby. He’s dead just 12 months
to-day. 5
She nudged me to walk away, and followed to
the gate. Here she looked round as if to see
whether she was watched ; then, putting her
handle her mouth, she came a step closer, again
looked furtively at the house, craned her head for-
ward, and whispered impressively •
“ ’E was 'anged / ”

PRDRO.
A will, almost unique for its brevity as for
other reasons, was lodged at Vic. Probate Office
recently It was written inside the lid of a glove-
box, ana stated, “I leave all to mother if any-
thing happens to me. 24/7/’98.”

The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-men’s
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Br ad v has contributed
to Tub Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
mto a pocket without squeezing; and the price is 55.,
post-free.
